Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
Detailed Proofs as Instances of Usul Subjects
Clarifying that detailed fiqh proofs are individual cases of usul subjects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify detailed proofs in fiqh as instances of usul categories such as command and prohibition, and explain how this relationship distinguishes usul from logic.

Core Idea
Detailed fiqh proofs are specific instances of usul subjects like command and prohibition.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial conceptual point: the detailed proofs that fiqh scholars use for individual rulings are not separate from usul al-fiqh; they are concrete examples of the very categories that usul studies. For instance, the Quranic command to give zakat is an instance of the general category of 'command' (al-amr). Understanding this relationship helps you see how usul and fiqh are intimately connected, unlike logic which studies abstract secondary intelligibles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage states that detailed proofs (al-dalail al-tafseeliyyah) are individuals (afrad) of the subject matter of usul issues. This means each specific verse or hadith is a particular instance falling under a general usul category.
The term 'subject matter' (mawdu') here refers to the fundamental concepts usul investigates, such as command and prohibition.
2
Examine the examples given
Two Quranic verses are provided: the command to give zakat (آتوا الزكاة) is an individual of command, and the prohibition of riba (ولا تأكلوا الربا) is an individual of prohibition. Recognize that these verses are not just proofs for fiqh rules, but also concrete instances of the usul categories of 'al-amr' and 'al-nahy'.
The verses are quoted to illustrate how detailed proofs manifest the abstract categories studied in usul.
3
Classify the type of usul subject
The two categories mentioned—command and prohibition—are core topics in usul al-fiqh. In Hanafi usul, chapters on al-amr and al-nahy discuss their definitions, implications (e.g., command indicates obligation), and exceptions. Each detailed proof like these verses is a real-life application of those discussions.
Understanding this link helps you see usul as the theory and fiqh as the applied practice.
4
Contrast with logic
The author contrasts usul with logic (mantiq), which investigates secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). In logic, the proofs (philosophical arguments) are not themselves instances of logic's subject matter. In usul, however, the proofs are exactly the subject matter (e.g., a verse containing a command is itself an instance of 'command').
This contrast underscores the uniquely intimate relationship between usul and fiqh.

Source Text

وذلك ظاهر، وأما ما ذكره المصنف بقوله فإن الدلائل التفصيلية الفقهية المخصوصة بمسألة مسألة (بموادها وصورها من أفراد موضوع مسائل الأصول فإن الدليل التفصيلي لوجوب الزكاة آتوا الزكاة من إفراد الأمر، ولحرمة الربا ولا تَأْكُلُوا الرّبوا أَضْعَافًا مُّضَعَفَةٌ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٣٠] من أفراد النهي (بخلاف المنطق الباحث عن المعقولات (الثانية فإن الدلائل «الفلسفية ليست بموادها معروضة معه،
And that is apparent. As for what the author mentioned in his statement: 'For the detailed fiqhi proofs specific to each issue, with their materials and forms, are individuals of the subject of usul issues.' For the detailed proof for the obligation of zakat 'give zakah' is an individual of command, and for the prohibition of riba 'and do not devour usury doubled and multiplied' [Aal-Imran 3:130] is an individual of prohibition. Contrary to logic, which investigates secondary intelligibles, for philosophical proofs are not presented with their materials to it.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage expands on the earlier point by explaining that the detailed proofs used in fiqh (like specific verses) are themselves particular instances of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. For example, the verse commanding zakat is an instance of 'command' (al-amr), and the verse prohibiting riba is an instance of 'prohibition' (al-nahy). These are the very subjects studied in usul (e.g., the rules of command and prohibition). In contrast, logic deals with secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) and its proofs are not derived from its own subject matter in the same way. This distinguishes the intimate relationship between usul and fiqh from that between logic and other sciences.

Key Terms
دليل تفصيلي (dalil tafseeli): A detailed proof specific to a particular fiqh issue, such as a verse or hadith addressing a specific rule.
معقولات ثانية (ma'qulat thania): Secondary intelligibles—concepts that are purely mental and not found in external reality, such as substance and accident in logic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the verse on riba considered an individual of the subject of usul?
A) Because it is a general rule applicable to all transactions.
B) Because it is an instance of the concept of 'prohibition' studied in usul.
C) Because it was revealed in Madinah.
D) Because it has been abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Detailed fiqh proofs are specific instances of the general categories studied in usul al-fiqh, such as command and prohibition.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Classification of Names of Sciences: Generic Nouns or Proper Nouns?
Debates whether terms like 'usul al-fiqh' are generic nouns, generic proper nouns, or individual proper nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the linguistic classification of terms like 'usul al-fiqh', apply the Hanafi view that they are generic nouns, and evaluate the consequences for the rule of 'amm.

Core Idea
Names of sciences are generic nouns, not proper nouns, preserving their general applicability in usul.

In this passage, the author examines a linguistic dispute with profound usul implications: are terms like 'usul al-fiqh' and 'fiqh' generic nouns or proper nouns? The answer determines whether they are considered ‘āmm (general) and thus subject to rules of generality, or whether they are treated as specific proper names, limiting their legal reach. The Hanafi position, upheld by the author, is that they are generic nouns, a conclusion defended through logical and linguistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage and list the three positions: generic nouns (ism jins), generic proper nouns (alam jinsī), and individual proper nouns (alam shakhṣī).
The author starts by stating the first view as apparent (zahir).
2
Analyse the Hanafi argument for generic nouns
Note the reasons: their meanings are universal (kulliya), and they accept the definite article (al-) and construct state (idafa) like other generic nouns.
The author refutes objections that al- does not enter 'usul al-fiqh' fully.
3
Evaluate the counterarguments
Examine the opposing view that they are generic proper nouns (by necessity) and individual proper nouns (because meaning doesn't apply to parts). Understand the Hanafi response: necessity is not proven and the universal can be composite.
The 'house' example refutes the individual proper noun claim.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The universal meaning may be composed of parts that are individually different (like sakanjabīn). Non-applicability to parts doesn't entail properness.
The composite nature of universal concepts is key.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf
If generic noun, the term is 'āmm and can be qualified; if proper noun, it is not 'āmm. This affects how we interpret commands related to these sciences.
Practical consequence for usul al-fiqh: generality of rulings.

Source Text

للقليل والكثير بالوضع العام كوضع هذا (وهو الظاهر) فإن معاني تلك الأسامي كلية، فلا علمية، والعلمية الجنسية تقديرية، وما استدل به من أنه يصح دخول اللام والإضافة، وهما من علائم كونها أسماء أجناس فليس بشيء لا لما قيل إنه لا يدخل على أصول الفقه»، ولا تصح إضافته وإن دخل على أحد جزأيه وأضيف، فإنه لا كلام في خصوص هذا اللفظ، ولا لما قيل أيضاً أن دخول اللام في كلام المولدين لأنه وقع في كلام الله عز وجل بل لأن الاعلام التي كان فيها المعنى الوصفي دخول اللام عليه فصيح كالحسن والحسين، وكذا الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة مع بقاء معنى العلمية، كعمرنا أي العمر الذي هو سيدنا ونرجو مدده في كل هول من الأهوال، وبعد التجريد تصح الإضافة بلا ريب، نعم: يصح الاستدلال بوقوع لفظ القرآن منصرفاً (وقيل) ليست أسماء جنسية بل أعلام جنسية قلنا: تثبت الأعلام الجنسية (بالضرورة) فإن وجد في بعض الألفاظ علائم المعارف، ولم يوجد التعريف فقدّر العلمية الجنسية، كالعدل التقديري (وليست) الضرورة متحققة هناك، وما قيل في إثبات العلمية الجنسية أن المسائل الحاصلة في الأذهان الكثيرة يقال إنها واحدة فدخل في معناه التعين والوحدة، وإذ ليس شخصياً فهو نوعي، ففيه أن غاية ما لزم أنه عرض لمعناه نوع وحدة وهو مسلم بل لمعنى كل اسم جنس، لكن لم يلزم أنه داخل في الموضوع له حتى يكون معرفة وعلماً (وقيل) ليست أسماء جنسية ولا أعلاماً كذلك (بل) أعلام (شخصية) لكون معانيها متشخصة، إذ لو كان كلياً لكان له أفراد، ولا يصلح للفردية ها هنا غير المسائل ولا يصدق عليها إذ لا يصدق الفقه مثلاً على مسألة مسألة، أقول وفيه أنه منقوض بالبيت إذ تجري فيه مقدمات الدليل، إذ لو كان له أفراد لكان الجدار والسقف ولا يصدق البيت عليه فلزم العلمية وليس علماً (والحل) أي حل كلام القائل أن المعنى الكلي قد يكون مركباً من أجزاء متفقة في أنفسها كما ينادي عليه قوله (نحو الأربعة وعلى هذا لا يظهر لذكر هذا التعميم فائدة، والأولى أن هكذا، سواء كانت تلك الأجزاء موافقة للكل في الحقيقة كالأجزاء المقدارية كما في الماء أو مختلفة) كأجزاء الماهية (كالسكنجبين، فلا يلزم من عدم الصدق على البعض الشخصية ولا نسلم أنه لو كان لهذه المفاهيم أفراد لكانت هي كل مسألة، بل مجموع المسائل متشخصة بتشخصات في أذهان كثيرة فحينئذ لا شخصية فأنصف.
Then they differed: the names of sciences, and likewise the names of books, are generic nouns (asmā' jins) placed for the total of reliable masā'il that apply to what is in this place also. It was said: they are the minds of many people, sometimes increasing and decreasing, and it may appear from the commentary that they are placed for the few and the many by a general placement like this one. And this is apparent, because the meanings of those names are universal, so they are not proper nouns, and generic proper nouns (a'lām jinsiyya) are estimative. The argument that the definite article (al-) and construct state are permissible—these being signs that they are generic nouns—is not valid, not because of what was said that 'al-' does not enter 'usūl al-fiqh' and its construct is not sound, though it enters one of its parts and is constructed; there is no discussion about this particular word. Nor because of what was said that the entry of 'al-' in the speech of the later scholars occurs because it occurs in the speech of Allah; rather, because proper nouns that have descriptive meaning, entry of 'al-' is eloquent like 'al-Ḥasan' and 'al-Ḥusayn'. Similarly, construct for minimal connection while preserving proper meaning, like 'Umarnā' i.e., the life that is our master [..]. After abstraction, the construct is valid without doubt. Yes, the argument can be made by the occurrence of the word 'Qur'an' being declinable. And it was said: they are not generic nouns but generic proper nouns. We say: generic proper nouns are established necessarily. If in some words there are signs of definite nouns but no definitiveness is found, then generic properness is estimated, like the estimative justice. But necessity is not realized there. What was said to prove generic properness—that the masā'il occurring in many minds are said to be one, so determination and unity enter its meaning, and since it is not individual it is specific—would entail that only one type of unity entered its meaning, which is admitted, but it does not entail that it is included in the subject matter so as to be definite and a proper noun. And it was said: they are neither generic nouns nor generic proper nouns but individual proper nouns (a'lām shakhṣiyya), because their meanings are individuated; if they were universal they would have individuals, but here nothing besides the masā'il can serve as individuals, yet they do not apply because fiqh, for example, does not apply to each individual mas'ala. I say that this is refuted by the example of 'house'; the premises of the proof run there: if it had individuals, the wall and ceiling would be individuals, but 'house' does not apply to them, so properness would be required—but it is not a proper noun. The solution is that the universal meaning may be composed of parts that are in agreement in themselves, as indicated by his saying 'like four', and on this the mention of this generalization shows no benefit. The better is that such parts agree with the whole in reality like quantitative parts in water, or differ like parts of a quiddity like sakanjabīn. So not applying to some does not entail individual properness. And we do not accept that if these concepts had individuals, they would be each mas'ala. Rather, the total of masā'il is individuated by individuations in many minds; then there is no individual properness. So be fair.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage addresses the linguistic classification of terms like 'usūl al-fiqh', 'fiqh', etc. The author examines three positions: (1) they are generic nouns (asmā' jins) – this is the apparent view, because their meanings are universal and can take definite article and construct state, though counterarguments are addressed; (2) they are generic proper nouns (a'lām jinsiyya) – but this requires necessity, which is not present; (3) they are individual proper nouns (a'lām shakhṣiyya) – this is rejected because the meaning does not apply to each individual part. The resolution (al-ḥall) is that universal meanings can be composed of parts (like four), and the non-applicability to parts does not indicate properness. The discussion is important for understanding whether these terms are considered 'āmm (general) or khāṣṣ (specific) in usul analysis.

Key Terms
اسم جنس (ism jins): A generic noun denoting a universal meaning that applies to many individuals.
علم جنسي (alam jinsī): A proper noun that denotes a genus (e.g., 'al-Ḥasan' as a name for a type of beauty).
علم شخصي (alam shakhṣī): An individual proper noun denoting a specific entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, what type of noun are names of sciences like 'usul al-fiqh'?
A) Individual proper nouns (a'lām shakhṣiyya)
B) Generic proper nouns (a'lām jinsiyya)
C) Generic nouns (asmā' jins)
D) Verbal nouns (maṣādir)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position classifies names of sciences as generic nouns, affirming their general applicability in usul al-fiqh.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

6 min
Classification of Generic Proper Nouns ('Alam Jinsi) in Hanafi Usul
Analysing the classification of terms like 'al-fiqh' as 'alam jinsi or shakhsi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'alam jinsi and 'alam shakhsi and apply the Hanafi classification to terms like 'al-fiqh' to determine their legal definiteness.

Core Idea
A generic proper noun is definite in meaning but not individual; it refers to a type, not a specific instance.

In Fawatih al-Rahamut, the commentator examines whether terms like 'al-fiqh' are individual proper nouns, common nouns, or generic proper nouns. This classification is foundational for later discussions on 'amm and khass. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the arguments and apply the correct classification in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its signs of definiteness
Look for the definite article 'al' and consider whether the term is a proper name. The passage focuses on 'al-fiqh' and 'al-Qur'an'.
2
Distinguish 'alam shakhsi from 'alam jinsi
Compare examples: 'al-Hasan' (individual proper noun) vs 'al-fiqh' (generic proper noun). Note that 'alam shakhsi refers to a specific person, while 'alam jinsi refers to a type.
3
Examine the argument against individual proper noun
The commentator refutes the claim that 'al-fiqh' is an individual proper noun using the analogy of 'house' (bayt). A house does not apply to its parts (wall, roof); similarly, 'al-fiqh' does not apply to each individual issue. Therefore it cannot be individual.
4
Understand the solution: universal meaning composed of similar parts
The commentator explains that a universal meaning (like 'four' or 'water') can be composed of similar parts, and the non-applicability to parts does not make it individual. Hence, 'al-fiqh' remains a generic proper noun.
5
Apply the classification
Conclude that 'al-fiqh' is definite (ma'rifa) as a generic proper noun, but it is not an 'amm (general) term in the sense of applying to each individual; it is a proper name for a type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خطبة الكتاب للشارح دخول اللام في كلام المولدين لأنه وقع في كلام الله عز وجل بل لأن الاعلام التي كان فيها المعنى الوصفي دخول اللام عليه ،فصيح كالحسن والحسين، وكذا الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة مع بقاء معنى العلمية، كعمرنا أي العمر الذي هو سيدنا ونرجو مدده في كل هول من الأهوال، وبعد التجريد تصح الإضافة بلا ريب، نعم : يصح الاستدلال بوقوع لفظ القرآن منصرفاً (وقيل) ليست أسماء جنسية بل أعلام جنسية قلنا : تثبت الأعلام الجنسية (بالضرورة) فإن وجد في بعض الألفاظ علائم المعارف، ولم يوجد التعريف فقدّر العلمية الجنسية، كالعدل التقديري (وليست) الضرورة متحققة هناك، وما قيل في إثبات العلمية الجنسية أن المسائل الحاصلة في الأذهان الكثيرة يقال إنها واحدة فدخل في معناه التعين والوحدة، وإذ ليس شخصياً فهو نوعي، ففيه أن غاية ما لزم أنه عرض لمعناه نوع وحدة وهو مسلم بل لمعنى كل اسم جنس، لكن لم يلزم أنه داخل في الموضوع له حتى يكون معرفة وعلماً (وقيل) ليست أسماء جنسية ولا أعلاماً كذلك (بل) أعلام (شخصية) لكون معانيها متشخصة ، إذ لو كان كلياً لكان له أفراد، ولا يصلح للفردية ها هنا غير المسائل ولا يصدق عليها إذ لا يصدق الفقه مثلاً على مسألة مسألة، أقول وفيه أنه منقوض بالبيت إذ تجري فيه مقدمات الدليل، إذ لو كان له أفراد لكان الجدار والسقف ولا يصدق البيت عليه فلزم العلمية وليس علماً (والحل) أي حل كلام القائل أن المعنى الكلي قد يكون مركباً من أجزاء متفقة في أنفسها كما ينادي عليه قوله (نحو الأربعة وعلى هذا لا يظهر لذكر هذا التعميم فائدة، والأولى أن هكذا، سواء كانت تلك الأجزاء موافقة للكل في الحقيقة كالأجزاء المقدارية كما في الماء أو مختلفة) كأجزاء الماهية (كالسكنجبين، فلا يلزم من عدم الصدق على البعض الشخصية ولا نسلم أنه لو كان لهذه المفاهيم أفراد لكانت هي كل مسألة ، بل مجموع المسائل متشخصة بتشخصات في أذهان كثيرة فحينئذ لا شخصية فأنصف.
The sermon of the book by the commentator: The entry of the lam (definite article) into the speech of the non-Arabs, because it occurred in the speech of Allah Almighty, but rather because the proper nouns which had a descriptive meaning, the entry of the lam upon them is eloquent, like al-Hasan and al-Husayn. Similarly, the idafa (construct phrase) for the slightest connection while preserving the meaning of properness, like 'Umaruna, meaning the 'Umar who is our master, and we hope for his help in every terror. After abstraction, the idafa becomes correct without doubt. Yes, it is permissible to use as evidence the occurrence of the word 'Qur'an' as being declined. (And it was said) They are not common nouns but rather generic proper nouns. We said: Generic proper nouns are established (by necessity). For if in some words the signs of definite nouns are found, and the definite article is not found, then generic properness is assumed, like in the case of hypothetical justice. (And it is not) that necessity is actualized there. What was said in proving generic proper nouns: that the numerous issues existing in many minds are said to be one, so specificity and unity enter into its meaning; and since it is not personal, it is specific. In it, the utmost that is required is that a type of unity occurs in its meaning, and this is conceded, rather it is for the meaning of every common noun. But it does not necessitate that it is included in what it is assigned for so as to be definite and a proper noun. (And it was said) They are neither common nouns nor proper nouns in this way, but rather (individual) proper nouns because their meanings are particular. For if it were universal, it would have individuals, and nothing other than the [individual] issues is suitable for individuality here, and it does not apply to them, for example, 'fiqh' does not apply to each individual issue. I say: In it is that it is refuted by 'house', because the premises of the proof run in it. For if it had individuals, they would be the wall and the roof, and 'house' does not apply to each, so properness would be necessary, but it is not a proper noun. (And the solution) Meaning the solution of the speaker's statement: that the universal meaning may be composed of parts that are in themselves similar, as indicated by his saying (like 'four'—and thus no benefit appears in mentioning this generalization, and it is better that it be thus, whether those parts correspond to the whole in reality like quantitative parts as in water, or different) like parts of the essence (as in secanjabin). So it does not necessitate from the lack of applicability to the part that it is individual, and we do not concede that if these concepts had individuals, they would each be an entire issue. Rather, the totality of issues is particularized by particularizations in many minds, so then there is no individuality. So be just.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on the classification of certain terms in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator clarifies that terms like 'al-fiqh' or 'al-Qur'an' are considered 'generic proper nouns' (a'lam jinsiyya), not individual proper nouns (a'lam shakhsiyya). The argument centers on the nature of these terms: they are definite in meaning but not individual; they refer to a collective concept rather than a specific entity. The commentator first refutes the claim that these are simply common nouns, then refutes the idea that they are individual proper nouns by using examples like 'house'—a house is not reducible to its parts (wall, roof). The solution affirms that a universal meaning can be composed of similar parts, and that the non-applicability to each part does not make it individual. This classification is important in Hanafi usul because it affects how such terms are handled in legal interpretation (e.g., their definiteness and scope). The reasoning is based on linguistic and conceptual analysis, typical of the Hanafi method.

Key Terms
العلم الجنسي (al-'alam al-jinsi): A proper noun that is not for a specific individual but for a genus or type, e.g., 'al-fiqh' as the name of the science.
العلم الشخصي (al-'alam al-shakhsi): A proper noun for a specific individual, e.g., 'al-Hasan' for a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentator in this passage, what is the correct classification of the word 'al-fiqh'?
A) Common noun (ism jins)
B) Generic proper noun ('alam jinsi)
C) Individual proper noun ('alam shakhsi)
D) Indefinite noun (nakira)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of terms like 'al-fiqh' as 'alam jinsi (generic proper noun) is essential for understanding their definiteness and scope in usul arguments.

The Hanafi View on Absolute Essence and Its Existence in Individuals
Argues that absolute essence exists identically in particular individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on absolute essence and evaluate the rational proof from similarity of substances.

Core Idea
Absolute essence exists in individuals, not separate — otherwise no commonality could exist.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' examines a foundational ontological question in usul al-fiqh: Does absolute essence (al-mahiyya al-mutlaqa) exist in the particular individuals, or is it a separate entity? The Hanafi position asserts its immanence in individuals. The author presents a proof from similarity of substances and then critiques it, inviting us to think carefully about what similarity means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The absolute essence exists identically in all individuals of a kind; the only difference is in universality and particularity.
This is the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the proof
If absolute essence did not exist, each individual would be a separate reality with no common essence, but similarity of substances is established.
Similarity is a key Kalamic principle.
3
Analyze the critique
The author questions what 'similarity' means: if it refers to attributes, it does not prove common essence; if it refers to essence itself, that is unproven.
This shows the dialectical method.
4
Draw the conclusion
The Hanafi view affirms commonality in attributes but remains agnostic about essential identity, thus the absolute essence exists in individuals as a common nature.
This has implications for how we understand universal terms in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها: الماهية المطلقة أي لا بشرط شيء (موجودة) بعين وجود الأشخاص، لا فرق بينهما إلاّ بالاشتراك والتعيين (وإلا) تكن موجودة لكان كل قطرة من الماء حقيقة على حدة) لأنه يلزم على هذا التقدير أن لا يكون في قطرات حقيقة مشتركة وقد تقرر تماثل الجواهر) فالتالي باطل وفيه ما فيه لأنه إن أريد بتماثل الجواهر الاشتراك في الأوصاف والعوارض فمسلم، لكن لا ينافي تخالف الحقيقة وإن أريد الاتفاق في الحقيقة، فالتماثل لم يثبت بعد ومن ادعى فعليه البيان
Among them: The absolute essence (al-mahiyya al-mutlaqa), i.e., unconditioned, exists with the very existence of individuals. There is no difference between them except by commonality and specificity. Otherwise, if it were not existent, then every single drop of water would be a separate reality, because on this assumption there would be no common reality in the drops, whereas the similarity of substances has been established. So the consequent is false. But there is something in it: if by similarity of substances is meant commonality in attributes and accidents, it is granted, but that does not contradict difference in essence. If agreement in essence is meant, then similarity has not been proven yet, and whoever claims it must provide proof.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage discusses the existence of absolute essence (الماهية المطلقة) — the essence of a thing considered without any conditions. The author asserts that this absolute essence exists identically in the particular individuals, and the only difference is in terms of universality (اشتراك) and particularity (تعيين). He provides an argument: if absolute essence did not exist, each drop of water would be a completely separate reality with no common essence, but it is established that substances are similar (تماثل الجواهر). However, the author then critiques this argument: similarity of substances could mean commonality in attributes, not essence. If it means commonality in essence, that itself needs proof. So the passage presents both a proof and a counterargument, typical of dialectical reasoning in usul.

Key Terms
الماهية المطلقة (al-mahiyya al-mutlaqa): Essence considered without any qualification or restriction, the universal nature.
تماثل الجواهر (tamathul al-jawahir): The similarity of individual substances to each other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What would follow if absolute essence did not exist according to the argument presented?
A) Water drops would be identical
B) Each drop would be a separate reality with no common essence
C) Substances would not be similar
D) Similarity would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Absolute essence, in Hanafi thought, exists in individuals, and the proof from similarity is critically assessed to avoid conflating attribute-commonality with essential identity.

The Nature of Wajib Mukhayyar in Hanafi Usul
Definition and ikhtilaf of wajib mukhayyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on wajib mukhayyar and distinguish it from competing views by identifying the key arguments and their practical consequence.

Core Idea
Wajib mukhayyar is an obligation on one unspecified act, not on all alternatives.

The concept of 'wajib mukhayyar' (optional obligatory) is a nuanced topic in usul al-fiqh where multiple schools have differed. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents a detailed definition and critique of four opinions, ultimately defending the Hanafi view that the obligation is on one unspecified act among known alternatives, and the mukallaf (responsible person) has genuine choice. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying rulings where sharia gives options, such as expiation options (kafarah).

Guided Path
1
Define wajib mukhayyar
Identify the term: an obligation that can be fulfilled by any one of several specified alternatives. Example: expiation for broken oath (freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them).
The obligation is not on all alternatives simultaneously.
2
Identify the four opinions
List the views presented: (1) obligation is on all alternatives (Mu'tazila and some), (2) obligation is on one specific act known to Allah but unknown to us, (3) obligation is on one specific act but falls by performing any alternative as a substitute, (4) the chosen Hanafi view: obligation is on one unspecified act with real choice.
The author refutes first three views.
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument: intellectual permissibility (al-jawaz aqlan) and the common element (al-qadr al-mushtarak) present in all obligations, e.g., prayer's common element across time. Text (an-nass) indicates conclusively that the obligation is on the common element, implying choice.
The unspecified is known as 'one of three' conceptually.
4
Analyze the rebuttals
Against view (1): combining all may be forbidden (e.g., appointing two imams). Against view (2): obligation must be known before action; God's knowledge does not negate our choice. Against view (3): the substitute argument still requires specification.
The refutations rely on both rational and textual evidence.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi, performing all alternatives yields reward for one obligation only, not multiple. In opposing view, reward for multiple, but that could be sinful if combination is forbidden.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position: genuine choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Issue [Obligation of the optional obligatory]: While it is that obligation of a command from among known things is correct and actual, it is the optional obligatory terminologically, like the expiation options. And it was said that obligation is of all, and it falls by doing some. So if the responsible person does all, he merits reward of obligations according to this view, because he performed obligations. I say: This is not uniform, because that depends on the permissibility of combining all, and it may not be permissible like appointing one of the two eligible for the supreme imamate, for it is obligatory, and appointing all is forbidden, so how can one who does all merit reward of obligations? Rather he merits sin. And perhaps the one who says this only speaks of rewards whenever possible; then the apparent is that the dispute is only about what came as a command alternated between known things. On this, the criticism would only apply if the command was established in this way, otherwise no. Then this probability has no famous proponent. The sayyid said some Mu'tazila held it. No, it was said in al-Hashiya: He said reliable ones among them, their famous ones: the obligation is all as a substitute, and this is exactly our school, so there is no dispute in meaning. Some considered the dispute meaningful and refined this school as the obligation is all, then they replied that if it were so, there would be sin in leaving some. (And it was said) the obligation is one specific in His (Exalted) knowledge, ambiguous to us. (It is) the specific that is done, so the obligation differs: whoever does freeing is his obligation, whoever does feeding or clothing is his obligation. (It was refuted) that obligation must be before the action; perhaps he meant that knowledge of obligation must be before the action, otherwise this speaker says that obligation is until it is obeyed, because obedience without knowledge is inconceivable, since it is performing the obligatory as obligatory and with intention in most obligations especially expiation. It was said in al-Hashiya: Obligation is a demand, and it is before the demanded, and specification even in Allah's knowledge is only after existence because knowledge follows the known. End. This guides you to the conclusion that the specified demand is not conceivable before existence, and this as you see is that Allah knows things before their existence as they exist, so He knows each thing the responsible person does specifically, so the demand can attach to it. The truth is what we said earlier, so understand. And it was said: The obligation is one specific that does not differ, but it falls from liability by his doing it and falls by doing the other also, because it occurs as a substitute for it. For us, the permissibility is intellectually realized. Moreover, all obligations require the common element; for example, prayer requires the common element among the particular instances occurring in each part of its time, and denying it is obstinacy. And the text indicates it with conclusive indication, so it must be said. Those who say obligation of all said in negating choice: It was restricted only because some of their evidences give only this capacity. It was said in al-Hashiya: In this restriction is an indication that there is no choice at all, unlike the other two opposing schools, for in one there is choice according to action and in the other according to dropping. So referring all to negation of choice as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar is not free from criticism. End. Perhaps its face is that choice in dropping is also in the school of obligation on all, for it falls by doing all or some, i.e., any; rather the intended meaning of choice is choice in performing the obligation under any particularity one wishes. This is only realized on the chosen opinion, not the other schools. For the obligation in the second is all, in the third is the one specific at most differing per person, in the fourth is the one specific at most that the last becomes first the one unspecified, and the unspecified is unknown and its occurrence is impossible, so one is not obligated because it is impossible to obligate with it, and with it one can invalidate the specific differing and the non-differing because it is unknown, so obligation with it is not valid either. We said: We do not grant that the unspecified is unknown, rather it is known from the perspective of being obligatory, and it is the concept of one from the three, even if it is unknown from the perspective of being specific, but it is not obligatory with this. Prayer creation about him, so understand, it will come.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of 'wajib mukhayyar' (optional obligatory) and presents multiple views on its nature. The term refers to an obligation that can be fulfilled by choosing one of several specified acts, like the expiation options (freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing). The text explains and refutes various opinions: (1) that the obligation is on all the alternatives (but refuted because sometimes combining is not allowed, like choosing one imam); (2) that the obligation is one specific act known to Allah but unknown to us, which differs based on what the person does (refuted because obligation must be known before action); (3) that the obligation is one specific act but falls by doing any alternative as a substitute. The author eventually defends the view that the unspecified alternative is actually known as a concept (e.g., 'one of three') and therefore valid for obligation. The passage uses the example of prayer to argue that the common element is what is demanded. The 'Hashiya' is cited for additional clarification. The author concludes that the chosen view is that the obligation is one unspecified act, and the choice is real in performance, not just in dropping.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wājib al-mukhayyar): An obligatory act that can be fulfilled by performing any one of several specified alternatives, such as expiation options.
القدر المشترك (al-qadr al-mushtarak): The common element that is the basis of obligation across alternatives; e.g., the common essence of prayer across its specific instances.
القول المختار (al-qawl al-mukhtār): The chosen or preferred opinion; the one relied upon for fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the nature of wajib mukhayyar?
A) The obligation is on all alternatives, and the mukallaf must do all.
B) The obligation is on one specific act known to Allah but unknown to us, so the mukallaf must guess.
C) The obligation is on one unspecified act; the mukallaf has real choice to fulfill any alternative.
D) The obligation is on the common element, but the mukallaf must perform all alternatives.

Key Takeaway
Wajib mukhayyar is an obligation on one unspecified act, and the mukallaf's genuine choice among alternatives discharges it.

Does a Restricted Command Imply the Absolute?
Analyzing the implication of a restricted command for qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that a restricted command implies the absolute when the restriction is lost, and apply it to the case of qada.

Core Idea
A restricted command leaves the absolute act still demanded in the liability when the restriction is lost, but the time is a strong obligating completive.

This passage dives into a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: if Allah commands 'Fast on Thursday' and Thursday passes, is the act of fasting still owed? The Hanafi school argues yes, but opponents see it as a new obligation. Here, the commentator refines the argument, introducing the concept of time as a 'strong completive' and addressing a challenging counterexample from a vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
State the two positions: Hanafi view that the absolute remains obligatory vs. opposing view that the obligation is completely gone when the restriction is lost.
The dispute hinges on whether time is a condition or a completive.
2
Trace the Hanafi Argument
Understand the key premise: 'negation of the restricted does not necessitate negation of the absolute.' The command 'Fast Thursday' implies fasting simpliciter, so when Thursday passes, fasting is still owed.
The text refines this by distinguishing between literal demand and implied demand for an equivalent.
3
Examine the Counterexample
Consider the vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan: if one fasts Ramadan but fails to do i'tikaf, the chosen Hanafi view requires a new fast for qada. This seems to contradict the principle.
Abu Yusuf and Hasan ibn Ziyad disagreed, holding no qada needed.
4
Understand the Hanafi Defense
The defense: the vow obligated the fast as a condition (hadith: 'No i'tikaf except with fasting'), but the prior Ramadan fast acted as a preventer. Once the preventer is removed (Ramadan ends), the obligation from the vow manifests, requiring a new fast.
This does not violate the principle because the original Ramadan fast was a different obligation.
5
Evaluate the Strength of the Position
Assess whether the Hanafi position remains coherent. The time as a strong completive explains why missing it incurs sin but does not erase the absolute obligation. The vow case is an exception due to a preventer, not a contradiction.
The text concludes that the objection is not valid against the chosen Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بالأول القضاء بنفس وجوب بوجوب الأداء، ومن لا فلا، فعلى هذا يمكن تقرير الجواب بأن الواجب مطلق ووجوبه مستقل، فبفوات المقيد لا يفوت هو نفسه بل يبقى الوجوب كما كان ولا يكون وجوبه في ضمن المقيد فقط، وأما ما رد به المصنف من أنه لو كان الوقت مكملاً لما أثم بالتأخير، فإنه حينئذ صار كسائر المندوبات، فلا يأثم بعدم مراعاته، مع أنهم أجمعوا على التأثيم فغير سديد فإن المكمل نوعان : نوع يكون واجباً كالفاتحة ونحوها، ونوع يكون مندوباً، ومن قال بالتكميل يقول بكونه مكملاً بالنوع الأول، والحاصل أن الوقت ليس شرطاً لوجود الواجب نفسه ولا لوجوبه، بل إنما هو مكمل تكميلاً قوياً بحيث لو لم يكن الواجب معه يكون ناقصاً موجباً للإثم، وإنما لم يجز الأداء بعده لأنه قد وجب التكميل، وقد فوت مع القدرة عليه فهذا المكمل كسائر المكملات بهذا الوجه ثم إنه لا يذهب عليك أنه لا يتم هذا إلا بالاستعانة بما قلنا سابقاً، وكذا لا يتم ما استدل به على المطلوب بأن الوقت كالأجل للدين فلا يفوت بفواته إلا بالاستعانة بما قلنا فافهم (في و) قال «شرح المختصر هذه المسألة مبنية على أن المقيد هو المطلق والقيد أي مجموعهما (وهما يتعددان وجوداً في الخارج فلا يلزم من انتفاء القيد انتفاء المطلق بل يبقى المطلق مطلوباً في الذمة (أو) هما يتحدان فيه وحينئذ يلزم من انتفاء القيد انتفاء المطلق فلا يبقى المطلق عند فوات القيد، فلا يبقى مطلوباً، فالطلب للقضاء غير ما كان في الأداء، وهذا نداء من بعيد، وإحسان إلى من يأبى عنه، فإن كون المطلق والقيد متغايرين في الخارج، لا يوجب أن يكونا مطلوبين استقلالاً، ولا أن يثبتا في الذمة كذلك حتى يبقى المطلق مطلوباً وثابتاً في الذمة عند انتفاء القيد، بل يجوز أن يكون المجموع بشرط الاجتماع في الذمة مطلوباً بذلك الشرط، فعند انتفائه لا يبقى شيء في الذمة، فالقضاء واجب مستقل، وكذا كونهما متحدين في الخارج لا يوجب أن تكون الذمة مشغولة بهذا الأمر الحاصل بعد الاتحاد فقط لا بغيره، بل يجوز أن تكون مشغولة بهذا وبمطلقه فعند انتفاء القيد يفوت الشغل بالواحد ولا يفوت شغل الذمة بمطلقه، بل نقول : الاتحاد إنما يقتضي اللزوم بين انتفاء القيد والمطلق الموجودين بهذا الوجود الواحد، لا اللزوم بين انتفاء الاشتغالين ولا ينافي: تضمن مطالبة الواحد مطالبة مثل، فتأمل أحسن التأمل، فإن ما ذكر مغلطة نشأت الخلط بين الوجود وثبوته في الذمة والمطلوبية (أقول) هذا من يلزم من يرد ما الكلام ناظر إلى اتحاد الجنس والفصل أو تغايرهما، ولا يصح فيما نحن فيه إذ (القيد ها هنا ظرف زمان فلا يصلح فصلاً، فإن قيل: نعم، ليس بفصل، لكنه مشابه له في الاتحاد، قال: واتحاد مقولة متى بالمظروف غير صحيح عند الوجدان (وإن صح ) بناء على اتحاد العرض والعرضي المتحد مع المعروض فلا يلزم من انتفاء فرد منها أي من مقولة متى (انتفاؤه) أي انتفاء المظروف الذي هو معروض المقولة (اتفاقاً) فإن الاتحاد اتحاد بالعرض فلا يوجب الانتفاء، الانتفاء بخلاف الجنس والفصل، فإن الاتحاد بينهما بالذات فبارتفاع كل يرتفع الآخر (فتأمل) وعلى ما قررنا لا يرد عليه أن كلام القائل مبني على مسألة اتحاد الجنس والفصل كما هو مشروح في موضعه وبانتفاء الفصل لا تبقى حصة الجنس قطعاً فتدبر وأنصف، وقد يقال : مقصوده أن الواجب منتف البتة، وهو مقيد، فهو إما أمر واحد يصدق عليه هذا المقيد ويعبر عنه به، أو أمران مستقلان والطلب يتعلق بكل منهما، فعلى الثاني هناك أمران مطلوبان لا انتفاء طلب أحدهما انتفاء طلب الآخر، وعلى الأول طلب واحد، وعلى هذا لا أورد المصنف، لكن يرد عليه ما قدمناه، إذ على الشقين النزاع باق، أما على الثاني فيجوز أن يكونا مطلوبين لا بشرط الاجتماع، فيبقى الاشتغال بالمطلق كما كان، أو بشرط الاجتماع، فالاشتغال زائل، فإيجاب القضاء إيجاب آخر، وأما على الأول فالواجب وإن كان أمراً واحداً هو ما يعبر عنه بهذا المقيد لكن اشتغال الذمة به هل يبقى ويقع المقيد الآخر مبرئاً للذمة أو لا، بل لم يبق اشتغال أصلاً، ولزم معصية فقط تزول بفعل القضاء بما أن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات، فافهم واستقم (ونوقض مختار الحنفية بنذر اعتكاف رمضان إذا صام ولم يعتكفه حيث يجب قضاؤه بصوم (جديد) فلو كان بالسبب الأول هو النذر لم يجب صوم جديد (ولم يوجبه النذر) أو كان لا يجب القضاء أصلاً، كما قال أبو يوسف الإمام، والإمام حسن بن زياد، وهو خلاف مختاركم، وهذا لا يختص وروده على القائل باتحاد السبب، بل يرد على القائل بالسبب الجديد أيضاً لأنه لا بد من التماثل بالاتفاق وها هنا لا يمكن، إذ الصوم الرمضاني المؤدى لا يمكن في غيره ومع الجديد لا تماثل وأيضاً أصحاب الجديد قالوا: السبب هنا : التفويت فيرد عليهم أنه غير موجب للصوم الجديد فمن أين جاء؟ والجواب : أن نذر الاعتكاف كان موجباً له أي للصوم لأنه شرطه بقوله عليه وآله الصلاة والسلام: «لا اعتكاف إلا بصوم» رواه الدارقطني والبيهقي وصححه النقاد، وإيجاب المشروط موجب لإيجاب الشرط لكن ما ظهر أثره الذي هو وجوب الصوم لمانع وهو وجوبه قبله أي هذا النذر (فلما زال)
He said, and it is in one respect: Fawatih al-Rahamut Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, Part One: That (the command) 'Fast Thursday' (means) the fast of Thursday (as an act). Likewise, bringing something analogous to an unintelligible or intelligible (matter), and that the demand for the original includes the demand for its equivalent at the time of loss, whether intelligible or not. And it became clear to you also that analogy (qiyas) for making up (qada) with an unintelligible equivalent is not valid at all, and also the corruption of what was imagined that this is specific to the obligation itself separate from the obligation of performance as we established earlier. This, moreover, is corrupt: fasting, its obligation is not separate from the obligation of its performance in the non-excused person. And what is answered with in the well-known (view) is that its implication, i.e., 'Fast Thursday' (is a command for) fasting and its being on Thursday. So if he is unable to do the second, i.e., being on Thursday (due to its passing), its implication for the absolute fasting remains. For the negation of the restricted does not necessitate the negation of the absolute. And we do not concede that it does not imply Friday; rather, it does not imply it specifically. As for absolute fasting, disregarding the specific, it implies it (but this is extremely weak, since there is no obligation except with the restriction. The required is a fast restricted to being on Thursday. As for absolute fasting, suitable for being in it and other than it, there is no obligation nor demand for it. Hence it is not obligatory before it. And from the obligation of the restricted, the obligation of the absolute unrestricted from the restriction does not necessarily follow; rather, only in it. And if the statement of the well-known is interpreted as that the command implies two things: the fast restricted to being on Thursday (as a literal match to discharge the liability), and absolute fasting by virtue of including its equivalent at the time of loss, then it reverts to what preceded and would be complete, and nothing would be objected to it. And it may be said: The foundation is whether the time is a completive of the obligation or a condition for itself? Whoever says the first: qada is by the same obligation as performance, and whoever does not, then not. On this, the answer can be formulated: that the obligatory is absolute and its obligation is independent, so with the loss of the restricted, the obligation itself does not disappear; rather, the obligation remains as it was, and its obligation is not only within the restricted. As for what the author refuted, that if the time were completive, he would not be sinful for delaying, then it would become like other supererogatory acts, so he would not be sinful for not observing it, while they (scholars) are unanimous on his sinfulness — this is not sound. For the completive is of two types: a type that is obligatory, like al-Fatiha and the like, and a type that is supererogatory. Whoever says completive says it is completive by the first type. The upshot: time is not a condition for the existence of the obligatory itself nor for its obligation; rather, it is a completive in a strong way such that if the obligatory is not with it, it becomes deficient, necessitating sin. And performance after it is not permitted because the completive has become obligatory, and he has let it slip while able. So this completive is like other completives in this respect. Then it should not escape you that this is not complete except by recourse to what we said earlier. Likewise, what is adduced as proof for the desired (conclusion), that time is like the term of a debt, so it does not pass with its passing, is not complete except by recourse to what we said. So understand. (And in it) He said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: This issue is based on whether the restricted is the absolute and the restriction, i.e., both together, and they are multiple in external existence, so the negation of the restriction does not necessitate the negation of the absolute; rather, the absolute remains demanded in the liability. Or they unite in it (existence) — then the negation of the restriction necessitates the negation of the absolute, so the absolute does not remain when the restriction is lost, and thus it is no longer demanded. So the demand for qada is different from what was in performance. This is a call from afar, and kindness to one who refuses it. For the fact that the absolute and the restriction are distinct in external existence does not require that they be demanded independently, nor that they be established in the liability as such such that the absolute remains demanded and established in the liability when the restriction is lost. Rather, it is possible that the whole (compound), with the condition of conjunction in the liability, is demanded with that condition, so when it is lost, nothing remains in the liability. So qada is an independent obligation. Likewise, their being united in external existence does not require that the liability be occupied only by this single thing obtained after union, not by anything else. Rather, it is possible that it is occupied by this and by its absolute. So when the restriction is lost, the occupation of the single (restricted) is lost, but the occupation of the liability by its absolute is not lost. Rather, we say: union only necessitates the necessary connection between the negation of the restriction and the absolute existing in this single existence, not the necessary connection between the negation of the two occupations. And it does not contradict that the demand of the single includes the demand of its equivalent. So reflect well, for what is mentioned is a fallacious argument arising from confusing existence and its establishment in the liability and the demandedness. (I say) This is from what necessitates, what repels. The speech looks to the union of genus and differentia or their distinctness, and it is not valid in our case, for the restriction here is a time adverbial, so it cannot serve as a differentia. For if it is said: Yes, it is not a differentia, but it resembles it in union. He said: And the union of the category of "when" with the container is not correct according to empirical observation, even if it is valid (theoretically) based on the union of the accident and the accidental united with the subject. So the negation of an individual of them (i.e., of the category of "when") does not necessitate its negation (i.e., of the container which is the subject of the category) by agreement. For the union is accidental, so it does not necessitate negation, unlike genus and differentia, for the union between them is essential, so by the removal of each, the other is removed. (So reflect). And according to what we have established, the objection that the speaker's statement is based on the issue of the union of genus and differentia, as explained in its place, and with the removal of the differentia no portion of the genus remains — certainly, so ponder and be fair. And it may be said: His purpose is that the obligatory is entirely negated, and it is restricted. So it is either one thing that is called this restricted and expressed by it, or two independent things and the demand pertains to each of them. On the second, there are two demanded things; the negation of the demand for one does not necessitate the negation of the demand for the other. On the first, there is one demand. On this, the author's objection does not arise, but what we presented earlier does come against him, for on both branches the dispute remains. As for the second, it is possible that they are demanded not with the condition of conjunction, so the occupation with the absolute remains as it was, or with the condition of conjunction, so the occupation is removed, thus the obligation of qada is another obligation. As for the first, although the obligation is one thing that is expressed by this restricted, does the occupation of the liability with it remain such that the other restricted act falls under it and discharges the liability, or not, rather no occupation remains at all, and only sin remains, which is removed by performing qada because good deeds erase evil deeds? So understand and be upright. (And the chosen view of the Hanafis was refuted by the case of a vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan: if he fasted but did not perform i'tikaf, qada is required with a new fast. For if it were by the first cause (the vow), a new fast would not be obligatory, or it would not be obligatory at all, as Abu Yusuf the imam and Imam Hasan ibn Ziyad said, which is contrary to your chosen view. This refutation is not specific to the one who says the cause is one; rather, it also comes against the one who says the cause is new because similarity is necessary by agreement, and here it is impossible, for the Ramadan fast performed cannot be in other than Ramadan, and with a new fast there is no similarity. Also, those who say the cause is new said: the cause here is the loss (tafwit), so it is objected that it does not necessitate a new fast — whence does it come? The answer: that the vow of i'tikaf necessitated the fast because it is its condition, by the Prophet's ﷺ saying: "No i'tikaf except with fasting" (reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi, and authenticated by critics). The obligation of the conditional obligates the obligation of the condition, but its effect (the obligation of the fast) did not appear due to a preventer, which is its prior obligation (i.e., the Ramadan fast). So when that preventer was removed (by the end of Ramadan), the obligation appeared.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: when a command is restricted by a time (e.g., 'Fast Thursday'), does that command imply that the absolute act (fasting) remains obligatory if the restriction is lost (Thursday passes)? The Hanafi position, as presented in the text, is that the absolute remains demanded in the liability because the negation of the restricted does not necessitate the negation of the absolute. However, the passage presents sophisticated objections and rebuttals. The important nuance is that the time is neither a condition nor a mere completive but a 'strong completive' whose loss leaves the absolute obligatory but deficient, incurring sin. The text then presents a counterexample from Hanafi fiqh itself: a vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan. If one fasts Ramadan but fails to perform i'tikaf, the Hanafi chosen view (mukhtar) requires making up the fast with a new fast (on another day). This appears to contradict the principle that the absolute remains, because the Ramadan fast cannot be repeated. Abu Yusuf and Hasan ibn Ziyad held that no qada is needed. The passage defends the Hanafi position by explaining that the vow of i'tikaf obligated the fast as a condition, but the prior Ramadan fast prevented its effect; after Ramadan, the obligation of the fast from the vow manifests, requiring a new fast. Thus, the refutation is not valid against the chosen Hanafi view.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): The unqualified act (e.g., fasting without time specification) that remains demanded in the liability after the restriction is lost.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): The act qualified by a restriction (e.g., fasting on Thursday). The restriction is the time or other condition.
القضاء (al-qada): Making up a missed obligatory act after its time has passed. The dispute is whether qada is by the same original obligation or a new one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in the text, if one vows to perform i'tikaf during Ramadan and fasts Ramadan but fails to perform i'tikaf, what is the ruling?
A) No qada is required at all, as the Ramadan fast suffices.
B) Qada of the fast is not required, but qada of i'tikaf alone is.
C) Qada of the fast is required with a new fast on another day.
D) He must repeat both the fast and the i'tikaf in Ramadan of another year.

Key Takeaway
Time in a restricted command is a strong completive, not a condition; the absolute obligation persists, but delay without excuse incurs sin.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Three Madhabs on the Ruling of the Opposite of a Command
Reviews three scholarly positions on whether a command implies a ruling on its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scholarly positions on the ruling of the opposite of a commanded act and explain why the Hanafi school adopts the position of dislike (karahah) rather than prohibition (tahrim).

Core Idea
A command does not explicitly forbid its opposite; it implies only dislike, because implicit address is weaker than explicit address.

This passage reviews three positions on the ruling of the opposite of a commanded act (ضد المأمور به). The Hanafi position is the third: the opposite is disliked (makruh) unless it nullifies the command, in which case it becomes forbidden. The argument hinges on the concept of 'implicit address' (خطاب الضمن) which is of lower authority than explicit address. This lesson maps out the three views and the Hanafi rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three madhabs: no ruling, prohibition/obligation (al-Jassas), dislike/sunnah (Hanafi). Understand each briefly.
The first position is that command/prohibition has no ruling on the opposite.
2
Understand the second position's proof
Al-Jassas argues that a command logically entails the prohibition of its opposite, and a prohibition entails the obligation of its opposite.
This is based on entailment (iltizam).
3
Grasp the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that entailment does not constitute explicit address; it only gives a weaker ruling (dislike) through implicit address (خطاب الضمن).
Implicit address is lower in rank than explicit address.
4
Apply the concept of implicit address
Recognize that the opposite is only addressed by necessary requirement (iqtida'), not by direct wording. Thus, it does not establish prohibition.
Iqtida' gives the lowest level of ruling.
5
Work through examples
Apply to standing/sitting in prayer and the pilgrim's ihram: the opposite is disliked unless it nullifies the command.
These examples illustrate the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الضد (كما في إيجاب المقدمة فعلى هذا إذا ترك الواجب مع الاشتغال بالضد فالمعصية معصية واحدة هي معصية ترك الواجب، وإنما ينسب إلى الضد بالعرض، وعلى هذا فلا يليق أن يخالف في هذا الحكم وجعل الشيخ ابن الهمام فائدة الخلاف في هذه المسألة أن عند قائلي التضمن أو العينية ،معصيتين وعند المنكر معصية واحدة وعلى هذا فالخطاب عند القائلين بالذات وأصالة إلى الواجب والكف عن الأضداد، وعند المنكرين ليس كذلك، وعلى الدليل قطعاً، لأنه لا يلزم من تعلق الخطاب بالملزوم تعلقه باللازم بالذات، لكن المصنف كما لا يخفى، وحينئذ فالنزاع لا طائل تحته (ومن ها هنا قيل يقتضي) الأمر بالشيء (كراهة ضده، فإن خطاب الضمن أنزل من خطاب الصريح) فلا بد من الفرق بينهما في الإطلاق ليعلم أنه ذكر الإمام فخر الإسلام أولاً ثلاثة مذاهب في ضد المأمور به والمنهي عنه :
هذا فلا يتم الظاهر مع
الأول: أنه لا حكم للأمر والنهي في الضد أصلاً.
الثاني : : وقد نسبه إلى الشيخ أبي بكر الجصاص قدس سره تحریم ضد المأمور به ووجوب ضد المنهي عنه إن كان واحداً.
ثم
الثالث : كراهة ضد المأمور به وكون ضد المنهي عنه في معنى سنة واجبة وقال : هذا أصح عندنا، قال بعد ذكر حجج الفريقين الأولين واحتج الفريق الثالث بأن الأمر على ما قال الجصاص، إلا أنَّا أثبتنا بكل واحد من القسمين أدنى ما يثبت به لأن الثابت بغيره ضرورة لا يساوي المقصود بنفسه وأما الذي اخترناه فبناء على هذا وهو أن هذا لما كان أمراً ضرورياً سميناه اقتضاء، ومعنى الاقتضاء ها هنا أنه ضروري غير مقصود، فصار شبيهاً بما ذكرنا من مقتضيات أحكام الشرع ثم قال بعد عدة سطور: وفائدة هذا الأصل أن التحريم لما لم يكن مقصوداً بالأمر لم يعتبر إلا من حيث يفوّت الأمر، فأما إذا لم يفوّته كان مكروهاً، كالأمر بالقياس ليس بنهي عن القعود قصداً، حتى إذا قعد ثم قام لا تفسد صلاته، ولكنه مكروه، ولهذا قلنا : إن المحرم لما نهي عن لبس المخيط كان من السنة لبس الإزار والرداء إلى آخر ما ذكر من التفريعات كما هو دأبه الشريف، وقد تحير العلماء الأعلام في حل هذا البحث، فحمله بعضهم على ما أشار إليه المصنف وحاصله أن ما يفيده خطاب الضمن وهو أنزل من الصريح لا يثبت به التحريم، بل أنزل منه وهو الكراهة، ومن هذا الوجه سماه كراهية، فلا في المعنى بينه وبين الشيخ أبي بكر، وعلى هذا لا يستقيم قوله : وأما إذا لم يفوته كان مكروهاً، إلا أن يقال من ها هنا شرع في كلام آخر فأراد بالمكروه ها هنا المتعارف من المعنى لا ما ثبت بخطاب غير صريح كما أراد سابقاً، ثم أورد المصنف عليه بقوله: (لكن يلزم على هذا إطلاق المكروه على الممتنع أي الحرام وهو بعيد جداً، وحمله آخرون على أن مقصوده رحمه الله إثبات الكراهة في غير المفوت من الأضداد وتقرير كلامه أنا أثبتنا لكل من الأمر والنهي أدنى درجة، وهو الخطاب الضمني الضروري الذي سميناه اقتضاء باصطلاح، وإذا ثبت الخطاب الضمني ففائدته أن الضد إذا غير مقصود بالحكم بالأمر، ولم يعتبر إلا من حيث يفوّت الأمر لا بالذات، فإذا لم يفوّته لم يكن حراماً بل مكروهاً، وعلى هذا التقرير لم يكن ذكر هذا المذهب في الخلافية مناسباً، ثم إن ما ادعاه رحمه الله بقوله : فإذا لم يفوته كان مكروهاً، غير مفهوم لهذا العبد إلى الآن فإنه إذا لم يفوته لم يتعرض له الأمر، فإن ثبت الكراهة فبدليل آخر لا بحكم الضدية، وأما القعود فإنما لا يفسد لأن القيام ليس فرضاً دائمياً الصلاة، وأما الكراهة فلأن تخلل غير الأفعال الصلاتية فيها مكروه إذا كان من جنسها، لا لأنه ضد الشيء، وأما لبس المحرم الإزار ، فلأنه لما نهي عن لبس المخيط وقد كان الستر فرضاً دائمياً تعين لبس الإزار والرداء ، لا لأنه ضد اللبس المخيط غير مفوت، هكذا الكلام في باقي الفروع لا نطول الكلام بذكره
Regarding the opposite (as in making the introduction obligatory, so if one abandons the obligatory while engaging in the opposite, the sin is one sin: the sin of abandoning the obligatory. It is attributed to the opposite only accidentally. Accordingly, it is not appropriate to differ in this ruling. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam made the benefit of the dispute in this issue that according to the proponents of inclusion or identity, there are two sins; according to the denier, one sin. Accordingly, the address according to the proponents is essentially and originally to the obligatory and refraining from opposites, but according to the deniers it is not so. It is definitely according to proof, because the attachment of the address to the necessary does not necessitate its attachment to the concomitant essentially. But the author, as is not hidden, and therefore the dispute has no benefit under it. (Hence it was said:) The command for a thing (implies the dislike of its opposite, for the implicit address is lower in rank than the explicit address) so it is necessary to differentiate between them in unqualified terms so that it is known that Imam Fakhr al-Islam first mentioned three schools regarding the opposite of the commanded and the forbidden:
This is not complete the apparent with
The first: that there is no ruling of command and prohibition on the opposite at all.
The second: and he attributed it to Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas (may Allah sanctify his soul): the prohibition of the opposite of the commanded and the obligation of the opposite of the forbidden if it is singular.
Then
The third: dislike of the opposite of the commanded and that the opposite of the forbidden is in the meaning of a recommended sunnah, and he said: This is the most correct with us. He said after mentioning the proofs of the first two groups: The third group argued that the command is as al-Jassas said, except that we established for each of the two categories the lowest level that can be established, because what is established by another by necessity is not equal to what is intended by itself. As for what we chose, it is based on this, that since this was a necessary matter, we called it 'requirement', and the meaning of requirement here is that it is necessary not intended. So it became similar to what we mentioned of the requirements of the rulings of the Shariah. Then he said after several lines: The benefit of this principle is that since prohibition was not intended by the command, it is not considered except where it nullifies the command. So if it does not nullify it, it is disliked, like the command to stand is not a prohibition of sitting intentionally, so if one sits and then stands, his prayer is not invalidated, but it is disliked. For this we said: When the pilgrim in ihram is prohibited from wearing sewn garments, it is sunnah to wear a waistcloth and a cloak, to the end of what he mentioned of the derivative rulings, as is his noble habit. The eminent scholars have been perplexed in solving this research. Some interpreted it as what the author indicated, and its summary is that what the implicit address conveys, which is lower than the explicit, does not prove prohibition, but something lower than it, which is dislike. From this aspect he called it dislike, so there is no difference in meaning between him and Shaykh Abu Bakr. On this, his statement 'and if it does not nullify it, it is disliked' is not consistent, unless it is said that from here he started another speech, so he intended by 'disliked' here the conventional meaning, not what is proven by non-explicit address as he intended earlier. Then the author objected to it with his statement: (But this would necessitate labeling the impossible—meaning forbidden—as disliked, which is very far-fetched). Others interpreted that his intent (may Allah have mercy on him) was to establish dislike for the non-nullifying opposites, and the explanation of his statement is: We established for each of command and prohibition the lowest degree, which is the necessary implicit address that we called 'requirement' by convention. Since the implicit address is established, its benefit is that the opposite, since it is not intended by the ruling of the command and is only considered where it nullifies the command, not essentially, so if it does not nullify it, it is not forbidden but disliked. In this interpretation, the mention of this school in the dispute was not appropriate. Then what he claimed (may Allah have mercy on him) in his statement: 'so if it does not nullify it, it is disliked' is not understood by this servant until now. For if it does not nullify it, the command does not address it. If dislike is established, then by another proof, not by the ruling of opposition. As for sitting, it does not invalidate the prayer because standing is not a continuous obligation in prayer. As for the dislike, it is because interposing non-prayer actions in prayer is disliked if they are of the same type, not because it is an opposite. As for the pilgrim in ihram wearing the waistcloth, because when he was prohibited from wearing sewn garments while covering was a continuous obligation, wearing the waistcloth and cloak became determined, not because it is the opposite of sewn garments and non-nullifying. Thus the speech in the remaining branches, we will not lengthen the speech by mentioning it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage discusses the various scholarly positions on the ruling of the opposite of a commanded act (ضد المأمور به). The author presents three established views: (1) No ruling at all from the command or prohibition on the opposite. (2) The opposite of a commanded act is forbidden, and the opposite of a forbidden act is obligatory (attributed to al-Jassas). (3) The opposite of a commanded act is disliked (makruh), and the opposite of a forbidden act is in the meaning of an obligatory sunnah—the preferred Hanafi view. The argument for the third position is based on the concept of 'implicit address' (خطاب الضمن), which is considered lower in rank than explicit address (خطاب الصريح). Thus, a command for something does not explicitly forbid its opposite; rather, it implies dislike if the opposite does not nullify the command. The passage also addresses criticisms and clarifies that the dislike here is technical, not necessarily the conventional meaning. Examples are given from prayer and ihram.

Key Terms
خطاب الضمن (khitab al-damn): The implicit address implied by a command or prohibition, without explicit wording
اقتضاء (iqtida'): The necessary requirement (not intended) that arises from a command, e.g., the requirement to avoid an opposite that nullifies the command
ضد المأمور به (didd al-ma'mur bihi): The opposite action of a commanded act; when one performs the opposite, he abandons the commanded act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is the ruling on the opposite of a commanded act when it does not nullify the command?
A) Forbidden (haram)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Obligatory (wajib)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a command implies the dislike of its opposite through implicit address, not prohibition, unless the opposite nullifies the command.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Command for a Thing Does Not Necessarily Prohibit Its Opposite
Analyzing whether commanding something entails forbidding its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position that a command does not automatically forbid its opposite, and evaluate the arguments for and against this view.

Core Idea
A command does not inherently forbid its opposite; only time-restricted obligations incidentally prohibit the opposite at the time of performance.

This passage from Fawātih al-Raḥmūt dives into one of the most debated questions in Usul al-Fiqh: Does the command for an act (e.g., prayer) simultaneously forbid its opposite (e.g., sitting)? The Hanafi school decisively answers no, rejecting both the identity thesis (command = prohibition) and the entailment thesis (command → prohibition). In this lesson, you will trace the logical arguments, understand the Hanafi proofs, and see how this principle affects real rulings, such as whether sitting during prayer is haram or merely makruh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputing positions
Recognize the three positions: identity (command is identical to prohibition), entailment (command implies prohibition), and independence (Hanafi view: no necessary relation).
2
Follow the Hanafi proof
Understand the key argument: 'al-amr la yaqtadi al-isti'ab' (the command does not require continuity), so the opposite is not perpetually forbidden.
3
Analyze the counter-arguments and responses
Examine the objections regarding prayer vs. Hajj and zinā vs. liwāṭ, and how the Hanafi response resolves them.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: sitting during prayer is makruh tanzihi, not haram.
5
Consider the time-restriction condition
Note that for mudayyaq obligations, the opposite may be prohibited incidentally, but for musawwa' obligations, it is not.

المقالة الثانية في الأحكام حيث يفوّت الأمر لا بالذات، فإذا لم يفوّته لم يكن حراماً بل مكروهاً، وعلى هذا التقرير لم يكن ذكر هذا المذهب في الخلافية مناسباً، ثم إن ما ادعاه رحمه الله بقوله : فإذا لم
