القائلين بالعينية، قال القاضي منهم : أولاً : لو لم يكن الأمر بشيء هو النهي عن الضد فهما إما مثلان أو ضدان أو خلافان، وعلى الأولين يلزم أن لا يصح الاجتماع، ويصح بالضرورة إذ لا استحالة في الأمر بشيء والنهي عن ضده وبالعكس، وعلى الثالث فيمكن اجتماع الأمر بالشيء مع ضد النهي عن الضد وضده أمر به، فيلزم اجتماع الأمر بالشيء مع الأمر بضده هذا خلف قلنا خلافان، ولا نسلم لزوم إمكان الأمر مع ضد النهي عن الضد، فإنه يجوز التلازم بين الأمر والنهي عن الضد، فلا يصح الانفكاك، نعم يلزم التضمن كما علمت ولعله لهذا رجع القاضي عنها إلى التضمن، وثانياً: إن السكون ترك الحركة، فالأمر بالسكون طلب لترك الحركة وهو النهي عن الحركة قلنا: الأضداد التي هي سلوب المأمورات مسلم أنه عين تركها لكنها خارجة عن النزاع، فإنه في الأضداد الثابتة الجزئية، وأما كون كل ضد مأمور به تركاً له فممنوع كيف وليس الأكل نفس ترك الصلاة، نعم ترك الضد من لوازم وجود المأمور به فالأمر به ملزوم النهي عن الضد، وظن المخصص العينية أو التضمن بالأمر إما أن النهي لا يقتضي إلا نفي الفعل وليس وجود الضد عينه أو لازم لجواز انتفاء الفعل بانتفاء المقتضى لا لوجود المانع ، وقد مر أنه وارد، وأما لزوم وجوب المحرمات، وقد مر الجواب عنه، وأما لزوم انتفاء المباح ، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى حاله، فظن المخصص بأمر الوجوب أحد الأمرين الأخيرين إذ ما من وقت إلا وفيه مندوب، فيلزم أن يكون كل مباح مكروهاً، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ما ينكشف به حاله، المنكرون للعينية والتضمن قالوا: لو كان الأمر بشيء هو بعينه النهي عن الضد أو ملزوماً له وبالعكس، من الأمر بشيء والنهي عن شيء تعقل الأضداد والتالي باطل بالضرورة، أما الملازمة فلأنه لزم لا يعقل أمر ولا نهي من غير تعقل متعلقهما قلنا : لزوم التعقل فيما يكون التكليف به بأمر أو نهي صريحاً أو لازماً بيناً لتكليف صريح وليس الأمر فيما نحن فيه، كذلك فإن النهي عن الضد لازم للأمر لزوماً غير بين وإن ادعى أنه بين بالمعنى الأعم، وأجيب في المشهور بأن المنفي تعقل ضد ضد وأما تعقل مطلق الضد فضروري، لأن الأمر لا يكون إلا حال انعدام المأمور به وإلا لزم طلب الحاصل، وعدمه لا يكون إلا باشتغال الضد فلزم تعقل الضد المطلق، والحاصل أن تعقل الأضداد الجزئية على التفصيل غير ضروري وعدمه مسلم، وأما تعقلها بالوجه الأعم كالضدية فلازم ضروري ومتحقق ها هنا فلا يرد أنه إذا سلم انتفاء تعقل ضد ضد فقد سلم ما ادعى المستدل فإن الكلام في أن الأضداد الجزئية منهي عنها أم لا كما
All from 'forbidden'... The second article on rulings: where the command is missed not by essence. If it does not miss it, it is not haram but makruh. On this basis, mentioning this madhhab in the dispute is not appropriate. Then what he claimed, may Allah have mercy on him, with his statement: 'If it does not miss it, it is makruh' is not understood by this servant until now, because if it does not miss it, the command does not apply to it. If makruh is established, it is by another evidence, not by the rule of opposition. As for sitting, it does not invalidate because standing is not a continuous obligation of prayer. As for the dislike, it is because inserting non-prayer actions in it is disliked if they are of its kind, not because it is the opposite of the thing. As for the pilgrim wearing the izār, it is because when he was forbidden from wearing sewn garments and covering was a continuous obligation, wearing the izār and ridā' became specified, not because it is the opposite of wearing sewn garments and not missing [the obligation]. This is the discussion in the remaining branches; we do not lengthen the discussion by mentioning it. If you say: 'The command for a thing is a prohibition of its opposite by identity, the opposite is forbidden, and the prohibition of the opposite entails the command of the other opposite by choice, so this other opposite is forbidden by identity and commanded by choice, thus obligation and prohibition meet in one thing.' This is a contradiction. I say: Possibility with respect to one thing does not negate impossibility by essence nor impossibility with respect to another thing. Similarly, the commanded and obligation with respect to one thing does not negate prohibition with respect to another thing, so there is no impossibility in their conjunction. And if it is said: The prohibition of the opposite of the obligatory is because it misses it, and avoidance of it is not desired by essence but for performing the obligatory; the opposite of the prohibited is obligatory so that avoidance of it is achieved, and the opposite of this opposite is desired so that avoidance of this opposite is achieved, and it is only for achieving the obligatory. If the opposite of the opposite is also an opposite of the obligatory, it is not obligatory or desired for avoidance of this opposite because avoidance of it in this way was not desired, but in a way that accompanies the performance of the obligatory—it is not far-fetched, rather more appropriate, as is not hidden from the contemplative. It should not be said: It entails on the first—which is that the obligation of a thing implies the prohibition of its opposite—the prohibition of obligations, for among obligations are those that are opposites of other obligations (like the prohibition of prayer in that it is opposite of Hajj, because prayer pillars do not coexist with Hajj pillars and vice versa), i.e., the prohibition of Hajj in that it is opposite of prayer. And it entails on the second—which is that the prohibition of a thing implies the obligation of its opposite—the obligation of forbidden things, even if by choice, for among forbidden things are those that are opposites of other forbidden things (like the obligation of zinā because it is leaving liwāṭ, since the two insertions cannot coexist, and vice versa), i.e., obligation of liwāṭ because it is leaving zinā. We say regarding the first: The command does not require continuity, so it is not a perpetual prohibition of the opposite, but only at these times; there is no shame in committing. So performing prayer in a way that Hajj is left is absolutely forbidden. Then the opposite of the obligatory can be done at another time. Hence it was said: The condition for prohibiting the opposite of the obligatory is that the obligatory be time-restricted (mudayyaq), for the expansive (musawwa') does not necessitate the prohibition of the opposite, since it may be left. The correct view is that this restriction is not needed, because just as the expansive is obligatory in a part of the time, likewise engaging in its opposite or opposites in that time; the prohibition is according to the obligation. But this entails that Hajj's time is not the entire lifetime, because it is forbidden in a part of the time, while there is consensus that the entire lifetime is its time. Likewise for prayer, its entire prescribed time would not be its time. Unless it is said in response: That (i.e., the entire lifetime is its time) is considered with respect to itself, so that performing Hajj any time is valid, and there is no blame for it; the prohibition only came in some times with respect to leaving another obligation. And we say regarding the second: The specification of prohibition by an original evidence that necessitates it removes the place from accepting choice as a corollary, for the Wise is not to command something to avoid the forbidden while it is itself like that forbidden. So abstention from zinā absolutely, even by liwāṭ, is not demanded, rather specific abstention. Thus obligation of liwāṭ is not entailed; ponder it. It can be answered that there is no conflict between subsidiary obligation by choice and essential prohibition; so reflect. The followers of other madhhabs have weak views mentioned in the extended works along with their criticisms; refer to them. And we mention them: Know that those who claim identity, among them al-Qāḍī said first: If the command for a thing were not the prohibition of its opposite, then they would be either similar, opposites, or contraries. On the first two, they would not be able to coexist, but by necessity they can coexist, since there is no impossibility in commanding a thing and forbidding its opposite and vice versa. On the third, it is possible for command of a thing to coexist with the opposite of the prohibition of the opposite, and its opposite is commanded by it, leading to command of a thing coexisting with command of its opposite—this is a contradiction. We said: They are contraries, and we do not accept the necessary possibility of command coexisting with the opposite of the prohibition of the opposite, because a necessary connection between command and prohibition of the opposite is possible, so separation is not possible. Indeed, entailment is necessary as you know; perhaps for this al-Qāḍī retracted from it to entailment. Second: Rest is the leaving of motion; the command for rest is a request for leaving motion, which is the prohibition of motion. We said: Opposites that are negations of commanded things—it is accepted that they are identical to their leaving, but they are outside the dispute, because it is about particular established opposites. As for every opposite being commanded as its leaving, it is denied; how is eating not the same as leaving prayer? Yes, leaving the opposite is one of the necessary concomitants of the existence of the commanded, so command of it is necessary for the prohibition of the opposite. The one who claimed identity or entailment thought that the prohibition requires only negation of the act, and the existence of the opposite is not identical to it or a necessary concomitant, because the act may be absent due to absence of the motive, not due to existence of a preventive; and this was already discussed. As for the entailment of the obligation of forbidden things, the answer was already given. As for the entailment of the negation of permissible things, its case will come later, God willing. So the one who claimed obligation thought one of the last two, since there is no time except that there is a recommended act in it, so every permissible would be makruh. Its clarification will come later, God willing. Those who deny identity and entailment said: If the command for a thing were identical to the prohibition of its opposite or a necessary concomitant of it and vice versa, then from the command for a thing and prohibition of a thing, opposites are conceptualized. The consequent is necessarily false. As for the necessity, it is because it would entail that no command or prohibition is conceptualized without conceptualizing their objects. We said: The necessity of conceptualization is regarding that which is obliged by an explicit command or prohibition or an obvious necessary concomitant of an explicit obligation; but the case here is not like that, because the prohibition of the opposite is a non-obvious necessary concomitant of the command, even if he claimed it is obvious in a broader sense. It was answered in the famous view that what is denied is the conceptualization of opposite of opposite; as for conceptualization of absolute opposite, it is necessary, because the command occurs only when the commanded thing is absent, otherwise it would be demanding the attained; its absence is only by engagement in its opposite, so conceptualization of absolute opposite is necessary. The result is that conceptualization of particular opposites in detail is not necessary—its absence is accepted—but conceptualization in a general sense like opposition is necessarily necessary and exists here. So it is not refuted that if the denial of conceptualization of opposite of opposite is accepted, then what the arguer claimed is accepted, because the discussion is whether particular opposites are forbidden or not, as...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the relationship between commanding something (al-amr) and forbidding its opposite (al-nahy 'an al-didd). The author (likely from the Hanafi tradition) is commenting on a matn that presented the view of those who claim that commanding something is identical to forbidding its opposite ('ayniyya) or at least entails it (tadamman). The text proceeds by examining various objections and responses. The passage first discusses the case where missing the commanded act is not by essence (i.e., the opposite act does not inherently nullify the obligation) but still may be makruh, not haram. The author clarifies that dislike (karaha) does not automatically follow from opposition but requires a separate evidence. Then the discussion turns to a direct objection: if commanding a thing were forbidding its opposite, then the opposite of the opposite would be both forbidden and commanded, leading to a contradiction. The Hanafi response is that while there is no impossibility in combining obligation and prohibition from different perspectives, the command does not necessarily prohibit the opposite unless the obligatory is time-restricted (mudayyaq). The text then addresses two main entailments: (1) obligation of a thing implies prohibition of its opposite leads to prohibition of some obligations (e.g., prayer vs. Hajj) and (2) prohibition of a thing implies obligation of its opposite leads to obligation of some prohibitions (e.g., zinā vs. liwāṭ). The Hanafi answers by explaining that the command does not require perpetuity, so the prohibition is only at specific times when the opposite is actually being performed. Regarding the second, the prohibition of a forbidden act is absolute, and its opposite is not demanded absolutely but only a specific way of abstaining. The passage then mentions the views of other madhhabs summarily and refers to fuller works. Finally, it discusses the argument of those who deny both identity and entailment, focusing on the necessity of conceptualizing opposites. The author concludes that conceptualization of particular opposites in detail is not necessary for the command, only absolute opposition is understood. Overall, this is a classic Hanafi stance: the command for a thing does not directly forbid its opposite, but under certain conditions (like time-restricted obligations) it may imply prohibition. The Hanafi position avoids the logical extremes of claiming identity or absolute entailment, which would lead to absurdities.

Key Terms
الضد (al-didd): A contradictory opposite that cannot coexist with the commanded act, e.g., sitting vs. standing in prayer.
الوجوب التبعي (al-wujūb al-taba'ī): A subsidiary obligation that arises as a consequence of avoiding a prohibition, e.g., performing a specific act to avoid a forbidden one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, does the command for an obligatory act (e.g., prayer) automatically make its opposite (e.g., sitting) haram?
A) Yes, always, because the command forbids the opposite by identity.
B) No, because the command does not require continuity, so the opposite is only prohibited at the time of performance, and may be makruh otherwise.
C) Yes, but only if the opposite is inherently evil.
D) No, the opposite is always permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a command does not inherently forbid its opposite; the prohibition only arises incidentally with time-restricted obligations.

The Distinction Between One by Genus and One by Type
Explains the concept of one by type and the possibility of combining obligation and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'one by genus' and 'one by type' in Hanafi usul and explain why combining obligation and prohibition is permissible in one by genus but disputed in one by type.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition can coexist in one by genus but conflict in one by type — a key Hanafi distinction.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into a subtle but critical distinction in usul al-fiqh: the difference between considering a legal reality as a generic category (jins) versus a specific type (naw'). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how the Hanafi school analyzes commands and prohibitions that seem to address the same act. We will unpack the commentator's argument and identify the disputed point.

Guided Path
1
Define the two conceptual categories
Identify 'one by genus' (al-wahid bi'l-jins) and 'one by type' (al-wahid bi'l-naw'). Note how obligation and prohibition attach differently.
In one by genus, the obligation and prohibition refer to distinct realities; in one by type, they refer to the same reality.
2
Understand the logic of combination in one by genus
In one by genus, obligation is to perform some type, and prohibition is to refrain from another type. No contradiction arises. The passage states this is agreed upon.
3
Analyze the logic of combination in one by type
In one by type, both obligation and prohibition attach to the same reality. Obligation requires performing it once, prohibition requires never performing it. This leads to a prima facie conflict when applied to the same individual.
4
Identify the dispute
The commentator notes that the combination in one by type is disputed. Some scholars allow it from different aspects; others reject it. Recognize the basis of this dispute.
5
Apply the distinction
Use this distinction to analyze textual scenarios where a command and prohibition appear to cover the same act. Determine whether the act is one by genus or one by type.
Example: 'Pray on time' and 'Do not pray when drunk' — the act is one by genus because obligation and prohibition address different aspects (time vs. state).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام وحاصل الوجه الثاني أن وجوب النوع عبارة عن وجوب الإتيان بفرض ما، وحرمته عن وجوب الكف عن جميع الأفراد فيلزم اجتماع المتنافيين في شخص واحد، اجتماعهما إلا من جهتين بخلاف الواحد بالجنس، لأن وجوبه عبارة عن وجوب فلا يصح نوع ما وحرمته عن لزوم الكف عن نوع ولا تنافي ولو أريد بتحريمه لزوم الكف عن جميع أفراده فهو تحريم لهذه الحقيقة المطلوبة الكف، فهو نوع بهذه الجهة، كما أنه إن حرم النوع في شخص وأوجب في آخر فهو بهذا الاعتبار اعتبر مبهما فهو جنس والحاصل أنه إن اعتبرت حقيقة مبهمة، وأوجبت باعتبار تحصلاتها المبهمة، فهي الواحد بالجنس في هذا الاصطلاح، وهذا النحو من الاجتماع جائز باتفاق من يعتد باتفاقهم، وإن اعتبرت نفسها وأوجب الإتيان بها بإتيان واحد من الأفراد ولم يلاحظ خصوص التحصل منها أو حرمت نفسها، بأن يكون المقصود عدم الإتيان بها نفسها لا بخصوص تخصصها فهو المراد من الواحد بالنوع، وهذا النحو من الاجتماع متنازع فيه ولا شك أن التعبير بالواحد بالنوع أولى من الواحد بالشخص هذا غاية التوجيه لكلام المصنف فافهم
The second chapter on rulings. And the summary of the second aspect is that the obligation of the type (naw') is an expression for the obligation of performing a certain obligation, and its prohibition is an expression for the obligation of refraining from all individuals, so the requirement is a combination of the two opposites in one person, their combination is only from two aspects, unlike the one by genus, because its obligation is an expression for obligation of a certain type not sound? And its prohibition is from the obligation to refrain from a type, and there is no opposition. And if by its prohibition is meant the obligation to refrain from all its individuals, then it is a prohibition of this reality that requires refraining, so it is a type from this aspect. Just as if the type is prohibited in one person and obligated in another, then from this consideration it is considered ambiguous, so it is a genus. The summary is that if an ambiguous reality is considered, and obligated in terms of its ambiguous realizations, then it is the one by genus in this terminology, and this type of combination is permissible by agreement of those whose agreement is counted. And if it is considered itself, and the performance of it by performing one of the individuals is obligated, without considering the particular realization of it, or it itself is prohibited, meaning the intention is not to perform it itself without particular specification, then this is what is meant by the one by type. And this type of combination is disputed. And there is no doubt that the expression by the one by type is more appropriate than the one by person. This is the utmost guidance for the words of the author, so understand.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that elaborates on the combination of wajib and haram in 'one by type' (al-wahid bi'l-naw'). The commentator distinguishes between one by genus and one by type. In one by genus, the obligation is to perform some type, and the prohibition is to refrain from another type, so no contradiction. In one by type, the obligation is to perform the reality itself (by performing one of its individuals), and the prohibition is to refrain from that same reality (i.e., not to perform it at all). This leads to a contradiction when considering the same individual, but it is permissible from different aspects. The commentator notes that this type of combination is disputed, and that the expression 'one by type' is more precise than 'one by person.' The conclusion is that if the reality is considered ambiguous and the obligation is on its ambiguous realizations, it is one by genus and permissible by agreement. If the reality itself is considered, it is one by type and subject to dispute.

Key Terms
الواحد بالجنس (al-wahid bi'l-jins): An ambiguous reality considered in terms of its ambiguous realizations; obligation or prohibition attaches to the genus without specifying a particular type.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wahid bi'l-naw'): A single type considered as a reality; obligation or prohibition attaches to the type itself such that performing an individual fulfills the obligation or violates the prohibition of the same type.
الواحد بالشخص (al-wahid bi'l-shakhs): A single individual act; obligation and prohibition attach to that specific instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the context of one by type (al-wahid bi'l-naw'), what does the obligation refer to?
A) Obligation to perform a specific individual act.
B) Obligation to perform the type itself by performing one of its individuals.
C) Obligation to refrain from all individuals of the type.
D) Obligation to perform the genus without specifying any type.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul distinction between one by genus and one by type resolves apparent conflicts between commands and prohibitions by analyzing whether they address the same reality or different realities.

Types of Prohibition Attachment to One of Several Things
Explains four types of prohibition attachment to one of multiple things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four ways prohibition attaches to one of multiple things and identify the legal consequence (general negation, non-gathering, or avoidance of aggregate) for each.

Core Idea
Prohibition of one thing among many can mean prohibit all, prohibit the combination, or prohibit the aggregate depending on wording.

In Hanafi usul, the phrase 'do not do X or Y' can carry different legal implications. The author of this passage dissects four possible ways the prohibition of one among several things can be understood. This parsing exercise will help you see how the wording (or, and, ambiguous) determines whether the prohibition targets every individual, the combination, or something else. Understanding these nuances prevents misinterpretation of prohibitory texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase structure
Examine whether the prohibition uses 'or', 'and', or ambiguous wording. Note the conjunction and sentence construction.
2
Classify the attachment of avoidance
Determine if the avoidance (tark) is attached to the concept of one, to the truth of one, to the aggregate, or if it is inherently ambiguous.
Refer to the four types: (1) concept, (2) truth as title, (3) aggregate, (4) ambiguous avoidance.
3
Determine the legal implication
For each type, infer the consequence: Does it require avoiding all individuals (general negation), prevent combining them (non-gathering), or prevent the aggregate?
Type 1: universal negation; Type 2: not universal; Types 3 and 4: non-gathering.
4
Apply to Quranic example
Analyze the verse 'Do not obey among them a sinner or an ungrateful one' (76:24) to see which type it represents.
This is Type 1: avoidance attached to the concept of one, yielding general negation.
5
Synthesize the three similar types
Note that the last three types all ultimately aim to prevent combination, differing only in linguistic method.
The third type (waaw) and fourth type (aw with ambiguous avoidance) both prevent combination; the second type is a midpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (يجوز تحريم أحد أشياء من الأشياء المعلومة (كإيجابه ، فهناك) أي في الأمر (المقصود منع الخلو) لأن الإتيان بأحدها لا يكون إلا بأن لا يخل بها جميعاً وها هنا) أي في «تحريم» أحدها المقصود (منع الجمع) لأن المقصود الاجتناب عن واحد وذلك إما بالاجتناب عن الكل أو عن واحد فقط، فامتنع الجمع وفيها ما تقدم في الواجب المخير دليلاً واختلافاً) واعلم أنه لما كان لمتوهم أن يتوهم أنه قد تقرر أن تحريم الواحد المبهم تحريم بكل فرد، وسيصرح كلمة أو، فكيف يكون لمنع الجمع أفاد (اعلم أن تعلق الترك بأحد أشياء على أنحاء أحدها أن يتعلق) الترك بمفهوم أحدها فيفيد التعميم) فلا يجوز إتيان واحد أصلاً لأن عدم الطبيعة إنما يكون بعدم جميع الأفراد وفيه أنه قد يكون عدم الطبيعة بعدم فرد واحد، فإنه قد حقق أنه إذا انتفى فرد فقد انتفى الطبيعة من حيث هي في الجملة، وسيجيء تحقيقه وتفصيله، أحكاماً ورداً إن شاء الله تعالى في مقام يليق به والتعميم هو المتبادر إلى الفهم من كلمة أو بعد النهي (نحو : ولا تطع منهُمْ ءَائِمًا أَوْ كَفُورًا ﴾ [الإنسان : ٢٤] والثاني : أن يتعلق الترك (بما صدق عليه مفهوم أحدها ويكون هذا المفهوم عنواناً وشرحاً للمنهي عنه غير مقصود بالذات بالنهي (فيفيد إما عدم ذلك) الفرد (ويتعلق بمفهوم أحدها بالعرض بناء على أن كل ما اتصف عدم به الفرد اتصف به الطبيعة في الجملة فلا يفيد هذا لنحو من الترك (عموم السلب) وهو المراد ها هنا (والثالث : أن يتعلق الترك (بالمجموع) من الأشياء (فيفيد عدم الاجتماع، وذلك فيما إذا كان العطف فيه بالواو، نحو لا تأكل السمك واللبن) أي مجموعهما، وهذا النحو ليس أنحاء تعلق الترك بأحد أشياء إلا أنه تسامح والرابع: أن يكون الترك نفسه مبهما) بالذات، أما ترك هذا أو ترك ذلك لا المتروك) إلا بالعرض (وذلك إذا كان العطف بأو، والمقصود عدم الجمع، نحو: لا تأكل السمك أو اللبن، والأظهر أنه حينئذ من عطف الجملة على الجملة ليعاد في الشقوق معنى الترك، ويكون الترديد بين التروك، ولا يخفى عليك أن مآل الأنحاء الثلاثة الأخيرة ،واحد إنما التفاوت في الطرق، فإن المقصود في الكل منع الجمع هكذا ينبغي أن يحقق هذا المقام) .
Issue: It is permissible to prohibit one of several known things (like making it obligatory). There, i.e., in the command, the purpose is to prevent voidness because performing one of them is only possible by not leaving them all. Here, i.e., in prohibiting one of them, the purpose is to prevent combination because the aim is to avoid one, which is achieved either by avoiding all or by avoiding only one, so combination is prevented. In this issue, there is what preceded regarding the optional obligatory in terms of proof and difference. He said: Know that the attachment of avoidance to one of several things has types: First, that avoidance attaches to the concept of one of them, so it indicates generalization, and it is not permissible to do any one at all because the non-existence of the nature is only by the non-existence of all individuals. But there is also that non-existence of the nature may be by non-existence of one individual, for it is established that if one individual is negated, the nature is negated in general. Its verification and detail, in terms of rulings and refutation, will come in an appropriate place, God willing. Generalization is the immediate understanding from the word 'or' after a prohibition, like: 'And do not obey among them a sinner or an ungrateful one' (Quran 76:24). Second, that avoidance attaches to what the concept of one of them applies to, and this concept is a title and explanation of the prohibited, not intended by itself in the prohibition, so it indicates either non-existence of that individual and attaches to the concept of one of them incidentally, based on the principle that everything characterized by the non-existence of the individual is characterized by the non-existence of the nature in general. So this type of avoidance does not indicate universal negation, which is what is meant here. Third, that avoidance attaches to the aggregate of things, so it indicates non-gathering, as when the conjunction is with 'and', e.g., 'Do not eat fish and milk', i.e., their aggregate. This type is not among the types of attachment of avoidance to one of several things except by leniency. Fourth, that avoidance itself is ambiguous, i.e., avoiding this or avoiding that, not the avoided, except incidentally, as when the conjunction is with 'or' and the purpose is non-gathering, e.g., 'Do not eat fish or milk'. The most apparent is that it is from the conjunction of a sentence to a sentence to repeat the meaning of avoidance in the branches, and the hesitation is between the avoidances. It is not hidden from you that the outcome of the last three types is one; the difference is only in methods, for the purpose in all is to prevent combination. This is how this topic should be investigated.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding the prohibition of one among multiple things, explaining the author's analysis of the different ways the prohibition can attach. The author distinguishes four types: first, general avoidance of all individuals (e.g., 'Do not obey a sinner or an ungrateful one') where the prohibition applies to each; second, avoidance of the truth of one individual with the concept as a title, which does not imply universal negation; third, avoidance of the aggregate (e.g., 'Do not eat fish and milk') where the prohibition is on the combination; fourth, ambiguous avoidance with 'or' where the purpose is non-gathering. The author concludes that the last three essentially aim at preventing combination, differing only in method. This is a Hanafi usul analysis of how prohibition operates with disjunctive and conjunctive phrasing. The text also references the Quranic verse (76:24) as an example of generalization with 'or' after prohibition.

Key Terms
الترك (at-tark): Avoidance or abandonment of an action.
منع الجمع (man' al-jam'): Prevention of combining multiple actions/objects.

Effect of Jest (Huzl) on Legal Acts
Rule with intra-madhhab dispute and detailed sub-cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on jest (huzl) to contracts and other legal acts, distinguishing between acts that admit rescission and those that do not, and evaluate the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, jest invalidates only if the parties thereafter show aversion; silence or construction indicates seriousness, making the act valid.

This passage unpacks the Hanafi position on legal acts performed in jest. While jest might seem to negate intention, the school carefully distinguishes between acts where the parties follow up with aversion, construction, or silence. The ruling hinges on the principle that the origin in contracts is validity, and jest is only given effect when the parties actively demonstrate rejection. This lesson walks you through the key scenarios and the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act type
First, determine whether the act is a contract (sale, marriage, etc.) or an act that does not admit rescission (divorce, manumission). This classification affects how jest is treated.
Acts not admitting rescission: jest has no effect; the act is valid regardless.
2
Determine the presence of jest (huzl) and the parties' post-jest behavior
Check if the parties explicitly agreed to jest (muwada'a). Then examine their subsequent actions: aversion (i'rad), construction (bina'), or silence (sukut). These three possibilities lead to different rulings.
Aversion = clear rejection; construction = acting as if serious; silence = no clear indicator.
3
Apply the Hanafi position based on behavior
If both parties show aversion, the act is void. If both construct, it is valid. If both are silent, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa says valid (origin of seriousness), while the Sahibayn say void (origin of jest). For disagreement cases, the majority rule applies.
Refer to the 72 forms of disagreement derived from combinations of aversion, construction, and silence.
4
Check special cases: jest in price amount or type
If the jest concerns the price (amount or currency type), additional rules apply. For amount, Abu Hanifa considers the contract valid with the lower price; the Sahibayn void unless both averse. For type (e.g., dirhams vs dinars), contract is valid by consensus because jest would leave sale without consideration.
These sub-rules protect the contract from complete nullity.
5
Consider acts that do not admit rescission
For divorce, manumission, oath, and similar acts, jest has no effect regardless of post-jest behavior. These are binding by nature and cannot be dissolved through jest. Marriage is partially affected only in the dower amount.
The reason is that for these acts, the element of financial obligation is either absent or subsidiary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن أبطل بطل، وإن أجاز جاز في ثلاثة أيام عنده وفي أي وقت شاء عندهما، وينبغي أن لا تصح الإجازة عند زفر وإن اتفقا في السكوت فالاعتبار للعقد عنده لا للهزل، إذ الأصل فيه أن يكون صحيحاً، وعندهما للهزل، إذ الموجود لا يبطل إلا بمبطل ولا مبطل ها هنا، إذ السكوت ليس إعراضاً، والعذر له أن الإقدام على العقد ناسخ للمواضعة فتأمل، وإن اختلفا في البناء والإعراض أو البناء والسكوت أو السكوت والإعراض، فعنده القول تسعة، وإذا أخذ كل واحد منها مع الثمانية الباقية في دعوى الآخر تكون اثنتين وسبعين، هذا: والقول بالصحة مع دعوى كل منهما بناء الآخر دون نفسه بعيد كما لا يخفى على المتأمل، وإن كان الهزل في القدر فالاعتبار للعقد عنده في الصور كلها لأنه لو اعتبر المواضعة في الزائد ويكون الثمن هو الأقل يلزم اشتراط ما ليس بثمن في العقد فيفسد، وحينئذ يلزم إبطال الأصل للوصف وعندهما للهزل إلا في صورة الإعراض منهما، إذ الهزل أصل لا يهدر إلا بمبطل، وإن كان الهزل في جنس الثمن بأن وضعوا أن يكون دراهم ويذكروا دنانير فالعبرة للعقد بالاتفاق، لأنه لو اعتبر الهزل بطل المسمى ويبقى البيع بلا بدل بخلاف الهزل في القدر والبناء عليه، لأنه إذا عمل بالهزل يبقى البيع بأقل الثمن والزيادة وإن كان شرطاً فاسداً إلا أنه لا مطالب له من جهة العبد ولا يورث الفساد ومنها ما لا يحتمل النقض فإما أن لا يكون يلزم فيه المال أصلاً، فلا يؤثر فيه الهزل، كالطلاق والعتاق والرجعة واليمين والعفو عن القصاص للنص في الرجعة والطلاق والنكاح وغيرها مقيس عليها بجامع أنها إنشاءات لا تحتمل الفسخ أو يلزم فيه المال وكان تبعاً كالنكاح فإن كان الهزل في أصل النكاح فالعقد لازم، وإن كان في القدر فإن اتفقا على الإعراض فالمسمى لازم وإن اتفقا على البناء فالأقل بالاتفاق، أما عنده فلأنه يمكن العمل بالهزل ها هنا لأن الأقل يكون مهراً، والزائد شرطاً فاسداً لا يفسد به النكاح، وإن اتفقا على السكوت أو اختلفا ولم يتفقا على شيء فالأقل في رواية الإمام محمد لما بينا، وفي رواية الإمام أبي يوسف المسمى، وفي «التحرير» وهو أصح، لأن العقل لا يجوز العاقل على الهزل، فكأنهما بدأ بالعقد الجديد، وعندهما الاعتبار للهزل، لأنه هو الأصل عندهما كما مر وإن كان في الجنس فإن اتفقا على الإعراض فالمسمى اتفاقاً أو على البناء، فمهر المثل اتفاقاً لأنه لا مسمى حينئذ، فبقي النكاح بلا بدل، وفيه مهر المثل، وإن اتفقا على السكوت أو اختلفا فمهر المثل عندهما وعنده في رواية الإمام محمد، والمسمى في رواية الإمام أبي يوسف، وقد تقدم الوجهان أو يلزم فيه المال ويكون مقصوداً من العقد، كالخلع والصلح عن دم العمد والعتق على المال، فعندهما الهزل لغو ويجب المسمى، لأنه غير قابل لخيار الشرط عندهما، وعنده يتوقف على اختيارهما إن بنيا إذ يصح خيار الشرط عنده فيهما، وإن أعرضا بطل الهزل وتم العقد، وإن سكتا أو اختلفا فالقول لمدعي الجد عنده ولمدعي البناء عندهما، لكن يبطل الهزل ويجب المال ويقع الطلاق، والثاني: أي الإخبارات لا صحة لها أصلاً، لأن الهزل قرينة على عدم المحكى عنه، وإنما كان الحجة باعتباره فلا تصح الإقرارت أصلاً والثالث أي الاعتقادات لا تصح مع الهزل أيضاً إلا أنه يكفر بالهزل لا لتبدل الاعتقادات بل لأن الهزل استخفاف بالدين
If he invalidates, it is invalid; if he permits, it is permitted within three days according to him, and at any time he wishes according to them. It should be that permission is not valid according to Zufar. If they agree on silence, then consideration is given to the contract according to him, not to jest, because the origin is that it is valid. According to them, consideration is to jest, because what exists is not invalidated except by an invalidator, and there is no invalidator here, since silence is not aversion. His excuse is that undertaking the contract abrogates the agreement, so consider. If they disagree on construction and aversion, or construction and silence, or silence and aversion, then according to him the word is of one who affirms validity, because validity is the origin. According to them, the word is of the agreement because it is the origin. In Al-Tahrir, the forms of agreement are six: their aversion, their construction, their silence, aversion of one with construction of the other, or with silence of the other, and construction of one with silence of the other. The forms of disagreement are seventy-two: either their aversion, or their construction, or their silence, or his own aversion with his companion's construction or with his silence, or with his own construction his companion's aversion or silence, or his own silence with his companion's aversion or construction. These are nine. When each one is taken with the remaining eight in the claim of the other, they become seventy-two. This: the statement of validity with the claim of each that the other constructed without himself is far-fetched, as is not hidden from the contemplator. If the jest is in the amount, then consideration is to the contract according to him in all forms, because if the agreement were considered in the excess and the price becomes the lesser, it would necessitate stipulating what is not a price in the contract, corrupting it. Then it would necessitate invalidating the origin for the attribute. According to them, consideration is to jest except in the form of aversion from both, because jest is an origin not wasted except by an invalidator. If the jest is in the type of price, such as if they agreed that it should be dirhams and they mention dinars, then the criterion is the contract by agreement, because if jest were considered, the named price would be void and the sale would remain without consideration, unlike jest in amount and construction upon it, because if jest is acted upon, the sale remains with the lesser price, and the excess, even if it is a corrupt condition, cannot be demanded from the slave and does not cause corruption. Among them is what does not admit rescission: either it does not involve financial obligation at all, so jest does not affect it, like divorce, manumission, revocable return, oath, and pardon for qisas, due to the text on revocable return, divorce, marriage, and others analogized upon them (i.e., divorce, etc.) by the common factor that they are creatory acts that do not admit dissolution; or it involves financial obligation and is subsidiary, like marriage. If the jest is in the origin of marriage, the contract is binding. If it is in the amount, if they agree on aversion, the named dower is binding; if they agree on construction, the lesser is binding by agreement. As for him (Abu Hanifa), because jest can be operative here: the lesser becomes the dower, and the excess is a corrupt condition that does not corrupt the marriage. If they agree on silence or disagree and do not agree on anything, then the lesser in the narration of Imam Muhammad, as we explained; in the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf, the named dower. In Al-Tahrir, it is more correct because a rational person does not permit jest, so it is as if they started a new contract. According to them, consideration is to jest, because it is the origin as previously. If it is in the type, if they agree on aversion, the named dower by agreement; or on construction, then the dower of the like by agreement because there is no named dower then, so the marriage remains without a consideration, and therein is the dower of the like. If they agree on silence or disagree, then the dower of the like according to them and according to him in the narration of Imam Muhammad, and the named dower in the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf, and the two views have preceded. Or it involves financial obligation and is intended in the contract, like khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and manumission for money. According to them, jest is null and the named amount is obligatory, because it is not subject to option of stipulation according to them. According to him, it is suspended on their choice if they constructed, since the option of stipulation is valid in them according to him. If they averse, jest is void and the contract is complete. If they are silent or disagree, then the word is for the one claiming seriousness according to him, and for the one claiming construction according to them. But jest is void, the financial obligation is binding, and divorce occurs. The second: namely, reports have no validity at all, because jest is an indication of the absence of the reported matter, and evidence is only valid by its consideration, so confessions are not valid at all. The third: namely, beliefs are also not valid with jest, except that he becomes an unbeliever by jest, not due to change of beliefs, but because jest is disrespect for the religion.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the effect of jest (huzl) on various legal acts according to the Hanafi madhhab. The author explains the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding when jest invalidates a transaction or other legal action. The key principle is that the default in contracts is seriousness, so if the parties are silent, the contract stands. However, if they explicitly agree on jest (through actions like building or aversion), then the jest is given effect. The text goes through several scenarios: jest in the original contract, jest in the price amount, jest in the type of price, and acts that do not admit rescission like divorce or manumission. For acts that do not admit rescission, jest has no effect — the act is valid regardless. For marriage, if jest is in the amount, the lesser dower is given effect if the parties constructed the jest, else the named dower. There is also a discussion of the seventy-two possible scenarios of disagreement based on the combinations of aversion, construction, and silence. The passage concludes with three types of acts: those that do not involve financial obligations (like divorce), reports (like confessions), and beliefs; in all cases, jest either has no effect or invalidates, but in the case of beliefs, jest constitutes disbelief.

Key Terms
هزل (huzl): Jest or play, where the parties do not intend the legal effect of their words.
إعراض (i'rad): Aversion, indicating clear rejection of the contract or jest.
بناء (bina'): Construction, meaning acting upon the jest as if it were serious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if two parties jest about the price amount in a sale and then both construct (build) the jest, what is the ruling for Abu Hanifa?
A) The contract is void because the price is uncertain.
B) The contract is valid with the lower price.
C) The contract is valid with the original named price.
D) The contract is suspended pending further agreement.

Key Takeaway
The effect of jest in Hanafi law depends on the parties' post-jest behavior: aversion voids, construction affirms, and silence creates a dispute between Abu Hanifa (validity) and the Sahibayn (void).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
The Impossibility of Unity Between Mutabaqah and Tadammun Denotations
Analyzing the logical impossibility of unifying two types of denotation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rational argument to refute the claim that mutabaqah and tadammun can be unified, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the philosophical view.

Core Idea
Two distinct things cannot become one; unity of denotations is logically impossible.

This lesson tackles a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute: whether the two primary denotations of a word—mutabaqah (matching the whole) and tadammun (implying a part)—can be considered a single unified denotation. The Hanafi school insists they are separate, with tadammun subordinate to mutabaqah. Understanding this position is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from composite terms and avoiding philosophical confusions that blur the boundaries of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Being Refuted
State clearly that some scholars (philosophers and some theologians) claim that the two denotations (mutabaqah and tadammun) can be unified into a single denotation. Also note the analogous claim that a single mental form can be analyzed into multiple realities.
The author uses the example of God's simple knowledge encompassing all things.
2
Understand the Rational Proof
Analyze the rational argument: if two things unite, either they remain two (no unity), one perishes (not unity), or something new arises (not unity). Therefore, unity of two distinct things is impossible.
This is a qat'i (certain) rational dalil; it applies to denotations as well.
3
Apply to Divine Knowledge and Analysis
Consider the philosophical objection that God's knowledge is a simple form that yields detailed knowledge by analysis. The Hanafi response: the analysis is only in the mind, not in the form itself; God's essence is the source of disclosure, not a composite form.
The doctrine of shajj (a mentally constructed form) is discussed but rejected as far-fetched.
4
Understand the Subordination of Tadammun
Examine why tadammun is said to be 'subordinate' to mutabaqah: it is not distinguished except by a mental consideration of analysis. This subordination does not imply unity but rather a dependence in understanding.
Ibn Sina's priority of the part without condition is rational, not temporal, and does not negate simultaneous existence.
5
Recognize the Thamarah (Practical Consequence)
Determine the practical difference: if unity were accepted, predication would be valid between any two things (even disparates), and the distinction between denotations would collapse. The Hanafi position preserves necessary distinctions in linguistic analysis and legal interpretation.
This directly affects how a mufti or mujtahid analyzes words like 'theft' (sariqa) or 'house' (bayt).

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الاتحاد واستحالته فلا هذا القول في العلم وفي اتحاد الدلالتين، فإنه يلزم على الأول يصح الحمل بين كل اثنين فترتفع نسبة التباين رأساً، وفي الثاني الحمل بين الأجزاء في منه أحقية نسبة الوجود إليه (عقلاً) فإن العقل إذا حلله واعتبر الجزء لا بشرط شيء، ونظر إلى أن المركب لا يحصل إلا بانضياف زيادة إليه، وصيرورته بشرط شيء حكم بأن الأحق بالوجود هو الأول دون الثاني، وهذا حق (وهو لا ينافي التحصل لهما في الذهن (معاً كما في الخارج يتحصلان معاً، وكذا لا ينافي أن تكون الطبيعة المأخوذة لا بشرط شيء تابعة لها مأخوذة بشرط شيء في الانفهام من اللفظ، هذا (و) الدلالة (على الخارج مما وضع له اللفظ التزام) وقيل لا مطلقاً، بل (إن كان) الخارج (لازماً: ذهنياً، ويرد عليه أنواع المجازات، فإنها واقعة) قطعاً، ولا لزوم ذهني هناك ، كون الدلالة فيها على الخارج التزاماً، وقيل: هناك أيضاً لزوم ذهني، فإن للقرينة دخلاً في تلك الدلالة، بل الدلالة المجازية لازمة للفظ إذا وجدت القرينة معه فيكون المعنى المجازي لازماً ذهنياً له معه، وفيه أن شارطي اللزوم العقلي أرادوا كون الخارج لازماً ذهنياً للموضوع له، واللزوم الذي ظهر من جهة القرينة هو لزومه للفظ مع القرينة إذ لا تجعل قرينة المعنى المجازي من لوازم الحقيقي، بل من لوازم اللفظ معها، وأين هذا من ذاك؟ إلا أن يقال: إن الشفاء مفهوماً
In it contrary to the two different [things], since there is no impossibility in their coming together. If you say: What do you do with the piebald [color] placed for black and white, the two opposites? He said: (As for piebald, by variation of locus the opposition is removed). And we only prevent the existence of a term for two opposites as they are opposites, i.e., considering their combination in one locus. So it became clear to you from this station—i.e., the station of claiming unity between the two denotations—that the single picture/form may be analyzed, i.e., the single picture/form into different realities. An example of allowing analysis into realities is (God's knowledge as a simple, comprehensive knowledge). For it is knowledge of all possible things while being simple; thus analysis is permissible in it (as established in its place. So understand, there is no priority or posteriority. He said in the gloss: Know that the discourse of most investigators concerning God's knowledge and concerning the unity of mutabaqah and tadammun is based on allowing the analysis of a single picture/form into differing realities; and intuition rejects it, how can two distinct things unite? That is rationally impossible. The detailed discussion is that the followers of the philosophers said: God knows all things at once without renewal and succession eternally. The source of this disclosure is His essence, just as the source in us is the imprinted form. So His essence is sufficient for the detailed disclosure of things in His knowledge; thus it is like the scientific form of all things. An objection arose against these: Things are certainly distinguished in knowledge; distinction is a branch of existence; if there is no existence, there is no knowledge. So it follows that things are unknown in pre-eternity. And the statement that time with its contents is ancient and present before Him, as some of them held, does not help, because His knowledge is active and prior to the known things; thus distinction must be for them before their existence, even if by nature. So some virtuous one answered, and their investigator al-Dawwani accepted it, that those known things exist in knowledge in a single comprehensive form, and they are created by obligation, because they are attributes. This is what the author alluded to in the book and in the gloss. Undoubtedly, this view is based on analysis and unity. So it is not objected that comprehensive knowledge is far from the analysis of the simple into many forms, because the meaning of comprehensiveness is not that the known is the single form that breaks down into many, and that the relation of that form to the many is the relation of the limited to the limit. How could this be their intended meaning? It would entail that the Eternal is not knowing in detail and distinction, contrary to their doctrine, indeed contrary to every rational person. Rather, they meant by comprehensive knowledge that His essence is the principle for the disclosure of things in detail, each distinguished from the other. So knowledge—i.e., the principle of disclosure—is single and simple, while the known things are many; there is no analysis at all. This objection would apply only if the author intended the necessity of analyzing the single thing which is knowledge into many; but he intended the analysis of the single form of the known things present in His knowledge, as they held, to avoid the entailment of distinguishing absolute non-existents. So understand. As for the unity of the two denotations, the construction is apparent; there is no doubt about the impossibility of this unity. For the unity of two things absolutely is impossible, because after unity they are as they were before; the state of unity and non-unity are the same. If something occurs—a quality or essence—then it is not unity; if something perishes—essence or quality—then it is non-existence and impossibility, not unity. Also, if after unity they remain two, then there is no unity; otherwise they become non-existent, or one of them ceases, so there is no unity either. And the unity of genus and species in the composition of the species is not an objection, for that is a philosophical statement we do not accept. And disregarding its validity, [the text continues:] the third treatise on linguistic principles: unity and its impossibility. So neither this statement about knowledge nor about the unity of the two denotations is valid. For the first would entail that predication is valid between every two things, thus the relation of disparity would be entirely removed; and in the second, predication between parts in every quiddity, even quantitative, because predication is the unity of two different things in a respect in some mode of existence, and there is no doubt about its impossibility. And from this it became clear to you the refutation of what might appear as an objection that this problem has no escape. For it follows in the unitary composition of genus and species, and the claim of its impossibility in something like 'house' as opposed to the mental composite is arbitrary. This is not far-fetched if built on the doctrine of shajj without the things obtaining by themselves. For the whole, comprehensively, is only conceived by a shajj-form with no composition at all; but that form is disposed to produce two shajj-forms for the two parts when one attends to them. In that case, there is no analysis at all. The truth is that this too is far-fetched, for we do not arbitrarily reject the doctrine of shajj; rather it is established with us through rational consideration. We have explained it in our glosses on the Zāhidiyyah glosses related to Sharḥ al-Mawāqif. Also, we concede that the form of the whole is only conceived comprehensively by one shajj, and it is disposed to produce two forms for conceiving the parts; but from this the unity of the two denotations does not follow. For the understanding obtained by the first shajj of the whole is other than the two understandings obtained by the two later shajjs for the two parts. So there is no unity in the two denotations. This is what this servant has obtained until now; perhaps God will bring something after that. If it is said: How did they claim unity of the two denotations while they said that tadammun is subordinate to muṭābaqah, and subordination requires duality which is incompatible with unity? He said: And what is said that it (i.e., tadammun) is subordinate to it (muṭābaqah) is a generous concession from them, because since it is not distinguished from it except by consideration of analysis, and this consideration is subordinate to the consideration of the whole itself, it was said: tadammun is subordinate to muṭābaqah. If it is said: Ibn Sīnā established in al-Shifā' that the part taken without condition is prior to the composite, and the later scholars among us accepted it. So how can the two denotations be unified? Rather, how can the judgment of subordination of tadammun to muṭābaqah be valid after this commitment? He answered with his statement: (And what is in the fact that the nature without condition —i.e., the part—is prior to the nature with condition —i.e., the whole—by the priority of the simple over the composite, the intended meaning is the greater right of existence attribution to it rationally). For the intellect, when it analyzes and considers the part without condition, and sees that the composite only obtains by an additional annexation and its becoming with condition, judges that the first has greater right to existence than the second. And this is true. (And it does not contradict their obtaining together in the mind, just as in the external they obtain together. Likewise it does not contradict that the nature taken without condition is subordinate to the nature taken with condition in understanding from the utterance. This, and the denotation of the external for which the word is placed is iltizām (committed denotation). It is said not absolutely, rather if the external is a mental necessary concomitant. And it is objected to by types of metaphors, for they occur certainly, and there is no mental necessary concomitant there, so the denotation of the external in them is iltizām. And it is said: There too there is a mental necessary concomitant, for the qarīna has a role in that denotation. Rather, metaphorical denotation is a necessary concomitant of the utterance when the qarīna is present with it; thus the metaphorical meaning is a mental necessary concomitant of it with it. And therein [it is said] that the conditioners of mental necessary concomitance intended that the external is a mental necessary concomitant of the term; and the necessary concomitance that appears by way of qarīna is its necessary concomitance for the utterance with the qarīna. Since the qarīna of the metaphorical meaning is not made from the necessary concomitants of the real meaning, rather from the necessary concomitants of the utterance with it. And where is this from that? Unless it is said that al-Shifā' is a concept.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a disputation on the claim of unity between two types of denotation: mutabaqah (matching denotation) and tadammun (implicative denotation). The author, a Hanafi usuli, argues that such unity is logically impossible. He begins by addressing a potential objection regarding a term that denotes two opposites (like black and white in a piebald pattern), explaining that opposition is removed due to variation in the locus. Then he transitions to the main issue: the analysis of a single mental form into multiple different realities. Some scholars, particularly philosophers and their followers, claimed that a single form can be analyzed into multiple realities, using God's knowledge as an example—that God's knowledge is simple yet encompasses all known things. The author refutes this by stating that intuition rejects analyzing a single form into differing realities. He then engages with the philosophical view that God's knowledge is a single comprehensive form from which all known things are disclosed. The author explains that the intended meaning of 'comprehensive knowledge' is not that the form itself breaks down, but that God's essence is the source for the detailed disclosure of things; thus there is no actual analysis. Then he directly addresses the unity of the two denotations (mutabaqah and tadammun), presenting a rational proof: two things cannot become one because either they remain two or one perishes. He rejects the analogy of genus and species as a philosophical notion not accepted by Hanafis. The discussion continues with the doctrine of 'shajj' (a kind of mental construction) and the priority of the part without condition (la bi-shart) over the composite. The author maintains that even with shajj, there is no unity of denotations because the understanding of the whole is distinct from the understanding of the parts. He then addresses the observation that tadammun is said to be subordinate to mutabaqah, which implies duality. The author explains that this subordination is only through analysis, not actual separation. He engages with Ibn Sina's dictum about the priority of the part without condition and clarifies that this priority is rational, not temporal, and does not contradict their simultaneous mental existence. Finally, he discusses denotation of the external (iltizam) and addresses metaphors, arguing that metaphorical meanings are not necessitated by the term itself but by the qarina (contextual indicator), so they do not constitute genuine iltizam denotation of the external.

Key Terms
المطابقة (al-muṭābaqah): The denotation of a word that corresponds to its entire meaning as a whole.
التضمن (al-taḍammun): The denotation that the word gives of a part of its meaning by implication.
الاتحاد (al-ittiḥād): The alleged unity of two distinct denotations (mutabaqah and tadammun) into a single denotation.
التحليل (al-taḥlīl): The analytical decomposition of a single mental form into multiple constituent realities.
الشج (al-shajj): A mental construction where a composite concept is conceived as a simple form that can generate separate forms for its parts upon reflection.
لا بشرط شيء (lā bi-sharṭ shay'): A part considered unconditionally, i.e., without the condition of being part of a whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul position presented in this passage, why is the unity of mutabaqah and tadammun denotations impossible?
A) Because the word cannot denote both the whole and the part simultaneously.
B) Because two distinct things cannot become one without either remaining two or one ceasing to exist.
C) Because the part has priority over the whole in existence.
D) Because tadammun is not a real denotation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that mutabaqah and tadammun cannot be unified is based on a rational argument that two distinct things cannot become one without contradiction, and this has direct implications for how words are analyzed in legal derivation.

Qarina and the Understanding of Metaphorical Meaning
Critique of the author's definition focusing on hidden qarina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of qarina in understanding metaphorical meaning and explain why a hidden qarina disrupts mental concomitance.

Core Idea
A qarina diverts intention, not understanding; without a known qarina, no mental concomitance for majaz.

The commentator begins with a fundamental rule: the original meaning is understood from the word even with a qarina present, because the qarina only diverts intention. But then he raises the author's objection: if the qarina is hidden, the metaphorical meaning is not understood, so mental concomitance fails. This lesson unpacks that objection and its implications for how we understand the relationship between the word and its metaphorical sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline claim
The commentator states that the original meaning is understood from the word even with a qarina, and the qarina only diverts intention, not understanding. Recognize this as a key principle.
2
Understand the objection about hidden qarina
The author objects: if the qarina is hidden (khafiyya), it cannot guide understanding, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood. Therefore mental concomitance (luzum dhihni) is absent. Grasp why this is a problem for the definition of iltizam.
3
Analyze the logical implication
If mental concomitance is absent when qarina is hidden, then iltizam (necessary signification) fails for hidden qiara. Consider how this affects the classification of majaz under iltizam.
This sets up the later discussion of whether majaz is really iltizam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ المعنى الموضوع له يفهم من اللفظ ، ولو كان مع قرينة وإنما هي صارفة عن إرادته، والإرادة غير الفهم والمعنى المجازي لازم للفظ حين القرينة، فالمعنى الموضوع له والمجازي متلازمان عند وجود القرينة في اللفظ للمعنى المجازي (لا يخرج اللفظ (عن كونه لفظاً على ما قيل فإن اللفظ لفظ دال، لكن لزوم المعنى المجازي ليس له فقط بل له مع القرينة (ألا ترى من الجائز أن يكون المركب من الجوهر والعرض عند من جوز التركيب منهما (جوهراً فتفكر وهذا التأييد ليس في محله، فإن المركب المذكور ليس له محل، فيكون جوهراً عليه، وأما المركب من اللفظ والقرينة فلا يتلفظ به الإنسان فلا يكون لفظاً، بل الأولى الاكتفاء على ما قيل فإنه من البين أن اعتبار شيء في شيء لأجل الاتصاف بصفة لا يلزم منه دخول ذلك الشيء في حقيقته، فاعتبار القرينة في كونه ملزوماً للمعنى المجازي لا يلزم منه دخولها في جوهره وحقيقته كذا ،قالوا ثم اعلم أن من اعتبر اللزوم الذهني في الالتزام لا مخلص له عن هذا الإشكال إلا إما بإخراج المجاز عن الالتزام وإدخاله في المطابقة بإرادة الوضع الأعم من النوعي والشخصي وإما بإخراجه عن الدلالة الوضعية باشتراط كلية الفهم للدلالة حين العلم بالوضع كما قال شارح المختصر، التحقيق فيه أنه فرع تفسير الدلالة، وأنه هل يشترط فيها أنه أنه مهما اللفظ أم لا، لكن الأول ،أولى فإن فيه كما قال المصنف أن سمع المجازات واقعة فهذا الاصطلاح المخرج لدلالتها خطأ، وإما بالإدارة من اللزوم الذهني كون الخارج له نوع علاقة مع الموضوع له بحيث يمكن الانتقال منه إليه وإن أمكن الانفكاك بينهما في التعقل، ثم المطلع الأسرار الإلهية رحمه الله كلام على المصنف نفصله بتمهيد مقدمة أن أهل الميزان قسموا الدلالة الوضعية إلى ما كان على تمام الموضوع له وهو المطابقة، وإلى ما كان على الجزء المنفهم في ضمن انفهام الكل وهو التضمن وإلى ما كان على خارج لازم ذهني له التزام والقصد إنما هو في المطابقة فقط عندهم، والأخيران دلالتان تابعتان لها، والدلالة المجازية إما أن تخرج من الدلالة باعتبار الانفهام الكلي كما نص عليه السيد قدس سره الشريف، وإما أن تدرج في المطابقة كما قيل وأما عند أهل العربية فالقصد معتبر في الدلالات كلها فالالتزام ما قصد من اللفظ خارج عن معناه وكذا التضمن ما قصد جزء معناه، فالدلالات المجازية داخلة في التضمن والالتزام وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول : إن في كلام المصنف اضطراباً، فإنه إن بني كلامه على اصطلاح أهل العربية كما هو الأليق فالتضمن عندهم دلالة اللفظ على الجزء المقصود من اللفظ ، بأن يستعمل اللفظ فيه مجازاً، فحينئذ لا يصح الاتحاد بين الدلالتين، كما لا يخفى بل لا يصح اجتماع الدلالة المطابقية معها حينئذ، دعوی كما لا يخفى، وإن بني كلامه على اصطلاح أهل الميزان فالتضمن الدلالة على الجزء المنفهم في الكل، فيصح دعوى الاتحاد بناء على رأيهم من أن ها هنا فهماً واحداً للكل بصورة وحدانية فهي من حيث الوحدة دلالة مطابقة، ومن حيث التحليل إلى الأجزاء تضمن لكن حينئذ الالتزام الدلالة على الخارج التابعة للمطابقة، وحينئذ لا بد من اللزوم بين هذا الخارج والموضوع له، وإلا لما الدلالة، وحينئذ لا وجه لإيراد أنواع المجازات نقضاً على الشارط، فالمصنف أخذ في التضمن اصطلاح أهل المنطق وفي الالتزام اصطلاح أهل العربية، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام
The word: the meaning for which it is coined is understood from the word, even if with a qarina, and the qarina only diverts from intending it, and intention is not understanding. The metaphorical meaning is necessary to the word at the time of the qarina, so the original and metaphorical meanings are mutually concomitant when the qarina exists in understanding from the word, so understand. Then the author objected to him by saying: The qarina may be hidden, so it is not known, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, so there is no mental concomitance. And objection was also made to this speaker that if the qarina is considered, the word exits from being a word; rather it is composed of the word and the qarina, for it may be rational. If what is meant is concomitance in the state of qarina's accompaniment without taking it as a condition, as in the conditional as long as the description exists, then it is apparent that it is not necessary in the time of the qarina, for the qarina is not necessary there, so what about the conditional with it? And if what is meant is with the condition of the qarina, as in the conditional with the condition of the description, then concomitance belongs to the word by virtue of association with it, so the restriction enters, and it is not a word. And the author indicated his refutation by saying: 'And considering the qarina in the word's being a necessary correlative of the metaphorical meaning does not take the word out of being a word, as has been said, for the word is an indicative word, but the concomitance of the metaphorical meaning is not solely for it but for it together with the qarina. Do you not see that it is possible for the composite of substance and accident, according to one who allows composition from them, to be a substance? So reflect.' And this support is not in its place, for the mentioned composite has no locus, so it would be a substance upon it. As for the composite of the word and the qarina, man does not utter it, so it is not a word. Rather, the best is to suffice with what has been said, for it is clear that considering something in something else for the sake of being qualified by a property does not necessitate the entry of that thing into its essence. So considering the qarina in its being a necessary correlative for the metaphorical meaning does not necessitate its entry into its substance and essence, thus they said. Then know that whoever considers mental concomitance in iltizam has no escape from this problem except either by removing majaz from iltizam and entering it into mutabaqa by intending a more general wad' (generic or specific), or by removing it from conventional signification by requiring complete understanding for signification at the time of knowing the wad', as the commentator of al-Mukhtasar said. The investigation is that it is subsidiary to the interpretation of signification: whether it is required that the word [be understood] or not. But the first is more appropriate, for in it, as the author said, that majazat are found, so this convention that excludes their signification is wrong. Or by turning away from mental concomitance such that the extraneous thing has some connection to the original meaning so that transition from it to it is possible, even if disjunction between them is possible in intellection. Then al-Muttali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may Allah have mercy on him) has a speech on the author which we detail by presenting a prolegomenon: the people of logic have divided conventional signification into that which is upon the entirety of the object (mutabaqa), that which is upon the part understood indirectly (tadammun), and that which is upon an extraneous mental necessary (iltizam), and the intention is only in mutabaqa according to them; the last two are subsidiary significations. Metaphorical signification either exits from signification by considering complete understanding as al-Sayyid texted, or it is included in mutabaqa as has been said. And according to the people of Arabic, intention is considered in all significations, so iltizam is what is intended from the word outside its meaning, and likewise tadammun is what is intended as part of its meaning. So metaphorical significations are included in tadammun and iltizam. And when this is settled, we say: There is confusion in the author's speech. For if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of Arabic, as is more suitable, then tadammun according to them is the word's signification of the intended part of the word, by using the word metaphorically for it. Then it is not correct to unite the two significations, as is not hidden; rather, it is not correct for mutabaqa signification to combine with it then, as claimed. And if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of logic, then tadammun is signification of the part understood in the whole, so the claim of unity is correct based on their view that there is one understanding of the whole in a unitary image, so from the aspect of unity it is mutabaqa, and from the aspect of analysis into parts it is tadammun. But then iltizam is signification of the extraneous thing subsidiary to mutabaqa, and then concomitance must exist between this extraneous thing and the original meaning, otherwise there would be no signification. And then there is no point in bringing types of majaz as a refutation of the condition. So the author took in tadammun the convention of the logicians, and in iltizam the convention of the people of Arabic. This is how this station should be understood.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of iltizam (necessary signification) and the role of qarina (contextual indicator) in understanding metaphorical meaning. The commentator begins by stating the basic principle: the original meaning is understood from the word even when a qarina is present; the qarina only diverts from intending the original meaning. Intention is distinct from understanding. The metaphorical meaning is considered necessary (lazim) to the word only when the qarina is present, so both meanings are mutually concomitant in the mind when the qarina exists. The author (of Musallam al-Thubut) objects: the qarina may be hidden, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, hence no mental concomitance. Another objection is raised: if we consider the qarina part of the signification, the word ceases to be a word because it becomes a composite of word and qarina. The author responds by arguing that considering the qarina does not remove the word's status as word, using an analogy from substance and accident. However, the commentator finds this analogy flawed because a composite of word and qarina is not uttered. The commentator then discusses possible solutions to the problem of mental concomitance, including reclassifying majaz as mutabaqa or excluding it from conventional signification. He then presents the division of signification according to logicians (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and compares it to the Arabic scholars' perspective, where intention is key. He identifies a confusion in the author's use of tadammun in the logical sense and iltizam in the Arabic sense, and concludes that this explains the inconsistency.

Key Terms
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator that diverts from the original meaning and allows understanding of the metaphorical meaning.
اللزوم الذهني (al-luzum al-dhihni): Mental concomitance between two meanings, such that one is necessarily understood from the other in the mind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's objection, what is the consequence when the qarina is hidden?
A) The metaphorical meaning is understood but not intended.
B) The metaphorical meaning is not understood, so mental concomitance fails.
C) The word loses its status as a word.
D) The original meaning is forgotten.

Key Takeaway
A hidden qarina prevents understanding of the metaphorical meaning, breaking mental concomitance and challenging the definition of iltizam.

The Composite Objection and Its Refutation
Refutation of the composite objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection that considering qarina makes the word composite, and evaluate the author's and commentator's responses.

Core Idea
Considering qarina does not turn the word into a composite; it is a condition for signification, not part of the word's essence.

A second objection arises: if we say the metaphorical meaning is 'necessary' due to qarina, doesn't that make the word dependent on the qarina, turning it into a composite entity (word + qarina)? The author and commentator each offer responses. This lesson analyzes the refutation and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the composite objection
The objection states that if qarina is considered in the word's signification, then the word would be a composite of word and qarina, ceasing to be a 'word'. Understand why this is a formal-logical concern.
2
Examine the author's refutation
The author responds by saying that considering qarina in the word's being a necessary correlative does not remove its being a word, using an analogy of substance and accident. Understand the analogy.
3
Identify the commentator's critique
The commentator finds the analogy flawed: a composite of substance and accident is still a substance, but a composite of word and qarina is not uttered, so it is not a word. Appreciate the nuance.
4
Learn the preferred resolution
The commentator says the best answer is that considering something as a condition for a property does not make it part of the essence. Qarina is a condition for the word's having metaphorical signification, not part of the word's essence.
