اللفظ المعنى الموضوع له يفهم من اللفظ ، ولو كان مع قرينة وإنما هي صارفة عن إرادته، والإرادة غير الفهم والمعنى المجازي لازم للفظ حين القرينة، فالمعنى الموضوع له والمجازي متلازمان عند وجود القرينة في الانفهام من اللفظ فافهم، ثم أورد عليه المصنف بقوله : والقرينة قد تكون خفية فلا تعلم فلا يفهم المعنى المجازي، فلا لزوم ذهني، واعترض على هذا القائل أيضاً بأنه لو اعتبر القرينة خرج اللفظ عن كونه لفظاً، بل هو مر بل هو مركب من والقرينة، فإنها قد تكون عقلية، وإن أريد اللزوم في حال مقارنة القرينة من غير أن تؤخذ شرطاً كما في المشروطة ما دام الوصف فظاهر أنه ليس لازماً في زمان القرينة فإن القرينة غير لازمة فيها فما بال المشروطة بها، وإن أريد بشرط القرينة كما في المشروطة بشرط الوصف، فاللزوم للفظ من حيث الاقتران معها فالتقييد داخل وهو ليس بلفظ، وأشار المصنف إلى رده بقوله : ( واعتبار القرينة في ملزومية اللفظ للمعنى المجازي (لا يخرج اللفظ (عن كونه لفظاً على ما قيل فإن اللفظ لفظ دال، لكن لزوم المعنى المجازي ليس له فقط بل له مع القرينة (ألا ترى من الجائز أن يكون المركب من الجوهر والعرض عند من جوز التركيب منهما (جوهراً فتفكر وهذا التأييد ليس في محله، فإن المركب المذكور ليس له محل، فيكون جوهراً عليه، وأما المركب من اللفظ والقرينة فلا يتلفظ به الإنسان فلا يكون لفظاً، بل الأولى الاكتفاء على ما قيل فإنه من البين أن اعتبار شيء في شيء لأجل الاتصاف بصفة لا يلزم منه دخول ذلك الشيء في حقيقته، فاعتبار القرينة في كونه ملزوماً للمعنى المجازي لا يلزم منه دخولها في جوهره وحقيقته كذا ،قالوا ثم اعلم أن من اعتبر اللزوم الذهني في الالتزام لا مخلص له عن هذا الإشكال إلا إما بإخراج المجاز عن الالتزام وإدخاله في المطابقة بإرادة الوضع الأعم من النوعي والشخصي وإما بإخراجه عن الدلالة الوضعية باشتراط كلية الفهم للدلالة حين العلم بالوضع كما قال شارح المختصر، التحقيق فيه أنه فرع تفسير الدلالة، وأنه هل يشترط فيها أنه أنه مهما اللفظ أم لا، لكن الأول ،أولى فإن فيه كما قال المصنف أن سمع المجازات واقعة فهذا الاصطلاح المخرج لدلالتها خطأ، وإما بالإدارة من اللزوم الذهني كون الخارج له نوع علاقة مع الموضوع له بحيث يمكن الانتقال منه إليه وإن أمكن الانفكاك بينهما في التعقل، ثم المطلع الأسرار الإلهية رحمه الله كلام على المصنف نفصله بتمهيد مقدمة أن أهل الميزان قسموا الدلالة الوضعية إلى ما كان على تمام الموضوع له وهو المطابقة، وإلى ما كان على الجزء المنفهم في ضمن انفهام الكل وهو التضمن وإلى ما كان على خارج لازم ذهني له التزام والقصد إنما هو في المطابقة فقط عندهم، والأخيران دلالتان تابعتان لها، والدلالة المجازية إما أن تخرج من الدلالة باعتبار الانفهام الكلي كما نص عليه السيد قدس سره الشريف، وإما أن تدرج في المطابقة كما قيل وأما عند أهل العربية فالقصد معتبر في الدلالات كلها فالالتزام ما قصد من اللفظ خارج عن معناه وكذا التضمن ما قصد جزء معناه، فالدلالات المجازية داخلة في التضمن والالتزام وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول : إن في كلام المصنف اضطراباً، فإنه إن بني كلامه على اصطلاح أهل العربية كما هو الأليق فالتضمن عندهم دلالة اللفظ على الجزء المقصود من اللفظ ، بأن يستعمل اللفظ فيه مجازاً، فحينئذ لا يصح الاتحاد بين الدلالتين، كما لا يخفى بل لا يصح اجتماع الدلالة المطابقية معها حينئذ، دعوی كما لا يخفى، وإن بني كلامه على اصطلاح أهل الميزان فالتضمن الدلالة على الجزء المنفهم في الكل، فيصح دعوى الاتحاد بناء على رأيهم من أن ها هنا فهماً واحداً للكل بصورة وحدانية فهي من حيث الوحدة دلالة مطابقة، ومن حيث التحليل إلى الأجزاء تضمن لكن حينئذ الالتزام الدلالة على الخارج التابعة للمطابقة، وحينئذ لا بد من اللزوم بين هذا الخارج والموضوع له، وإلا لما الدلالة، وحينئذ لا وجه لإيراد أنواع المجازات نقضاً على الشارط، فالمصنف أخذ في التضمن اصطلاح أهل المنطق وفي الالتزام اصطلاح أهل العربية، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام
The word: the meaning for which it is coined is understood from the word, even if with a qarina, and the qarina only diverts from intending it, and intention is not understanding. The metaphorical meaning is necessary to the word at the time of the qarina, so the original and metaphorical meanings are mutually concomitant when the qarina exists in understanding from the word, so understand. Then the author objected to him by saying: The qarina may be hidden, so it is not known, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, so there is no mental concomitance. And objection was also made to this speaker that if the qarina is considered, the word exits from being a word; rather it is composed of the word and the qarina, for it may be rational. If what is meant is concomitance in the state of qarina's accompaniment without taking it as a condition, as in the conditional as long as the description exists, then it is apparent that it is not necessary in the time of the qarina, for the qarina is not necessary there, so what about the conditional with it? And if what is meant is with the condition of the qarina, as in the conditional with the condition of the description, then concomitance belongs to the word by virtue of association with it, so the restriction enters, and it is not a word. And the author indicated his refutation by saying: 'And considering the qarina in the word's being a necessary correlative of the metaphorical meaning does not take the word out of being a word, as has been said, for the word is an indicative word, but the concomitance of the metaphorical meaning is not solely for it but for it together with the qarina. Do you not see that it is possible for the composite of substance and accident, according to one who allows composition from them, to be a substance? So reflect.' And this support is not in its place, for the mentioned composite has no locus, so it would be a substance upon it. As for the composite of the word and the qarina, man does not utter it, so it is not a word. Rather, the best is to suffice with what has been said, for it is clear that considering something in something else for the sake of being qualified by a property does not necessitate the entry of that thing into its essence. So considering the qarina in its being a necessary correlative for the metaphorical meaning does not necessitate its entry into its substance and essence, thus they said. Then know that whoever considers mental concomitance in iltizam has no escape from this problem except either by removing majaz from iltizam and entering it into mutabaqa by intending a more general wad' (generic or specific), or by removing it from conventional signification by requiring complete understanding for signification at the time of knowing the wad', as the commentator of al-Mukhtasar said. The investigation is that it is subsidiary to the interpretation of signification: whether it is required that the word [be understood] or not. But the first is more appropriate, for in it, as the author said, that majazat are found, so this convention that excludes their signification is wrong. Or by turning away from mental concomitance such that the extraneous thing has some connection to the original meaning so that transition from it to it is possible, even if disjunction between them is possible in intellection. Then al-Muttali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may Allah have mercy on him) has a speech on the author which we detail by presenting a prolegomenon: the people of logic have divided conventional signification into that which is upon the entirety of the object (mutabaqa), that which is upon the part understood indirectly (tadammun), and that which is upon an extraneous mental necessary (iltizam), and the intention is only in mutabaqa according to them; the last two are subsidiary significations. Metaphorical signification either exits from signification by considering complete understanding as al-Sayyid texted, or it is included in mutabaqa as has been said. And according to the people of Arabic, intention is considered in all significations, so iltizam is what is intended from the word outside its meaning, and likewise tadammun is what is intended as part of its meaning. So metaphorical significations are included in tadammun and iltizam. And when this is settled, we say: There is confusion in the author's speech. For if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of Arabic, as is more suitable, then tadammun according to them is the word's signification of the intended part of the word, by using the word metaphorically for it. Then it is not correct to unite the two significations, as is not hidden; rather, it is not correct for mutabaqa signification to combine with it then, as claimed. And if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of logic, then tadammun is signification of the part understood in the whole, so the claim of unity is correct based on their view that there is one understanding of the whole in a unitary image, so from the aspect of unity it is mutabaqa, and from the aspect of analysis into parts it is tadammun. But then iltizam is signification of the extraneous thing subsidiary to mutabaqa, and then concomitance must exist between this extraneous thing and the original meaning, otherwise there would be no signification. And then there is no point in bringing types of majaz as a refutation of the condition. So the author took in tadammun the convention of the logicians, and in iltizam the convention of the people of Arabic. This is how this station should be understood.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of iltizam (necessary signification) and the role of qarina (contextual indicator) in understanding metaphorical meaning. The commentator begins by stating the basic principle: the original meaning is understood from the word even when a qarina is present; the qarina only diverts from intending the original meaning. Intention is distinct from understanding. The metaphorical meaning is considered necessary (lazim) to the word only when the qarina is present, so both meanings are mutually concomitant in the mind when the qarina exists. The author (of Musallam al-Thubut) objects: the qarina may be hidden, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, hence no mental concomitance. Another objection is raised: if we consider the qarina part of the signification, the word ceases to be a word because it becomes a composite of word and qarina. The author responds by arguing that considering the qarina does not remove the word's status as word, using an analogy from substance and accident. However, the commentator finds this analogy flawed because a composite of word and qarina is not uttered. The commentator then discusses possible solutions to the problem of mental concomitance, including reclassifying majaz as mutabaqa or excluding it from conventional signification. He then presents the division of signification according to logicians (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and compares it to the Arabic scholars' perspective, where intention is key. He identifies a confusion in the author's use of tadammun in the logical sense and iltizam in the Arabic sense, and concludes that this explains the inconsistency.

Key Terms
المركب (al-murakkab): A composite; something composed of two or more elements. In the objection, the word would be a composite of word and qarina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the author's analogy of substance and accident inadequate?
A) Because a composite of substance and accident cannot be a substance.
B) Because the composite of word and qarina is not uttered, so it is not a word.
C) Because accidents are not real properties.
D) Because the author misquoted the analogy.

Key Takeaway
A condition for a property does not become part of the essence; qarina conditions metaphorical signification without entering the word's essence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Rethinking Iltizam: Solutions and the Logician vs. Arabic Scholar Divide
Critical analysis of the author's inconsistent use of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three solutions to the problem of mental concomitance in iltizam and distinguish between the logicians' and Arabic scholars' definitions of tadammun and iltizam.

Core Idea
The author conflates two traditions; to resolve the iltizam problem, one must decide on a definition for tadammun and iltizam.

Having accepted the problem that a hidden qarina breaks mental concomitance, the commentator presents possible solutions. These include reclassifying majaz as mutabaqa, excluding it from conventional signification, or redefining iltizam to require only a possible mental connection. He then reveals the deeper issue: the author mixed the logical and Arabic conventions, causing confusion. This lesson clarifies both traditions and shows how consistency resolves the earlier objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three solutions
The commentator lists three ways to avoid the problem: (1) remove majaz from iltizam and treat it as mutabaqa via a broader wad'; (2) exclude majaz from wad'iyyah signification entirely, requiring complete understanding only at the time of knowing wad'; (3) redefine iltizam to require only a possible mental connection (not actual concomitance). Understand each.
2
Learn the logicians' division
Logicians divide signification into mutabaqa (whole meaning), tadammun (part implied in whole), and iltizam (extraneous mental necessary). They consider only mutabaqa as intended; the other two are subsidiary. Grasp this hierarchy.
3
Learn the Arabic scholars' division
For Arabic scholars, intention (qasd) is required in all significations. Tadammun is signification of an intended part via metaphorical use; iltizam is signification of an intended meaning external to the original. Metaphorical signification falls under tadammun or iltizam.
4
Detect the author's confusion
The commentator points out that the author used the logicians' notion of tadammun (part of whole) but the Arabic scholars' notion of iltizam (intended external meaning). This inconsistency invalidates the objections he raised.

The word: the meaning for which it is coined is understood from the word, even if with a qarina, and the qarina only diverts from intending it, and intention is not understanding. The metaphorical meaning is necessary to the word at the time of the qarina, so the original and metaphorical meanings are mutually concomitant when the qarina exists in understanding from the word, so understand. Then the author objected to him by saying: The qarina may be hidden, so it is not known, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, so there is no mental concomitance. And objection was also made to this speaker that if the qarina is considered, the word exits from being a word; rather it is composed of the word and the qarina, for it may be rational. If what is meant is concomitance in the state of qarina's accompaniment without taking it as a condition, as in the conditional as long as the description exists, then it is apparent that it is not necessary in the time of the qarina, for the qarina is not necessary there, so what about the conditional with it? And if what is meant is with the condition of the qarina, as in the conditional with the condition of the description, then concomitance belongs to the word by virtue of association with it, so the restriction enters, and it is not a word. And the author indicated his refutation by saying: 'And considering the qarina in the word's being a necessary correlative of the metaphorical meaning does not take the word out of being a word, as has been said, for the word is an indicative word, but the concomitance of the metaphorical meaning is not solely for it but for it together with the qarina. Do you not see that it is possible for the composite of substance and accident, according to one who allows composition from them, to be a substance? So reflect.' And this support is not in its place, for the mentioned composite has no locus, so it would be a substance upon it. As for the composite of the word and the qarina, man does not utter it, so it is not a word. So considering the qarina in its being a necessary correlative for the metaphorical meaning does not necessitate its entry into its substance and essence, thus they said. The investigation is that it is subsidiary to the interpretation of signification: whether it is required that the word [be understood] or not. But the first is more appropriate, for in it, as the author said, that majazat are found, so this convention that excludes their signification is wrong. Or by turning away from mental concomitance such that the extraneous thing has some connection to the original meaning so that transition from it to it is possible, even if disjunction between them is possible in intellection. Then al-Muttali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may Allah have mercy on him) has a speech on the author which we detail by presenting a prolegomenon: the people of logic have divided conventional signification into that which is upon the entirety of the object (mutabaqa), that which is upon the part understood indirectly (tadammun), and that which is upon an extraneous mental necessary (iltizam), and the intention is only in mutabaqa according to them; the last two are subsidiary significations. Metaphorical signification either exits from signification by considering complete understanding as al-Sayyid texted, or it is included in mutabaqa as has been said. And according to the people of Arabic, intention is considered in all significations, so iltizam is what is intended from the word outside its meaning, and likewise tadammun is what is intended as part of its meaning. So metaphorical significations are included in tadammun and iltizam. And when this is settled, we say: There is confusion in the author's speech. For if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of Arabic, as is more suitable, then tadammun according to them is the word's signification of the intended part of the word, by using the word metaphorically for it. Then it is not correct to unite the two significations, as is not hidden; rather, it is not correct for mutabaqa signification to combine with it then, as claimed. And if he bases his speech on the convention of the people of logic, then tadammun is signification of the part understood in the whole, so the claim of unity is correct based on their view that there is one understanding of the whole in a unitary image, so from the aspect of unity it is mutabaqa, and from the aspect of analysis into parts it is tadammun. But then iltizam is signification of the extraneous thing subsidiary to mutabaqa, and then concomitance must exist between this extraneous thing and the original meaning, otherwise there would be no signification. And then there is no point in bringing types of majaz as a refutation of the condition. So the author took in tadammun the convention of the logicians, and in iltizam the convention of the people of Arabic. This is how this station should be understood.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of iltizam (necessary signification) and the role of qarina (contextual indicator) in understanding metaphorical meaning. The commentator begins by stating the basic principle: the original meaning is understood from the word even when a qarina is present; the qarina only diverts from intending the original meaning. Intention is distinct from understanding. The metaphorical meaning is considered necessary (lazim) to the word only when the qarina is present, so both meanings are mutually concomitant in the mind when the qarina exists. The author (of Musallam al-Thubut) objects: the qarina may be hidden, so the metaphorical meaning is not understood, hence no mental concomitance. Another objection is raised: if we consider the qarina part of the signification, the word ceases to be a word because it becomes a composite of word and qarina. The author responds by arguing that considering the qarina does not remove the word's status as word, using an analogy from substance and accident. However, the commentator finds this analogy flawed because a composite of word and qarina is not uttered. The commentator then discusses possible solutions to the problem of mental concomitance, including reclassifying majaz as mutabaqa or excluding it from conventional signification. He then presents the division of signification according to logicians (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and compares it to the Arabic scholars' perspective, where intention is key. He identifies a confusion in the author's use of tadammun in the logical sense and iltizam in the Arabic sense, and concludes that this explains the inconsistency.

Key Terms
المطابقة (al-mutabaqa): Signification of the entire meaning for which the word was coined (the primary signification).
التضمن (al-tadammun): Signification of a part understood indirectly from the whole (logicians) or signification of an intended part via metaphorical use (Arabic scholars).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as the source of confusion in the author's speech?
A) The author used a hidden qarina as an example.
B) The author mixed the logicians' definition of tadammun with the Arabic definition of iltizam.
C) The author rejected the concept of mental concomitance entirely.
D) The author relied on unreliable narrators.

Key Takeaway
Consistency in defining iltizam and tadammun is crucial; the author's mixing of traditions invalidates his critique.

Types of Denotation and Subject of Linguistic Assignation
Explaining three types of denotation and object of wad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of verbal denotation (mutabaqah, tadammun, iltizam) and explain why Hanafi usul holds that the object of wad' is meanings in themselves.

Core Idea
Words denote meanings wholly, partially, or by necessary connection; wad' assigns words to meanings in themselves, not to mental images or external entities.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut lays the linguistic groundwork for Hanafi usul al-fiqh. You will learn the three categories of verbal denotation that underlie how legal texts convey rulings, and you will examine the precise object of lexical assignation (wad'). Mastering these concepts is essential for correctly interpreting divine speech and avoiding hermeneutical errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of denotation
From the passage, list the three types: mutabaqah (whole), tadammun (part), iltizam (external necessary).
The passage uses both logicians' and grammarians' terminology.
2
Define each type with its condition
For tadammun, the part must be understood from the whole. For iltizam, there must be a necessary link between the external thing and the assigned meaning.
Without these conditions, the denotation is invalid.
3
Apply to a Quranic example
Consider the word 'yad' (hand) in the Quran. Under mutabaqah, it denotes the entire hand. Under tadammun, it denotes the palm. Under iltizam, it denotes power or bounty if the connection is necessary.
The context determines which denotation is operative.
4
Analyze the object of wad'
The passage discusses three possible objects: meanings in themselves, mental images, and external entities. Note the correct Hanafi view: meanings in themselves, because the purpose is to express what is in the mind.
The phrase 'للتعبير عما في الضمير' is key.
5
Resolve the apparent dispute
Understand that the disagreement among scholars is verbal; all positions converge on meanings in themselves. The passage states that those who say 'mental images' actually mean meanings in themselves.
This verbal dispute does not affect legal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية صح دلالة اللفظ على الجزء المقصود من اللفظ ، بأن يستعمل اللفظ فيه مجازاً، فحينئذ لا يصح الاتحاد بين الدلالتين، كما لا يخفى بل لا يصح اجتماع الدلالة المطابقية معها حينئذ، دعوی كما لا يخفى، وإن بني كلامه على اصطلاح أهل الميزان فالتضمن الدلالة على الجزء المنفهم في الكل، فيصح دعوى الاتحاد بناء على رأيهم من أن ها هنا فهماً واحداً للكل بصورة وحدانية فهي من حيث الوحدة دلالة مطابقة، ومن حيث التحليل إلى الأجزاء تضمن لكن حينئذ الالتزام الدلالة على الخارج التابعة للمطابقة، وحينئذ لا بد من اللزوم بين هذا الخارج والموضوع له، وإلا لما الدلالة، وحينئذ لا وجه لإيراد أنواع المجازات نقضاً على الشارط، فالمصنف أخذ في التضمن اصطلاح أهل المنطق وفي الالتزام اصطلاح أهل العربية، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام (ثم وضع الأصول) يفهم هذا المقام (ثم وضع الأصول من اللغات للمعاني من حيث هي هي) من غير لحاظ كونه في الذهن أو الخارج (لأنه) أي الوضع (للتعبير عما في الضمير) أي عن شيء معلوم مراد إفادته وكونه في الضمير ليس في الضمير) أي ليس معلوماً مراد الإفادة، لا أن هذا الوصف ليس ثابتاً بما هو في الضمير (فليس) الوضع (للصورة الذهنية)، فإما للمعاني من حيث هي (أو) الأمر الخارجي كما قيل فقوله : هذا معطوف على المجرور في قوله للمعاني، يعني أن الصورة الذهنية بمراحل عن كونها موضوعاً لها، فهو إما الشيء من حيث هو أو الأمر الخارجي كما قيل وأشار إلى أن الحق هو الأول : فإن كونه في الخارج أيضاً قد لا يراد إفهامه بتة وقيل : النزاع مبني على أن المعلوم بالذات هو الحاصل في الضمير أو في الخارج أو مع قطع النظر عنهما والمعلوم بالذات هو الموضوع له حقيقة، وهذا كلام خال عن التحصيل، فإنه لم يذهب ذاهب إلى أن المعلوم بالذات هو الحاصل في العوارض الضميرية الذي هو الصورة الذهنية عند قائلها فتدبر (وقد جعل بعض الأعلام النزاع) الواقع بين أهل هذه المذاهب (لفظياً) وقال من قال إنها موضوعة للصور الذهنية، أراد المعاني من حيث هي، فإن الصورة بما تطلق عليها أيضاً، ومن قال إنها للأمر الخارجي أراد المعاني من حيث كونها متصفة بالوجود الخارجي
The third article on linguistic principles. Correct: the denotation of a word to the intended part of the word, by using the word in it metaphorically. Then it is not valid for the two denotations to unite, as is not hidden; rather it is not valid for the mutabaqah denotation to be combined with it then. Claim as is not hidden. If his statement is built on the terminology of the logicians, then tadammun is denotation to the part understood in the whole, so the claim of union is valid based on their view that here there is one understanding of the whole in a unitary form. So from the perspective of unity it is mutabaqah denotation, and from the perspective of analysis into parts it is tadammun. But then iltizam is denotation to an external thing dependent on mutabaqah, and then there must be a necessary connection between this external thing and the assigned meaning, otherwise there is no denotation. And then there is no ground for bringing types of metaphors as a refutation against the condition. So the author adopted in tadammun the terminology of the logicians and in iltizam the terminology of the Arabic grammarians. Thus it should be understood this station. (Then the assignment of fundamentals) It is understood this station (Then the assignment of fundamentals from languages to meanings in terms of themselves) without regard to it being in the mind or external. (Because) it, i.e., assignment, is for expressing what is in the heart, i.e., something known intended to be communicated, and its being in the heart is not in the heart, i.e., not known intended to be communicated, not that this description is not established by what is in the heart. So assignment is not for the mental image. Rather it is either for meanings in themselves, or for the external entity as was said. So his saying: this is conjoined to the prepositional phrase in his saying 'for meanings', meaning that the mental image is far from being the thing assigned to. So it is either the thing in itself or the external entity as was said. And he indicated that the truth is the first: because its being external also may not be intended to be understood at all. And it was said: the dispute is based on whether the known per se is what is in the heart or what is in the external, or with disregard to both, and the known per se is the real assigned thing. And this statement is devoid of verification, for no one has held that the known per se is what is in the accidental mental attributes which is the mental image according to its proponents, so ponder. (And some leading scholars have made the dispute) occurring among the proponents of these schools (a verbal one) and said: whoever said they are assigned to mental images intended meanings in themselves, for 'image' is applied to them as well; and whoever said they are for the external entity intended the meanings in terms of their being qualified by external existence.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage discusses the linguistic principles of denotation and the object of wad' (linguistic assignment). The author first explains the three types of verbal denotation: mutabaqah (equivalence) where the word denotes its entire meaning; tadammun (implication) where it denotes a part of the meaning; and iltizam (necessary accompaniment) where it denotes something external necessarily connected to the meaning. The author clarifies that if we adopt the logicians' terminology, tadammun is valid as a single understanding of the whole from a unitary perspective, but iltizam requires a necessary link between the external thing and the assigned meaning. He points out that the matn author used different terminologies for tadammun and iltizam. Then the passage addresses the object of wad': whether words are assigned for meanings in themselves, for mental images, or for external entities. The author concludes that the correct view is that wad' is for meanings in themselves, because the purpose is to express what is in the mind, and the mental image is not the direct object. The dispute among scholars is largely verbal, as those who say wad' is for mental images actually mean meanings in themselves, and those who say it is for external entities actually mean meanings qualified by external existence.

Key Terms
دلالة مطابقية (dalālah muṭābaqiyyah): Denotation where the word indicates the entirety of its assigned meaning in a one-to-one correspondence.
دلالة تضمن (dalālah taḍammun): Denotation where the word indicates a part of its assigned meaning, based on the implication that the part is contained in the whole.
دلالة التزام (dalālah iltizām): Denotation where the word indicates something external to its assigned meaning but necessarily connected to it.
وضع (waḍ‘): The act of assigning a word to a meaning for the purpose of expressing what is in the mind, for meanings in themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The word denotes its entire meaning
B) The word denotes something external necessarily connected to its meaning
C) The word denotes a part of its meaning understood from the whole
D) The word denotes the mental image of the meaning

Key Takeaway
The three types of denotation and the correct object of wad' are foundational for interpreting legal texts in Hanafi usul.

Appropriateness vs. Inherent Correspondence: A Usul Dispute
A usul dispute on linguistic theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi position on munasaba and the rejected doctrine of tanasub dhati, and explain the rational proof used to reject inherent correspondence.

Core Idea
Words have external appropriateness based on temperaments, but no inherent essential link.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explores a foundational question: are words arbitrarily assigned to meanings, or is there a natural connection? The Hanafi school affirms a type of appropriateness (munasaba) based on external factors like the temperaments of peoples, but firmly rejects the idea of an inherent, essential correspondence (tanasub dhati) that would make assignment unnecessary. This distinction has implications for how we understand language as a divine creation and how we derive rulings from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The author discusses two views: one accepting munasaba (appropriateness) and one (by Abbas ibn Sulayman) claiming inherent correspondence (tanasub dhati) that suffices for understanding without assignment.
2
Understand the rationale for munasaba
Without some appropriateness, assigning a specific word to a meaning would be arbitrary—a preference without a preferrer, unfitting for a wise assigner (Allah). Therefore, a form of munasaba based on temperaments exists.
3
Examine the rejection of tanasub dhati
The rational proof: a thing cannot have an inherent correspondence to two opposite meanings simultaneously, as that is rationally impossible. For example, a single word cannot inherently mean both 'hot' and 'cold'.
This is the core rational argument (dalil aqli).
4
Evaluate the counterargument and its resolution
One objection is that inherent correspondence would make words inseparable from their meanings. The author refutes this by clarifying that 'necessitation' here is not essential but a separable accident—so inseparability does not follow.
5
Consider the anecdote of the Brahmin
The story appears to support Abbas's view but is reinterpreted: the Brahmin may have known the appropriateness for which words were assigned, not the inherent link, so assignment is still needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف في أن وضع اللفظ لمعناه هل لمناسبة بينه وبينه أم لا والحق اعتبار المناسبة بين اللفظ ومعناه وإلا فجعل بعضها لمعنى وبعضها الآخر ترجيح من غير مرجح، وشأن الحكيم يأبى عنه، وهذا ظاهر جداً عند كون الواضع هو الله تعالى (حتى) بین اللغات وبين (الأمزجة التي اكتسب هيولى كل قوم أراد بها ما فيه صلوح كمال لا ما يقول به الفلاسفة (من) عوارضها السماوية والأرضية) أي العارضة من خارج فاختصت لغة كل قوم بهم بحسب هذه المناسبة، وإلا فإعطاء الهندية لأهل الهند والعربية للعرب ليس أولى من العكس ومن ها هنا أي من أجل اعتبار المناسبة بين كل قوم ولغتهم (رأينا لسان سكان الجبال صلبة ثقيلة لكون أمزجتهم كذلك هذا : (وأما القول بالتناسب الذاتي) بين الألفاظ ومعانيها بأن يكون بين ذاتيهما مناسبة تقتضي عدم الانفكاك لو خليت وطباعها والاكتفاء به في الدلالة) بأن يكون العلم بهذا التناسب يكفي في انفهام المعنى منها كما ذهب إليه عباد بن سليمان وغيره من أتباعه ( فهو بعيد) لأن المناسبة الذاتية بين الشيء والضدين مما يمجه العقل، وأما الرد بأنه يلزم أن لا ينفك اللفظ عن الدلائل أصلاً فإن مقتضى الذات لا ينفك عنها فغير وارد، فإنه أراد بالاقتضاء ما ذكرنا ولو أراد بالاقتضاء الذاتي مقابل الوضع وإن كان من أعراض مفارقة لم يبعد وحينئذ لا يرد عليه ما ذكر ثم بعد لا يخلو عن بعد ومكابرة (وسمعت من بعض الشيوخ) سمعت من الثقات أنه أراد ببعض الشيوخ جدي المولى قطب الملة والدين الشهيد قدس سره أنه لقيه) أي لقي بعض الشيوخ رجل من البراهمة من جبال الشمال كان عنده قوانين يفهم منها كل لسان على وجه كلي هكذا سمعت ويمكن أن يرجع الضمير المنصوب إلى عباد بن سليمان وعلى كل تقدير يؤيد هذا النقل مذهب عباد بن سليمان نحو تأييد، ويجوز أن يكون فهمه بمعرفة تلك المناسبة التي وضعت الألفاظ لأجلها لا للاكتفاء بالمناسبة فقط، فحينئذٍ احتيج إلى الوضع والطريق
Then there is a disagreement about whether the assignment of a word to its meaning involves an appropriateness (munasaba) between them or not. The correct view is to consider the appropriateness between the word and its meaning; otherwise, assigning some words to meanings and others to other meanings would be a preference without a preferrer, and the way of the Wise (Allah) precludes that. This is very clear when the assigner is Allah. Hence, there is an appropriateness between languages and the temperaments (amzija) from which the matter (hayula) of each people is acquired—He intended by it [the temperament] what contains suitability for perfection, not what the philosophers say about their celestial and terrestrial accidents, i.e., those that come from outside. Thus, the language of each people is particular to them according to this appropriateness; otherwise, giving Hindi to the people of India and Arabic to the people of Arabia would not be more appropriate than the reverse. From here, due to the consideration of appropriateness between each people and their language, we see that the speech of mountain dwellers is solid and heavy because their temperaments are likewise. As for the statement of inherent correspondence (tanasub dhati) between words and their meanings—that there is a correspondence between their essences that necessitates inseparability if left to their own nature and suffices for indication, such that knowledge of this correspondence alone enables understanding of the meaning, as held by Abbas ibn Sulayman and others among his followers—this is far-fetched, because the correspondence between a thing and its two opposites is something the mind abhors. As for the refutation that it would entail that the word never separates from its indication, since a necessary property of the essence does not separate from it—this is not valid, because he meant by 'necessitation' what we mentioned. If he meant by 'inherent necessitation' in contrast to assignment, even if it is a separable accident, then it is not far-fetched; in that case, the above criticism does not apply. Nevertheless, it still involves some distance and contrariness. (And I heard from some shaykhs)—I heard from reliable persons that he meant by 'some shaykhs' my grandfather, the master of the milla and religion, the martyr—may Allah sanctify his secret—that a Brahmin man from the mountains of the north met him, and he had rules by which he could understand every language universally—this is what I heard. It is possible that the pronoun refers to Abbas ibn Sulayman. In any case, this narration supports the doctrine of Abbas ibn Sulayman to some extent. It is also possible that his understanding was by knowing that appropriateness for which the words were assigned, not by relying solely on the appropriateness; in that case, assignment (wad') and the method [of knowing it] are needed.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses a secondary issue in the theory of language: whether the assignment of a word to a meaning is based on some natural appropriateness (munasaba) between the word and its meaning. The author affirms that some appropriateness exists, otherwise the specific pairing of words to meanings would be arbitrary, which is not fitting for a wise assigner (Allah). This appropriateness is linked to the temperaments of different peoples; for example, mountain dwellers have heavy, solid speech corresponding to their solid temperaments. However, the stronger claim of an inherent, essential correspondence (tanasub dhati) that could bypass the need for assignment altogether—as alleged by Abbas ibn Sulayman and his followers—is rejected as far-fetched. The author provides a rational argument: an inherent correspondence between a word and two opposite meanings is rationally impossible. The author then relates a story about a Brahmin who reportedly could understand any language based on universal rules, but clarifies that this may be based on knowing the appropriateness rather than pure inherent correspondence. Ultimately, the passage upholds that while appropriateness exists, it does not eliminate the need for actual assignment (wad') and a method (tariq) to know it. This discussion is foundational for later issues like qiyas in language, since if words had inherent meanings, analogy could be used to deduce meanings.

Key Terms
المناسبة (al-munasaba): An appropriateness or suitability between a word and its meaning, often based on external factors like temperament.
التناسب الذاتي (al-tanasub al-dhati): An inherent, essential correspondence between word and meaning such that the meaning is understood without assignment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea of inherent correspondence (tanasub dhati) between words and meanings?
A) Because it would make language learning unnecessary.
B) Because it is impossible for a word to have an inherent relation to two opposite meanings.
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies it.
D) Because it contradicts the consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on language accepts external appropriateness (munasaba) but denies inherent correspondence, preserving the necessity of conventional assignment (wad') for legal interpretation.

Two Definitions of Mufrad and Murakkab
Definition of mufrad and murakkab with two technical usages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of mufrad and murakkab and apply them to examples like 'Ba'labak' and 'adrib'.

Core Idea
A word's classification depends on whether we consider customary unity or whether its parts indicate part of its meaning.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the basic classification of words into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) is critical for understanding how legal texts convey meaning. This passage presents two competing definitions: one based on customary unity, the other on whether the parts of a word individually contribute to the overall meaning. These definitions yield different results for proper names like 'Ba'labak' and verbs like 'adrib', directly impacting how we analyze the Qur'an and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
The passage states two definitions for mufrad: 'if it is unified, even in custom' and 'if no part of its word indicates a part of its meaning'. Each definition implies a corresponding definition for murakkab.
Note the role of 'urf (custom) in the first definition.
2
Apply the first definition to examples
Test 'al-rajul' (single word, mufrad) and 'Ba'labak' (composite, murakkab). Understand that the first definition relies on customary unity.
3
Apply the second definition to examples
For 'Ba'labak', since its parts do not indicate part of the proper name's meaning, it is mufrad by the second definition. For 'adrib' (I strike), the hamza indicates the speaker and the root indicates the event, so it is murakkab by the second definition.
The second definition focuses on whether parts individually contribute to meaning.
4
Analyze the disagreement
The passage discusses Ibn Sina's view (derived verbs are murakkab) against the grammarians (subject is implicit). Understand the argument: the verb can be true or false, so it must contain a subject indicator.
This disagreement is a key point in Hanafi usul.
5
Extend to participles
'Darib' (hitter) is murakkab by the second definition because its form indicates essence and relation, and its root indicates the event. Note the counter-argument and critique.
The author criticizes the counter-argument that the whole indicates the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم وهو) أي اللفظ الدال بالوضع مفرد إن توحد ولو عرفا) فنحو الرجل مفرد، لأنه لفظ واحد في العرف (وقيل) هو مفرد (إن لم يدل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه وإلا فمركب فيهما أي في الإصطلاحين المذكورين فغير المتوحد مركب وقيل : الدال جزؤه على جزء معناه مركب (ونحو: بعلبك) أي المركب الذي جعل علماً مركب على الأول لأن اللفظ غير متوحد، (لا) على (الثاني) بل مفرد عليه لأن جزء لفظه لا يدل على جزء معناه العلمي وأضرب بالعكس أي مفرد على الأول، لأن اللفظ واحد لكنه يدل على مسند ومسند إليه، بد من لفظين بإزائهما وهو الهمزة والمادة فجزؤه يدل على جزء معناه، فمركب على الثاني، هذا رأي ابن سينا في الشفاء المخالف فيه جمهور أهل العربية، فإنهم يقولون : إن اللفظ بتمامه يدل على المعنى الفعلي وأما المسند إليه فمنوي فيه ولا يلزم إجماعهم، هذا حجة عليه فإنه تحكم من غير دليل بل الفحص يحكم بما قال ابن سينا، فإنه لا شك أنه يفهم منه معنى يحتمل الصدق والكذب والحروف التي فيه تكفي للدلالة عليه، فالعدول عنه واعتبار المنوي لا يرخصه بصيرة أحد كما في ضربت هذا وأما المضارع الغائب فلا يدل على جملة، لأنه تبقى حاجته في احتمال الصدق والكذب إلى أن يذكر بعده منسوب إليه، ولذا قد يذكر الفاعل فيه فظهر الفرق بينهما بأوكد وجه فإن قلت المضارع الحاضر والمتكلم جملة، فينبغي أن يكون المعنى الفعلي مدلولاً للفظ ، والآخر للآخر، فينبغي أن تدل الهمزة والتاء فرداً على ذات المتكلم والحاضر والباقي يدل على المعنى الفعلي، قلت: لا يلزم ذلك لجواز أن يكون شرط الدلالة وضعا اجتماع الكلمتين، فلا يدلان عند الانفراد على شيء، كما لا يدل تاء ضربت على الانفراد على المخاطب ولا يرد على الاصطلاح (الثاني: نحو ضارب فإنه يدل بهيئته على المعنى الاشتقاقي من الذات والنسبة وبمادته على الحدث فقد دل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه فيلزم أن يكون مركباً، وإنما لا يرد التصريحهم بأن المراد الأجزاء التي هي ألفاظ (مرتبة) في السمع يدل على جزء معناه وههنا ليس كذلك، وأجيب في «البديع» بمنع أن الدال على الذات الهيئة وعلى الحدث المادة بل الدال المجموع على المجموع، وتعقب المصنف عليه بأنه لا فرق بين ضارب وضرب وحينئذ يلزم أن لا تكون هيئة الثاني
Division (of the word): [The word that indicates by placement] is mufrad if it is unified, even in customary terms. So 'al-rajul' is mufrad, because it is one word in custom. And it is said: it is mufrad if no part of its word indicates a part of its meaning; otherwise it is murakkab in both [definitions]. So what is not unified is murakkab. And it is said: the one whose part indicates a part of its meaning is murakkab. And like 'Ba'labak' (a place name): it is murakkab according to the first [definition] because the word is not unified; not according to the second, rather it is mufrad according to it because its part does not indicate a part of its [scientific] meaning. And [the verb] 'adrib' (I hit) is opposite: it is mufrad according to the first, because the word is one, but it indicates a predicate and a subject; it necessarily has two words corresponding to it, namely the hamza and the root, so its part indicates a part of its meaning, so it is murakkab according to the second. This is the view of Ibn Sina in al-Shifa', contradicted by the majority of Arabic grammarians. For they say: The whole word indicates the event meaning, while the subject is implicit (manawi) in it, and their consensus is not binding. This is an argument against him because it is an arbitrary claim without evidence; rather, investigation supports what Ibn Sina said. For there is no doubt that from it is understood a meaning that admits truth and falsehood, and the letters in it suffice for indication. So departing from that and considering the implicit is not conceded by anyone's insight, as in 'darabtu' (I hit). As for the third person imperfect, it does not indicate a complete sentence, because its capacity for truth and falsehood remains dependent on the mention of the agent afterwards; hence sometimes the agent is mentioned in it. So the difference between them appears most clearly. If you say that the present tense first person and second person are sentences, so the event meaning should be indicated by the word, and the other by another, then the hamza and the ta' should individually indicate the speaker and the addressee, and the rest indicates the event. I say: That does not follow, because it is possible that the condition for indication by placement is the combination of the two words, so they do not indicate anything individually, just as the 'ta' of 'darabtu' does not individually indicate the addressee. And this does not contradict the second [definition]: like 'darib' (hitting): it indicates by its form the derivative meaning of essence and relation, and by its root the event; thus part of its word indicates part of its meaning, so it must be murakkab. It is not refuted by their statement that what is meant is parts that are words arranged in the sense of hearing that indicate a part of the meaning, and here this is not the case. And it was answered in 'al-Badi'' by denying that the form indicates the essence and the root indicates the event; rather, the whole indicates the whole. And the author criticized him [the answerer] saying that there is no difference between 'darib' and 'daraba', and then the form of the second would not be...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the division of words into mufrad (simple) and murakkab (compound) according to two technical usages. The first definition (based on customary unity) considers 'al-rajul' as mufrad because it is one word; compound words like 'Ba'labak' are murakkab. The second definition (based on part indicating part of meaning) considers 'Ba'labak' as mufrad because its parts do not indicate part of the meaning of the proper name. The verb 'adrib' (I hit) is mufrad by the first definition but murakkab by the second because its parts (hamza and root) each indicate part of the meaning (subject and event). The author supports Ibn Sina's view that such verbs are compound, against the majority of Arabic grammarians who claim the subject is implicit. He argues that the verb clearly indicates a proposition that can be true/false, so it must be compound. The discussion then turns to participles like 'darib', which are murakkab by the second definition because the form indicates the essence and relation, and the root indicates the event. A counter-argument that the whole indicates the whole is rejected because it would erase the difference between 'darib' and 'daraba'.

Key Terms
المفرد (al-mufrad): A word that is single in customary terms (first definition) or whose part does not indicate a part of its meaning (second definition).
المركب (al-murakkab): A word that is not single (first definition) or whose part indicates a part of its meaning (second definition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second definition of 'mufrad' (part does not indicate part of meaning), which of the following is mufrad?
A) الرجل (al-rajul - the man)
B) بعلبك (Ba'labak - a place name)
C) ضارب (darib - hitter)
D) أضرب (adrib - I strike)

Key Takeaway
A word may be mufrad by customary unity but murakkab by the criterion of part-indicating-part, depending on whether its components individually contribute to the meaning.

The Four Types of Wad' and Compound Coinage
Expanding the types of wad' and refuting denial of compound wad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of al-wad' (linguistic coinage) and explain the Hanafi position on the separate wad' of compound words.

Core Idea
Words are assigned to meanings in four ways; compounds have a separate wad' beyond their parts.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, the author explains the Hanafi classification of linguistic coinage (al-wad') into four types, clarifying how words relate to their meanings. He defends the separate wad' of compound expressions and refutes Taftazani's denial of a particular type. Understanding these distinctions is foundational for later usul discussions on 'amm and khass.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of wad'
According to the Hanafi classification, wad' can be: (1) specific word for specific meaning, (2) general word for general meaning, (3) specific word for general meaning, (4) general word for specific meaning. The passage focuses on types (2) and (4) and their implications.
Type (4) is controversial; Taftazani denies its possibility.
2
Analyze the example of 'fa'il'
The author critiques the matn's example of 'fa'il' as general-to-general wad', arguing that the wad' of action nouns is actually for specific events. He reinterprets type (2) as multiple words observed under a general aspect assigned to specific meanings.
This critique shows the precision required in defining wad' types.
3
Recognize the separate wad' of compounds
The author affirms that compound expressions (e.g., 'Zayd standing') have a wad' as a whole, not just the sum of their parts. He refutes the view that only individual words have wad'.
This is a key Hanafi position for interpreting legal compounds.
4
Understand the 'general for specific' type
Demonstratives, pronouns, and particles are assigned to specific individuals through a general concept, avoiding both metaphor and homonymy. The author refutes Taftazani's claim that a fourth type (specific for general) is impossible.
The Hanafi view considers the fourth type possible but not actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

في مادة متصرفة، فهو لحدث حدث مما اشتق منه منتسب إلى فاعل معين في زمان الماضي، فليس هناك عام موضوع له، بل الحق أن حاصل هذا القسم أن الألفاظ المتعددة الملحوظة بلحاظ أمر كلي عام ، وكذلك المعاني، وكل اللفظ ملحوظ بخصوصه دون المعاني، فإنها أمور متعددة ملحوظة بوجه عام ومنه وضع المركبات) فإنها وضعت لمعان تركيبية في ضمن ضابطة كما يقال : كل مركب أحد جزأيه فعل والآخر فاعل، فقد وضع للأخبار بأن حدثه قائم به وليس الأمر كما ظن البعض أن ليس للمركب وضع على حدة سوى وضع المفردات، بل أوضاع المفردات كافية، وهذا الرأي فاسد فساداً يظهر بالتأمل (وقد يكون) الوضع (عاماً لخاص) أي لموضوع له خاص وقد تقدم شرحه (كوضع وفي المشترك أوضاع كثيرة، وفيه رد على من زعم أنها موضوعة لمفاهيم كلية لتستعمل في الإفراد لا للإفراد، وإلا يلزم الاشتراك بين المعاني الغير المحصورة، وعزي إلى الفاضل التفتازاني (وأما الوضع الخاص للعام لوحظ فيه الألفاظ بمفهوم كلي والمعنى بخصوصه، ويوضع كل واحد من الألفاظ لهذا المعنى مرة واحدة ولا شك في إمكانه وعدم وقوعه
The third article on linguistic principles: the assigner may make the assigned-to an general matter observed with a general concept at the time of assignment (like placing that every agent is assigned to a self from which the act emanates, so the self from which the act emanates is general, made a mirror for a general meaning which is a self from which hitting or helping or other things emanate. And this is not free from a touch of affectation, for the assignment of the word 'actions' is only for a specific event occurring with a specific agent in a specific time as in this and its likes. For the assignment of the past tense is only that every word on the pattern of fa'ala in a conjugated material is for an event that occurred from what it is derived, attributed to a specific agent in the past time, so there is no general assigned-to there. Rather the truth is that the outcome of this division is that multiple words observed with a view to a general universal matter, and likewise the meanings, and all words observed with the universal matter are assigned to a meaning from among the specific meanings observed with the universal concept. So in the first division both are observed with their specificities, and in this division both are multiple observed with a general universal aspect, just as in the third division the word is observed with its specificity without the meanings, for they are multiple matters observed with a general aspect. And from it is the assignment of compounds, for they are assigned to compositional meanings within a rule, as it is said: every compound whose one part is a verb and the other an agent has been assigned to the informing that its event is based on it. And it is not as some have thought that there is no separate assignment for compounds besides the assignment of individual words; rather individual assignments suffice. This view is corrupt with a corruption that appears through reflection. And the assignment may be general for specific, i.e. for a specific assigned-to, and its explanation has preceded (like the assignment of demonstrative pronouns, personal pronouns, relative pronouns, and particles, for what is observed at the time of assignment is the general universal matter, like the concept of the one pointed to or the one absent male or the one described by the relative clause, but it is not observed so that it would be assigned for it, but rather to make it a mirror for observing the unrestricted individuals, so it is assigned to them specifically, so neither metaphor nor homonymy follows from its use in specific instances. This contains a refutation of the one who claimed that they are assigned for universal concepts to be used in particular instances, not for the particulars, otherwise homonymy among unrestricted meanings would occur. And it is attributed to al-Fadil al-Taftazani: as for the specific assignment for the general according to his view, that is to observe the general multiple assigned-to in a specific particular aspect and assign the word to it, so it has not occurred but rather is impossible due to the impossibility of this kind of observation. But according to what we explained in the rule of the second and third divisions, the fourth division emerges: what is observed in it is words with a universal concept and meaning with its specificity, and each word is assigned to this meaning once, and there is no doubt of its possibility and non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the types of linguistic coinage (al-wad'). The author discusses the third type (general to general) where both word and meaning are general, but he critiques the example given by the matn author regarding 'fa'il'. He explains that the real import of this type is that general words correspond to specific meanings under a general concept. Then he discusses the assignment of compound constructions (al-murakkabat), affirming that they have a separate wad' beyond that of their individual components, refuting a contrary opinion. Next, he explains the type 'general for specific' (e.g., demonstratives) and refutes Taftazani's view that a fourth type (specific for general) is impossible, showing it is possible but not actual. The discussion clarifies the Hanafi linguistic framework for wad'.

Key Terms
الوضع (al-wad'): Linguistic coinage; the assignment of a word to a meaning for the purpose of indication.
المشتق (al-mushtaq): A derived word that shares original letters and meaning with its root.
المركب (al-murakkab): A compound expression consisting of two or more words, assigned a meaning as a whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's main argument against the view that compound words have no separate linguistic coinage?
A) Compounds are used metaphorically, so they need separate wad'.
B) Compounds are assigned to compositional meanings within a rule, which cannot be reduced to the sum of parts.
C) Compounds are rare, so a separate wad' is unnecessary.
D) The wad' of individual words already covers compound meanings.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes four types of wad', including a separate category for compound words, which is essential for interpreting composite legal terms.

Do Derived Nouns Include the Essence? The Hanafi Position
Debate on whether derived nouns imply a subject or are simple concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on whether derived nouns include the essence, and apply the Hanafi author's argument using relational existence to evaluate the dispute.

Core Idea
Derived nouns are simple predicates; including the essence is unnecessary for predication.

In this passage, we delve into a classic usul al-fiqh debate: do derived nouns (mushtaqqat) like 'aswad' (black) implicitly include an essence (dhat) or are they simple concepts? The Hanafi author (al-ashbah) takes the position that derivatives are predicates with relational existence, directly linking to subjects without needing an essence in their concept. This lesson unpacks the arguments from both sides and clarifies the Hanafi reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the author's view (al-ashbah): derived nouns do not include the essence; they are predicates with relational-existential existence.
Look for the phrase 'al-ashbah' and the claim that 'mahsulat mahmudat' have relational existence.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Note the opponent's claim: derivatives indicate a general essence (or specific essence). Understand why they argue this, e.g., to avoid redundancy or to capture the compound nature of reality.
The opponent's argument is partly reconstructed from the text; they say that if derivatives didn't include essence, predication would be problematic.
3
Analyze the opponent's proof
Examine the logical flow: if 'aswad' included particular essence (e.g., body), then saying 'the body is black' would be redundant. The opponent counters that the derivative might include a general essence to avoid this.
Follow the discussion about 'dhati bayyin' and the distinction between detailed and summary observation.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Understand the author's rebuttal: predicates have relational existence, so they unite directly with subjects; no need for a separate essence. The relational existence makes predication valid without positing an intermediate essence.
Focus on the concept of 'wujud rabiti' and the distinction between unconditioned (la bi-shart) and conditioned with nothing (bi-shart la).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider how this debate affects the analysis of propositions in usul al-fiqh. For example, when interpreting a Quranic term like 'qatil', does it automatically imply the subject's essence? The Hanafi view simplifies logical analysis.
This is a theoretical dispute with implications for how we understand the meaning of terms in revelation.

لا بشرط شيء كان مفهوم أسود ،وعرضياً، وإذا أخذ بشرط لا شيء كان عرضاً وعين السواد، وإذا أخذ بشرط المحل كان الثوب الأسود، ومقصوده أنه على هذا التقرير يحصل هذا المقيد لا أنه يكون عين المحل ويتحد معه، كيف وقد عرفت أن اتحاد الاثنين باطل مطلقاً، وكيف يتفوه به أمثال هذا المحقق هذا تقرير كلامه على وجه يوافق مرامه، ثم إنه دعوى نظرية في مقابلة السواد الأعظم، فلا تسمع من غير دليل وأما أنه معنى بسيط فمسلم لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون إجمال هذا المركب أي ذات له السواد لا بد لنفيه من دليل وما قال إنه لا يفهم منه ذات له السوادان أراد هذا المفصل فمسلم ولا يلزم منه المطلوب. وإن أراد إجماله فممنوع أنه غير منفهم بل هذا المعنى البسيط الذي ادعاه هو إجماله لا غير وقال المصنف : (وهو الأشبه فإن) المشتقات محمولات والمحمولات من حيث هي هي لها وجودات رابطية اتحادية مع الموضوعات فلا حاجة إلى أخذ الذات في مفهوماتها فإن مفهوم له السواد كما أنه يتحد مع الذات على تقدير أخذها كذلك يتحد بالجسم الموضوع بلا توسيط الذات، نعم : لو قصد إلى تعبيره ولم يكن خصوص الموضوع في البين احتيج في التعبير إلى أخذ الذات المبهمة تحصيلاً للتعبير لا غير بخلاف المبادىء لها فإن وجوداتها ليست وجودات رابطية (بناء على أن الفرق بينهما أن الأولى) أي المشتقات (لا) بشرط شيء) فهي صالحة لأن ترتبط بغيرها بهوهو لأن فيها نوعاً من الإبهام (والثانية) أي المبادىء (بشرط لا شيء) فهي متحصلة بالذات، وموجودة بوجود مغاير للموضوع فلا تصلح لأن ترتبط بغيرها بهوهو هذا تقرير كلامه (فافهم) وفيه: أن كون وجود المحمولات رابطية لا يستلزم أن تكون معانيها بسائط، بل يجوز أن تكون مركبة ويكون وجوداتها ،رابطية ثم إن ما قال لا حاجة إلى الذات حينئذ منظور فيه، فإن الخصم لا يقنع عليه، وأيضاً لم يؤخذ الذات لصحة الحمل، ولا لأنه لا يمكن صحته بدونه بل لأن معاني المشتقات وجدت في الواقع كذلك مركبات من الذات والصفة وهل هذا إلا كما يقال : يكفي للحمل على زيد مفهوم الناطق، فلا يحتاج إلى أخذ الحيوان في الإنسان ثم هذه التفرقة الاعتبارية التي ادعاها من الفرق لا بشرط شيء وبشرط لا شيء غير لازم من كون وجودات المحمولات رابطية، فإنه يجوز أن يكون معاني المشتقات بسيطة ووجوداتها رابطية لكن
Claim from the issue of 'black' and similar derivatives: they indicate a certain essence qualified by blackness, for example, that derivatives indicate the absolute essence, not the particularity of the essence such as being a body or otherwise. Otherwise, meaning if it were not so but indicated the particularity of the essence, then our statement 'the black' would benefit 'body', because the essential is evident in its existence for that which it is essential. And the body on this assumption becomes essential for 'black' due to its inclusion in it. In it is that it is only essential, its existence is evident if the whole is observed in detail, which is denied here. So 'the black' as conceptualized in summary does not have a evident essential existence for it. The commentator said in the Hashiyya: It is not unlikely to be established like this: if the particularity of body were included in 'black', then its predication on it in the level of detail would be non-beneficial. This contains a type of doubt, for the opponent may deny the falsity of the necessary consequent, so it involves denying the entailment if the whole is observed in summary, and denying the falsity of the necessary consequent if observed in detail. And some verifiers, namely al-Mawla Jalal al-Din, may God have mercy on him, hold that the derivative does not indicate the essence at all, neither generally nor specifically. For we know necessarily that its concept is nothing but what is expressed as 'siyah' and 'safid'. So the meaning of 'the body is black' is 'the body has blackness', rather 'a black body' not that it is 'a body having blackness' so that the body is observed twice, once as subject and once in the predicate. Nor does it also mean an essence having blackness, for that is not understood at all. But this simple concept entails an essence having blackness, not that it is identical to it. This simple concept has two individuals: the body, to which it applies accidentally because it is related to it by a specific relation by which its existence is attributed to it, and it is said that it is it; and another individual that applies to it essentially, for it is its complete reality, as blackness is black by itself. The difference between them is by absoluteness and restriction. And it is what this verifier said: if this concept is taken unconditionally (la bi-shart shay'), it is the concept of 'black' and accidental; if taken with the condition of nothing (bi-shart la shay'), it is the accident and the very blackness; if taken with the condition of a locus (bi-shart al-mahal), it is the black garment. His intent is that on this analysis, the restricted is obtained, not that it is the very locus and united with it. How could it be? When you have known that union of two is absolutely false, and how could someone like this verifier utter that? This is a presentation of his speech in a manner consistent with his aim. Then it is a theoretical claim against the vast majority, so it is not accepted without evidence. As for it being a simple meaning, it is granted, but why is it not possible that it is an abbreviation of this compound, i.e., an essence having blackness? There must be evidence for its negation. And what he said that 'an essence having blackness' is not understood from it: if he means this detailed meaning, it is granted but the required result does not follow; if he means its abbreviation, it is denied that it is not understood; rather this simple meaning he claims is nothing but its abbreviation. The author said: (and it is the more appropriate, for) derivatives are predicates, and predicates as such have relational-existential existence that unifies with subjects, so there is no need to take the essence in their concepts. For the concept of 'having blackness' just as it unites with the essence on the assumption of taking it, so too it unites with the subject body without meditating the essence. Yes, if one intends to express it and there is no particular subject in view, then in expression one needs to take the vague essence for achieving expression only, unlike the principles (mabadi'), for their existences are not relational-existential. Based on the difference that the first (i.e., derivatives) are unconditioned (la bi-shart shay') so they are suitable to be connected to others by 'huwa huwa' because they have a type of ambiguity; and the second (i.e., principles) are conditioned with nothing (bi-shart la shay'), so they are self-determined and exist with an existence different from the subject, so they are not suitable to be connected to others by 'huwa huwa'. This is the presentation of his speech (so understand). And in it: that the existence of predicates being relational does not entail that their meanings are simple; rather they may be compound while their existences are relational. Then what he said that there is no need for the essence is then subject to consideration, for the opponent is not satisfied with it. Also, the essence is not taken for the sake of the validity of predication, nor because it cannot be valid without it; rather because the meanings of derivatives are found in reality as compounds of essence and attribute. Is this not like it is said: for predication on Zayd, the concept of 'natiq' suffices, so there is no need to take 'animal' in 'human'. Then this notional distinction he claimed of the difference between unconditioned and conditioned with nothing is not necessary from the existence of predicates being relational, for it is possible that the meanings of derivatives are simple while their existences are relational, but...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a complex debate on the linguistic meaning of derived nouns (mushtaqqat). The discussion starts with the example of 'aswad' (black) and whether its meaning includes a specific essence (dhat) or not. The initial claim is that derivatives indicate a general essence (mulaq al-dhat), not a particular essence like being a body. If it were otherwise, then 'aswad' would imply body, making body an essential part of its meaning, which is problematic. A scholar in the marginal commentary (Hashiyya) argues that the first view leads to a difficulty because if 'body' were part of 'aswad', then saying 'a body is black' would be repetitive. The text then presents the view of a leading scholar, al-Mawla Jalal al-Din (al-Dawwani), who asserts that derivatives do not indicate any essence at all—neither general nor specific. According to him, the meaning of 'aswad' is a simple concept expressed in Persian as 'siyah' and 'safid' (black and white). Thus, 'the body is black' means 'the body has blackness' or 'a black body', not 'a body having blackness' (which would observe body twice). This simple concept entails but is not identical to a compound meaning of 'essence having blackness'. The simple concept has two applications: to a body (accidental) and to blackness itself (essential, since blackness is black by itself). The difference is based on taking the concept with or without conditions. Al-Dawwani distinguishes three modes: unconditioned (la bi-shart shay') gives the concept of 'aswad' as an accident; conditioned by nothing (bi-shart la shay') gives the accident itself; conditioned by a locus (bi-shart al-mahal) gives the black object. The commentator then criticizes this view, arguing that it is a theoretical claim against the majority and requires proof. The author of the matn (Musallam al-Thubut) then gives his own view: 'the more appropriate is that...' He argues that derivatives are predicates, and predicates have relational-existential existence that unites with subjects, so there is no need to include the essence in their concepts. The concept 'having blackness' unites directly with the subject body without needing the intermediate of an abstract essence. However, he notes that if there is no particular subject in view, one must use a vague essence for expression. He distinguishes between derivatives (unconditioned, fit for connection) and principles (mabadi', conditioned by nothing, self-subsistent). The commentator then raises objections: the relational existence of predicates does not entail that their meanings are simple; they can be compound while having relational existence. Also, the claim that no essence is needed is controversial. The passage ends mid-sentence, continuing onto the next page.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaqq): A derived noun, e.g., aswad; its meaning is a simple concept without inherent inclusion of the subject.
ذات (dhat): Essence or subject; whether it is part of the meaning of a derived noun is disputed.
وجود رابطي (wujud rabiti): Relational existence, i.e., the existence of a predicate that links to a subject without independent subsistence.
بشرط لا (bi-shart la): Conditioned with nothing; used to describe principles that are taken as self-subsistent and not fit for relational predication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's view (al-ashbah), why is there no need to include the essence in the meaning of derived nouns?
A) Because derived nouns are always used with a specific subject.
B) Because predicates have relational-existential existence that unites with subjects directly.
C) Because the essence is never part of the meaning of any word.
D) Because the derived noun 'aswad' includes the essence by definition.

Key Takeaway
Derived nouns are predicates with relational existence; their meanings are simple and do not include the essence. This view is preferred in the Hanafi usul tradition.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Rebuttal of the Infinity Argument Against Commonality
Refuting the argument that finite words cannot cover infinite meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to summarize the Hanafi rebuttal of the argument that finite words cannot cover infinite meanings, including the counterexample of number ranks, the limitation of general placement, and the alternative answers.

Core Idea
Finite words can cover infinite differing meanings, as shown by number ranks, and commonality does not require actual infinite, only potential.

The objector argues that since meanings are infinite and words finite, commonality (اشتراك) must be false to avoid some meanings being unexpressible. The Hanafi madhhab rejects this through several lines of reasoning: (1) the ranks of numbers are infinite and differing yet are expressed, (2) general placement only applies to identicals, not differings, (3) comprehensible meanings are finite, (4) composition can be infinite, (5) metaphorical expression suffices, and (6) only potential commonality is needed. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its premise
State the objector's claim: words are finite, meanings are infinite, so if commonality (اشتراك) is not admitted, infinite meanings would lack words, which is impossible. Hence commonality must be denied.
This is the standard anti-commonality argument based on the mismatch between word count and meaning count.
2
Analyze the first rebuttal: ranks of numbers
Identify that the ranks of numbers (1,2,3,...) are infinite and are differing types (odd, even, prime, etc.). Yet they are expressed by the same finite set of numeral words (e.g., 'one', 'two', etc.) through composition. This disproves the premise that infinite differing meanings cannot be expressed.
The author stresses that التخالف النوعي (specific difference) among number ranks is conventional, not real, but still suffices.
3
Examine the general placement argument
The objector might claim that general placement (وضع عام) for specific things solves the problem, but the Hanafi response is that general placement only applies to identical particulars (متماثلة), not to differing universals (متخالفة). Thus it does not cover the infinite differing meanings under dispute.
E.g., demonstrative pronoun 'this' covers all pointed objects by virtue of being pointed, which is an identical aspect, not by their differing natures.
4
Consider the alternative answers
The passage gives further answers: (a) what we comprehend (ما نعقله) is finite, so finite words suffice; (b) word composition can be infinite if the number of steps is unlimited, matching infinite meanings; (c) metaphorical expression (التعبير بالمجاز) can fill gaps; (d) only potential commonality (بالقوة) is needed, not actual infinite commonality.
The last answer is particularly important: the objector demands actual infinite commonality, but potential commonality is sufficient and not impossible.
5
Evaluate the overall strength
The Hanafi position is robust because it not only refutes the objection but offers multiple independent arguments. The objector's argument is shown to be based on a false premise (that infinite differing meanings cannot be expressed) and an overly strong requirement (actual infinite commonality).
The rebuttal aligns with linguistic reality, where words like 'ayn' have multiple meanings without causing communication breakdown.

من حيث إنها متخالفة لأن الهذية في اسم الإشارة إنما تعتبر من حيث إنها محسوسة ومشار إليها والإنسان والفرس سواء في هذا المعنى وليس المراد التخالف النوعي حتى يرد عليه ما يرد ومراتب الأعداد متخالفة، فلا بد بإزائها بالتماثل عدم من وضع بتلك الحيثية، وإذ ليس فالخلو لازم كذا في الحاشية والإيراد عليه بتجويز جريان الوضع العام في المتخالفة من حيث التخالف يعد من المكابرات كيف لا ولا بد فيه من معرفة متماثلة المذكور ويعبر حين الإفادة بهذا الاسم وتفهم المعاني بالقرينة كما لو كان اللفظ مشتركاً فتدبر و أجيب بأن ما نعقله متناه، وهو المحتاج إليه حين الإفادة فإن أريد خلو ما نعقل عن الوضع له فممنوع فإنها المتناهي اللايقفي هو مراد المستدل (بل) (الجواب عن الدليل (منع) أن الألفاظ متناهية و ( أن المركب من المتناهي متناه وإنما يكون المركب من المتناهي متناهياً (لو كان) التركيب بمرات متناهية) وهو ممنوع بل ألفاظ كثيرة، فكذا هذا، فكذا هذا نعم لا يصح أن يجعل المركب من ألفاظ فوق ثلاثة أسماء واحداً كبعلبك، ولا حاجة إليه، بل يكفي الوضع ولو بالوضع الذي يكون في المركبات، سواء جعل اسماً واحداً أو لا فتدبر (وأيضاً) الجواب بتسليم لزوم الخلو ومنع بطلانه ولا يضر الإفادة، إذ (يجوز التعبير بالألفاظ المجازية ولا بأس به قيل: أكثر اللغة مجاز، وأيضاً الجواب بالنقض، فإنه يقال : (لو تم الدليل لكان بعض الألفاظ
