The utmost that follows from this exposition, if one concedes the impossibility of being empty [of a term], is commonality among identical particulars, which is not what is claimed, for the claim is commonality among differing universals. (In it: the ranks of numbers are infinite and are differing types, so the non-finiteness of differing meanings is established, and the prevention of the first branch is removed, which is the chosen view. And what is said that the specific difference among ranks of numbers is prevented, rather they argued against it, is nothing because what is meant by specific difference is not real specific difference but what is conventionally considered a specific difference. Also, theologians and philosophers consider such denial as obstinacy, so reflect. (And by it) i.e., by this answer, what was said is repelled: we do not concede the emptiness of named things when commonality is absent, because it is permissible to place a word for many meanings at once, by way of general placement for specific things, and that is repelled because general placement for specific things only occurs among identical individuals, i.e., insofar as they are identical, not among differing ones i.e., insofar as they are differing, because the suchness in the demonstrative pronoun is considered only insofar as it is sensibly perceived and pointed to, and man and horse are equal in this meaning. It is not intended as specific difference such that the objection would apply. And the ranks of numbers are differing, so there must be for them by identity a lack of placement in that respect, and since it is not, emptiness is necessary, as in the gloss. And objecting to it by allowing general placement in differing ones in terms of difference is counted as obstinacy, because it requires knowledge of identical particulars for which the word is placed by the universal common aspect, so they are insofar as they are this in the general aspect, placed for them, nothing else, so reflect. Yes, it is objected that the necessity of placement in terms of difference is denied; rather, a name can be placed for them in terms of the mentioned identity, and at the time of communication one expresses with this name and the meanings are understood by context, as if the word were common, so reflect. And it was answered: what we comprehend is finite, and it is what is needed at the time of communication. If what is meant is the emptiness of what we comprehend from having a word for it, it is denied, for they are finite like words, so equality between them is possible. And if what is meant is the emptiness of what is not comprehended, then the falsehood of the consequent is denied. (In it: that) i.e., what we comprehend is non-finite, in the sense that it does not stop at a limit, even if it is finite in terms of quantitative finitude. (And that is what is meant) i.e., the non-finite that does not stop is what the arguer intends. (Rather, the answer to the proof is to deny that words are finite, and that the composite of finite is finite, but that the composite of finite is finite only if the composition is by finite steps, which is denied, rather the number of compositions does not stop at a limit like meanings. If you say: this is because the singular is not composed of more than seven letters, and the composite is not composed of more than words, so the number of compositions is not non-stopping. I say: the ranks of numbers are expressed only by composite words many times, so likewise this. Yes, it is not valid to make the composite of more than three names one like Baalbek, and there is no need for it; rather, placement suffices even with placement that occurs in composites, whether it is made one name or not, so reflect. (Also) the answer is to concede the necessity of emptiness and deny its invalidity, and it does not harm communication, because expression with metaphorical words is permissible and there is no harm in it. It is said: most language is metaphorical. Also the answer is by counterexample, for it is said: if the proof were valid, some words would be placed for infinite meanings, for then the finite part would be for the finite part of meanings, and the remaining infinite would be empty. He said in the gloss: if non-finiteness is meant as not stopping, then the utmost that follows is non-stopping commonality, not commonality in actual infinite, which is impossible. This, and it is not hidden that it turns against the original proof, for it can be said: actual commonality is not necessary, because placement can be non-stopping; the placet invents at the time and places it for a meaning, and so on, and intelligence of what exceeds the number of word compositions does not pass from potency to actuality, so commonality is not necessary except in potency, so reflect.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rebuttal of the argument that since meanings are infinite and words finite, commonality (اشتراك) must be rejected. The author first refines the claim: the objector argues that if words are not common, then the infinite differing meanings would lack words. The reply shows that the ranks of numbers (which are infinite and differing) already have words, so the objection fails. Then, the possibility of general placement for specific things is discussed, but it only applies to identical particulars, not differing ones. Later, the author counters that our comprehensible meanings are finite, or that composition of words can be infinite. Finally, several alternative answers are given: concede emptiness but deny harm (metaphorical expression suffices), and a counterexample against the objector's own argument. The passage closes by noting that the opponent's demand for actual infinite commonality is not required; potential commonality suffices.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Having one word for multiple meanings (homonymy/ambiguous).
تواطؤ (tawāṭu’): Univocity: a single meaning applied to multiple individuals equally.
وضع عام (waḍ‘ ‘āmm): Setting a word by a general aspect for each particular under that aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary counterexample used by the Hanafi school to refute the infinity argument against commonality?
A) The finite number of letters in Arabic.
B) The ranks of numbers being infinite and differing yet expressed.
C) The use of metaphor in everyday language.
D) The fact that comprehensible meanings are finite.

Key Takeaway
The infinity argument against commonality fails because finite words can express infinite differing meanings, as demonstrated by the ranks of numbers and other arguments.

The Reality of Homonymy (Ishtirak) in Language and Revelation
Analyzing arguments for and against the reality of linguistic homonymy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi arguments for the occurrence of homonymy in language and revelation, and evaluate opposing objections using usul al-fiqh principles.

Core Idea
Homonymy is real and beneficial; context and deliberate ambiguity serve deeper communicative goals.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut delves into the contentious issue of linguistic homonymy (ishtirak) in both human language and divine revelation. The Hanafi school affirms that homonymy not only occurs but serves important functions, from facilitating diverse meanings to allowing strategic ambiguity. Understanding this debate is crucial for accurately interpreting Quranic and Hadith texts that may be semantically ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the rational proof from 'existent'
The Hanafis first present a rational argument: if homonymy did not exist, the term 'existent' would be univocal between the Necessary Being and contingent beings, leading to a contradiction. Your task is to trace why univocality would cause a problem and how the opponent responds.
The proof assumes a particular metaphysical stance. Note the various theological positions discussed (Ash'ari, Peripatetic, etc.) that affect the validity of the proof.
2
Examine the Hanafi response to the objectors
Deniers of homonymy argue that homonyms disrupt communication (tafhim). The Hanafis counter that context (qarina) resolves ambiguity, and that sometimes obscurity (ibham) is the intended purpose. Identify the example of Abu Bakr and explain how it refutes the objectors.
Also note that the objection fails against those who hold that both meanings are intended simultaneously (Shafi'is) or when coinage is human (where purpose may be absent).
3
Evaluate the textual evidence from Quran and Hadith
The Hanafis cite specific examples: 'qur'' in Quran 2:228 meaning both menstruation and purity, and 'casa' in 81:17 meaning both approaching and departing. The hadith 'ayyam aqra'ik' is also used. Analyze how these examples serve as evidence for ishtirak.
Opponents argue that explained homonyms are redundant (tawil) and unexplained ones useless. The Hanafis rebut that ambiguity followed by explanation has eloquence, and that sometimes no single non-homonymous term can convey the same meaning.
4
Synthesize the Hanafi position on ishtirak
Combine the rational, textual, and pragmatic arguments to formulate the complete Hanafi view: homonymy is real, occurs in language and revelation, and does not inherently hinder communication. Understand the practical implications for interpreting ambiguous texts.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that Hanafis rely on context to determine one meaning, while Shafi'is permit using the homonym for both meanings simultaneously.

بالنظر إلى موصوف آخر) فالموجود بالنظر إلى ذات الباري عز وجل واجب دون غيره من الذوات ومن ههنا أي من أجل أن المقصود من الجواب الأول النقض علم سقوط ما قيل عليه أن للمستدل أن يقول : كلامي في نفس هذه الحقيقة أي حقيقة الموجود لا في الحقائق المندرجة تحتها فإنها من حيث هي إما واجبة أو ممكنة ولا يصح الاجتماع، فحينئذ الاختلاف بالوجوب والإمكان فيه يضر وحدة الحقيقة فيضر التواطؤ واعلم أن مناط كلام هذا الواجب واجب وفي الممكن ممكن ويمكن إرجاع كلامه إلى جواب المصنف، وحينئذ يندفع إيراد هذا القائل هذا وتحقيق هذا المقام أن المستدل إما أن يريد نفس مفهوم الوجود والموجود الانتزاعي، وإما به يصير الشيء واقعاً صالحاً لانتزاع هذا المفهوم ومطابقاً لحمله فإن أراد الثاني وبنى كلامه على قول الشيخ الأشعري من العينية لسائر الذوات كما هو الحق فلا توجه للجواب أصلاً إذ الحاصل حينئذ أنه لو كان الموجود غير مشترك بل متواطئاً يلزم أن تكون الذوات كلها متوافقة بالحقيقة، لأن الذوات نفسه فيلزم أن تكون حقيقة الواجب والممكن واحدة، هذا خلف فحينئذ تم الدليل ولا يرد النقض ولا يتوجه الحل كما لا يخفى على ذي كياسة، وكذا لو يفيد وإن بني كلامه على مذهب باقي المتكلمين من الزيادة في الكل وإن كان هذا المبنى فاسداً في نفسه فحينئذ يتم جواب المصنف فإنه حينئذ ممكن بالذات واجب باقتضاء الذات وإيجابه في الباري وكذا بالنظر إلى الواجب الموصوف وممكن بالنظر إلى الذوات الممكنة وإن أراد الأول تعين جواب المصنف أيضاً كما قررنا،

النافون للاشتراك (قالوا : لو وضعت الألفاظ (مشتركة لاختل المقصود) من الوضع (وهو التفهيم) للمراد فإنه لا يفهم منه عند الإطلاق شيء أصلاً، فإن قلت : فما تصنعون بوجود الألفاظ المشتركة؟ قالوا: وما يظن به ذلك) أي الألفاظ التي يظن بها الاشتراك (فإما مجاز) موضوع لواحد فقط (أو متواطىء) موضوع للقدر المشترك بين المعاني (قلنا) لا نسلم إخلال الاشتراك بالمقصود ، بل يعرف المراد بالقرائن فلا يخل بالتفهيم ولو سلم الإخلال بالتفهيم كما عند عدم القرينة فلا نسلم أن المقصود من الوضع التفهيم كيف (وقد يكون الغرض من إطلاق اللفظ الإبهام كقول خليفة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبي بكر الصديق (رضي الله عنه) الهجرة حين سأله رجل عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : من معك؟ (رجل يهديني السبيل) فإنه أراد يهديني سبيل الله وأوهمه أنه رجل يهدي طريق السير، وكان الإبهام هو المقصود هناك كما لا يخفى على أنه لا تنتهض هذه الحجة على من قال بعمومه كالشافعية فإنه عندهم يراد المعنيان فلا يضر التفهيم المقصود، فإن المقصود حينئذ تفهيمهما لا تفهيم أحدهما (ولا) ينتهض أيضاً على قال (بوضع البشر) فإن الإخلال بالمقصود لا خلف فيه حينئذ، فإن أكثر أفعال البشر لا من يترتب عليها أغراضهم منها (وهو) أي وضع البشر (السبب) للاشتراك (غالباً) فإنه يوضع المعنى مع الجهل بوضعه لآخر.

البيان
(هل وقع المشترك في القرآن اختلف فيه ؟ فقيل : لا وقيل : نعم (وقيل و) هل وقع (في الحديث) اختلف فيه أيضاً والأصح الوقوع في القرآن بل وفي الحديث أيضاً (ولنا) قوله تعالى : ﴿ثلاثة قروء﴾ [البقرة: 228] والقرء للحيض والطهر كما مر وقوله تعالى : ﴿والليل إذا عسعس﴾ [التكوير: 17] (لأقبل وأدبر) وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «دعي الصلاة أيام أقرائك» رواه الترمذي المنكرون (قالوا : إن وقع المشترك في القرآن (مبيناً طال) الكلام (بلا فائدة) وهو مخل بالبلاغة والملازمة لأن المنفرد عن الاشتراك الدال على ما أريد من المشترك مع مغن عنه أي عن المشترك المبين، وعلى هذا لا يرد ما أورد الفاضل التفتازاني على التقرير المشهور بأن البيان يكفي، فيكون المشترك حشواً أنه يجوز أن يحصل البيان من المجموع واحد حتى يكون الآخر طولاً (وغير المبين غير مفيد للتفهيم فلا يقع البتة (قلنا) نختار الشق الأول ولا نسلم الملازمة، بل (الإبهام) أولاً ثم التفسير ) له من البلاغة) فلا يكون بلا فائدة (و) أيضاً ربما لم يكن هناك منفرد) يفيد معنى هذا المشترك المقرون بالبيان، فلا يكون تطويلاً، بل هو المتعين طريقاً إلى الإفهام (و) أيضاً قد تكون (القرينة) لتعيين المراد (حالية) فلا طول في اللفظ ونختار أيضاً الشق الثاني، ولا نسلم أنه غير مفيد إذ لا يلزم أن تكون الفائدة الإفهام، بل هناك فوائد أخرى تحصل من غير المبين كيف وغير المبين يفيد الذهاب
The third article on linguistic principles. Annotation: If by non-finiteness is meant non-termination, then what follows is non-terminating homonymy, not actual infinite homonymy, which is impossible. This is not hidden that it reverses the basis of the proof, for it can be said: actual homonymy does not necessarily follow, because it is possible that the coinage is non-terminating, so the coiner invents upon... and places it for a meaning, and so on, and the conception of what exceeds the number of combinations of words does not go from potentiality to actuality, so homonymy does not necessarily follow except potentially, so ponder. (And) he argued (secondly) for the occurrence of homonymy, rather its necessity, by saying that if homonymy were not real, then 'existent' would be univocal between the Necessary and the contingent, leading to the one in reality being both necessary and contingent—this is false. (The answer: difference by necessity and contingency does not prevent univocality, like 'knower' and 'speaker', for they are univocal between the Necessary and contingent. The author said: its summary is refutation by them two. It is not hidden that then his statement 'difference by necessity and contingency etc.' is amended... rather mentioning only the matter of refutation suffices. (And the solution: that necessity by another does not contradict contingency by essence, so the concept of existent is necessary by another which is the essence in the Necessary because it is its requisite, and contingent in itself, just as necessity with respect to a described does not contradict contingency with respect to another described). So the existent with respect to the essence of Allah, Exalted is He, is necessary, not other essences. And from here, i.e., because the purpose of the first answer is refutation, it is known that what was said against it falls—that the arguer can say: my speech is about this very reality, i.e., the reality of existent, not about the realities subsumed under it, for they in themselves are either necessary or contingent, and combination is not valid. So then the difference in necessity and contingency harms the unity of the reality, thus harming univocality. Know that the focus of this speaker's speech is that he understood the gist of the answer that all that follows from the univocality of existent between the Necessary and contingent is the difference of its individuals by necessity and contingency, and that is permissible. So he objected: no, rather it necessitates that this very concept be necessary and contingent, since existence in the Necessary is necessary and in the contingent is contingent. And it is possible to refer his speech to the author's answer, and then the objection of this speaker is repelled. The verification of this station is that the arguer either intends the very concept of existence and existent as an abstracted notion, or that by which a thing becomes actual, fit for abstraction of this concept and corresponding to its predication. If he intends the second and bases his speech on the saying of al-Ash'ari of essential identity with all essences, as is the truth, then there is no facing of the answer at all, because then the result is that if existent were not shared but univocal, it would necessitate that all essences be identical in reality, because essences themselves, so it would necessitate that the reality of the Necessary and contingent be one—this is false. So then the proof is complete, and the refutation does not stand nor the solution is directed, as is not hidden to anyone with intelligence. Similarly, if he bases on the opinion of the Peripatetic philosophers of identity in the Necessary and addition in the contingent, and this base is far, yes it could be objected that on this your desired conclusion follows only if the word 'existent' is established for its particular referent in language, otherwise it does not benefit. And if he bases his speech on the doctrine of the rest of the theologians of addition in all, even if this foundation is invalid in itself, then the author's answer is fulfilled, for then it is contingent by essence, necessary by the requirement and entailment of the essence in the Creator, and similarly with respect to the described Necessary, and contingent with respect to contingent essences. If he intends the first, the author's answer is also determined as we have established.

The deniers of homonymy said: If words were coined as homonyms, the purpose of coinage—which is to convey the intended meaning—would be disrupted, because nothing would be understood from them when uttered absolutely. If you say: What do you do with the existing homonymous words? They said: What is thought to be that—i.e., words thought to be homonyms—are either metaphor, coined for one only, or univocal, coined for the common aspect among meanings. We say: We do not concede that homonymy disrupts the purpose; rather, the intended meaning is known through context, so it does not disrupt conveyance. Even if we concede disruption of conveyance as when there is no context, we do not concede that the purpose of coinage is conveyance. How so? For sometimes the goal of uttering a word is obscurity, like the saying of the Caliph of the Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) on the day of migration when a man asked him about the Messenger of Allah: 'Who is with you?' He said: 'A man who guides me on the way.' He meant 'guides me to the path of Allah' and gave the impression that it is a man guiding the road. Obscurity was the intended goal there, as is not hidden. Moreover, this argument does not stand against those who say two meanings are intended together (like the Shafi'is), for with them both meanings are intended, so it does not harm the intended conveyance, because the purpose then is to convey both, not one of them. Nor does it stand against those who say human coinage, because disruption of purpose is not a problem then, since most human actions do not have purposes attached to them. And human coinage is usually the cause of homonymy, for a meaning is coined while ignorant of its coinage for another meaning.

Issue: Clarification. Did homonymy occur in the Quran? There is disagreement: some say no, some say yes. And did it occur in hadith? There is also disagreement. The correct view is that it occurred in the Quran and also in hadith. Our evidence: The Almighty's saying: 'three periods (quru')' [al-Baqara 2:228]—and 'qur'' is for menstruation and purity, as previously mentioned; and His saying: 'And by the night when it departs (ʿasʿasa)' [al-Takweer 81:17]—it means when it approaches and when it goes away; and the Prophet's saying: 'Leave off prayer during your menstrual periods (ayyam aqra'ik)' narrated by al-Tirmidhi. The deniers said: If homonyms occur in the Quran, if they are explained, speech becomes long without benefit, and that undermines eloquence—and the implication is because a single term without homonymy that indicates the intended meaning from the homonym is sufficient and dispenses with the homonym after explanation. On this, the objection of al-Taftazani against the well-known formulation—that explanation suffices so the homonym becomes redundant—does not stand, because it is possible that explanation is obtained from the whole, one part may be lengthy. And if they are unexplained, they are useless for conveying meaning, so they should never occur. We say: We choose the first branch, and we do not concede the implication. Rather, first having ambiguity (ibham) and then its explanation contains eloquence, so it is not without benefit. Also, sometimes there is no single term that conveys the meaning of this homonym when coupled with explanation, so there is no prolongation; rather, it is the necessary way to convey. Also, context (qarina) may be situational (haliyya), so there is no length in wording. We also choose the second branch, and we do not concede that it is useless, for benefit is not necessarily limited to conveying meaning; there are other benefits obtained from the unexplained homonym. How so? The unexplained homonym is useful for prompting thought, etc.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from the third article of Fawatih al-Rahmut discusses the linguistic principle of ishtirak (homonymy) from a Hanafi usul perspective. The author first presents a rational proof for the occurrence of homonymy based on the concept of 'existent' (al-mawjud). The argument is that if 'existent' were univocal between the Necessary Being (Allah) and contingent beings, then the same reality would be both necessary and contingent, which is impossible. Therefore, the term 'existent' must be shared (mushtarak) between different realities. The response refutes this by showing that difference in necessary/contingent does not prevent univocality, as in terms like 'knower' and 'speaker' applied to both Allah and humans. The author then delves into a detailed dialectical exchange, considering various theological positions from Ash'ari, Peripatetic, and other mutakallimun, to show that the proof only works if certain metaphysical assumptions are made. The Hanafi position is that homonymy is possible and actually occurs.

Next, the passage presents the case of the nafun (deniers) of ishtirak. They argue that if words were homonymous, the purpose of language—conveying meaning—would be defeated because no specific meaning would be understood. To the counter that existing homonyms (like 'ayn) exist, they claim such words are either metaphorical or univocal with a common core meaning. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, context (qara'in) resolves ambiguity, so communication is not disrupted. Second, the purpose of coinage is not always clear communication; sometimes obscurity (ibham) is intended, as exemplified by Abu Bakr's ambiguous reply during the Hijra. The Hanafis also note that the argument fails against those who hold that homonyms can be used to convey multiple meanings simultaneously (like the Shafi'is) or against human coinage, which often leads to homonymy without intentionality.

The passage then addresses the specific issue: does homonymy occur in the Quran and hadith? The Hanafi position is that it does, with evidence from the Quranic word 'qur'' (meaning both menstruation and purity) and 'casa' (meaning both approaching and departing), and the hadith 'ayyam aqra'ik' (your menstrual periods). The opponents object that explained homonyms in the Quran would be redundant and undermine eloquence, while unexplained ones are useless. The Hanafis counter that ambiguity followed by explanation has its own eloquence, that sometimes no single non-homonymous term can convey the same nuance, and that situational context (qarina haliyya) can remove ambiguity without extra wording. Moreover, even unexplained homonyms can serve other purposes like prompting inquiry or reflection. This shows the Hanafi commitment to the reality of linguistic homonymy in divine revelation, consistent with their broader usul methodology that values istihsan and the practicality of language.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that is coined for more than one meaning without priority, like 'ayn for eye, spring, etc.
التفهيم (al-tafhim): The purpose of coinage: to convey the intended meaning to the listener.
القرينة (al-qarina): Contextual clue (verbal or situational) that determines the intended meaning of a homonym.
الإبهام (al-ibham): Deliberate ambiguity in speech for a purpose, such as taqiyya or wisdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, what is the main reason homonymy does not disrupt the purpose of language?
A) Because homonyms are rare in Arabic.
B) Because the intended meaning is known through contextual clues.
C) Because the Quran avoids homonyms.
D) Because all homonyms are actually metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms the reality of linguistic homonymy in both human language and divine revelation, relying on context and recognizing that ambiguity can be intentionally employed.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Deniers of Mushtarak and the Hanafi Rebuttal
Presenting and rebutting the argument that mushtarak defeats the purpose of language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the deniers' argument against mushtarak and explain the Hanafi response using qara'in and purposeful ambiguity.

Core Idea
Mushtarak does not impair communication because context clarifies intent, and ambiguity can be intended.

The existence of homonyms (mushtarak) in language has been contested by some scholars who argue that they defeat the purpose of communication. This passage presents their argument and provides a robust Hanafi rebuttal, showing that context and intentional ambiguity preserve the function of language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deniers' claim
Recognize that the deniers argue mushtarak would make clear communication impossible upon absolute utterance.
2
Understand their reasoning
Analyze their premise: the purpose of word-placement (wad') is tafhim (making understood), and if a word has multiple meanings, no specific meaning is conveyed when uttered without context.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli).
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal via qara'in
Learn that context (qara'in) resolves ambiguity, and even without context, the purpose of language is not always tafhim—sometimes ibham (obscurity) is intended, as in Abu Bakr's response.
The example shows that ambiguity can be purposeful.
4
Note additional responses
Observe that the argument does not apply to those who hold mushtarak has generality (Shafi'is) or that it arises from human convention where purpose is not always attached.
These responses strengthen the Hanafi position.

النافون للاشتراك (قالوا : لو وضعت الألفاظ (مشتركة لاختل المقصود) من الوضع (وهو التفهيم) للمراد فإنه لا يفهم منه عند الإطلاق شيء أصلاً، فإن قلت : فما تصنعون بوجود الألفاظ المشتركة؟ قالوا: وما يظن به ذلك) أي الألفاظ التي يظن بها الاشتراك (فإما مجاز) موضوع لواحد فقط (أو متواطىء) موضوع للقدر المشترك بين المعاني (قلنا) لا نسلم إخلال الاشتراك بالمقصود ، بل يعرف المراد بالقرائن فلا يخل بالتفهيم ولو سلم الإخلال بالتفهيم كما عند عدم القرينة فلا نسلم أن المقصود من الوضع التفهيم كيف (وقد يكون الغرض من إطلاق اللفظ الإبهام كقول خليفة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبي بكر الصديق (رضي الله عنه) الهجرة حين سأله رجل عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : من معك؟ (رجل يهديني السبيل) فإنه أراد يهديني سبيل الله وأوهمه أنه رجل يهدي طريق السير، وكان الإبهام هو المقصود هناك كما لا يخفى على أنه لا تنتهض هذه الحجة على من قال بعمومه كالشافعية فإنه عندهم يراد المعنيان فلا يضر التفهيم المقصود، فإن المقصود حينئذ تفهيمهما لا تفهيم أحدهما (ولا) ينتهض أيضاً على قال (بوضع البشر) فإن الإخلال بالمقصود لا خلف فيه حينئذ، فإن أكثر أفعال البشر لا من يترتب عليها أغراضهم منها (وهو) أي وضع البشر (السبب) للاشتراك (غالباً) فإنه يوضع المعنى مع الجهل بوضعه لآخر.
Those who deny mushtarak said: If words were placed as homonyms, the purpose of placing (which is making the intended meaning understood) would be impaired, because nothing at all would be understood from it when uttered absolutely. If you say: What do you do with existing homonyms? They said: Those words which are thought to be homonyms are either metaphorical (assigned to only one meaning) or univocal (assigned to the common denominator between meanings). We said: We do not accept that mushtarak impairs the purpose; rather, the intended meaning is known through contextual clues, so it does not impair understanding. Even if we accept impairment of understanding as when there is no context, we do not accept that the purpose of word-placement is understanding. How so? It may be the purpose of uttering a word to be ambiguous, like the saying of the Caliph of the Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the Hijrah, when a man asked him about the Messenger of Allah: 'Who is with you?' (He said: 'A man who guides me on the way.') He meant 'guides me to the path of Allah' and made him think it was a man guiding the travel route. Ambiguity was intended there. Also, this argument does not stand against those who say mushtarak has generality like the Shafi'is, since according to them both meanings are intended, so the purpose of understanding is not harmed; rather the purpose then is to make both understood, not one of them. Nor does it stand against those who say (it is due to) human word-placement, because then impairment of purpose is not problematic, since most human actions do not have their purposes attached to them. And human word-placement is the cause of mushtarak mostly, for a meaning is placed while unaware of its placement for another.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the existence and validity of mushtarak (homonyms) in language. The deniers argue that if words were homonyms, they would fail their primary purpose of communication (tafhim) because upon utterance no specific meaning would be understood. The author rebuts by stating that context (qara'in) resolves ambiguity, and that sometimes ambiguity itself is intended, as shown by Abu Bakr's response. Furthermore, the argument does not apply to those who hold that mushtarak has generality (like Shafi'is) or that mushtarak arises from human convention where purposelessness is common.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): لفظ وضع لأكثر من معنى حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of those who deny mushtarak?
A) Mushtarak is impossible because it would require multiple contradictory meanings.
B) Mushtarak would impair communication because no specific meaning is understood upon absolute utterance.
C) Mushtarak is rare and can be ignored.
D) Mushtarak only occurs in metaphorical speech.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts mushtarak and relies on context (qara'in) to resolve ambiguity, preserving the communicative function of language.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Does Mushtarak Have General Applicability (Umum)?
Presenting the debate on whether a homonym can apply to all its meanings simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether mushtarak has umum and evaluate the rational proofs for and against.

Core Idea
A homonym cannot apply to all its meanings simultaneously; the mind attends to only one meaning at a time.

This lesson delves into a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: can a single word with multiple meanings (mushtarak) simultaneously indicate all of its meanings? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, argues that it cannot, while the Shafi'i school allows it under certain conditions. We will analyze the rational proofs, explore sub-positions such as the exception in negation, and see how this debate affects real legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is whether a mushtarak (homonym) can have 'umum (general applicability) such that when used, it simultaneously refers to all its meanings.
This is a linguistic/usul question, not a theological one.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proofs
The Hanafi position is that mushtarak does not have umum. The two main proofs: (1) it is impossible to attend to multiple detailed relationships at once, and (2) common usage shows that upon hearing a homonym, the mind waits for a single meaning.
These are rational (aqli) proofs.
3
Analyze the opposing Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools allow umum for mushtarak, arguing it is like a general term (amm). They also debate whether the usage is literal or metaphorical.
The text mentions sub-positions: some allow in dual/plural, some only in negation.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the analogy with amm is flawed because amm applies to each individual, not collectively. Moreover, simultaneous attention is impossible or at least not the default.
Ibn al-Humam's example of the oath on 'mawla' shows the exception in negation.
5
Extract the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference matters in oaths and contracts: if a homonym is used in a positive statement, only one meaning is intended; in negation, some Hanafis allow generality, as seen in the 'mawla' oath example.
This illustrates how a linguistic principle affects real fatwa.

Chapter: Does mushtarak have generality? There is disagreement. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from the Shafi'is, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, and al-Basri, Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, and Abu Hashim, the three from the Mu'tazila, denied it. And the two Imams al-Shafi'i and Malik, and the two judges Abu Bakr al-Baqillani from the Shafi'is, and Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili allowed its generality in non-contrary concepts. It is said in Sharh al-Minhaj that al-Shafi'i stated it in al-Umm. Rather, it is transmitted from al-Shafi'i and al-Baqillani that it must be taken as general unless there is a diverting factor; otherwise it is taken as singular. Among the preventers, some allowed it in the dual and plural, and also among them some allowed it in negation but not affirmation. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam chose in al-Hidaya: if one swears, 'I will not talk to your mawla', and he has higher and lower mawali, and the word mawla is a homonym, whichever he talks to, he breaks his oath, because mushtarak in negation has generality. The locus of disagreement is only in the individual countable sense, meaning it indicates each single one by correspondence, such that each single one is the basis of the ruling in itself, so that the ruling conveyed becomes two rulings. And it is said the locus is the collective meaning: then the ruling is attached to the totality as such, so the ruling does not necessarily apply to each single one. For according to them, mushtarak is like a general word in conveying a ruling to many, except here the many are of different realities and there they are similar; the general word conveys the ruling on the totality, and so does the homonym. This is a fallacy, because the general word also conveys the ruling on each single one, not on the totality, as will become clear. Then there is disagreement about this usage among those who allow it: al-Qarafi and Ibn al-Hajib said it is metaphorical because the word was for one and then used for two. And it is transmitted from al-Shafi'i and the judge, and Imam al-Ghazali held that it is literal because it is used for each single one, which is what it was placed for, since it is the basis of the ruling; the most that can be said is that it is used in two usages. Yes, if used for the totality as such, it would be metaphorical. Our proof against permissibility: First, it would require the mind's attention to two relationships in one instant in detail, as the placement and usage are present for both, and there is no preponderance for one over the other, so both are understood together; but attending to two relationships in one instant is impossible. This is insufficient, because there may be an external preponderant factor like frequent use of one, etc. Moreover, no proof has been established for the impossibility of attending to two relationships; it is not self-evident. One could even argue for its occurrence through intuition, where principles are observed in detailed order at once and then one moves to the desired conclusion. And our second proof: the immediate understanding is one specific meaning, as evidenced by common correct usage; when a homonym is uttered, the mind awaits to know which meaning is intended. Denying this is obstinacy.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether a mushtarak (homonym) can have general applicability (umum) to all its meanings simultaneously. Abu Hanifa and many others deny this, while Shafi'i and Malik allow it. The Hanafi argument is based on the impossibility of the mind attending to multiple relationships at once, and on the fact that common usage of homonyms immediately calls for a single specific meaning. The text also discusses sub-positions (allowing in dual/plural, or in negation) and the nature of the usage (metaphorical or literal).

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): لفظ له معنيان أو أكثر بينهما تباعد (homonym)
عموم (umum): شمول اللفظ لجميع أفراده أو معانيه (generality)
عام (amm): لفظ يستغرق جميع أفراده بدون حصر (general term)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on whether mushtarak has 'umum?
A) Mushtarak always has 'umum.
B) Mushtarak does not have 'umum; it indicates one meaning at a time.
C) Mushtarak has 'umum only in negation.
D) Mushtarak has 'umum only for dual and plural.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school denies that mushtarak can apply to all its meanings simultaneously; only one meaning is intended unless context or negation requires otherwise.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Generality of Homonyms (Umum al-Mushtarak): Hanafi Position
Dispute over generality of homonyms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on umum al-mushtarak and identify the key arguments distinguishing it from the opposing view.

Core Idea
A homonym cannot denote all meanings at once – the mind cannot attend to two detailed relationships simultaneously.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: can a word with multiple distinct meanings (mushtarak) be used to refer to all of those meanings at once, i.e., have generality (umum)? The Hanafi school firmly denies this, while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools permit it under certain conditions. Through this passage, we will unpack the Hanafi rational and linguistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: does a mushtarak have 'umum that covers all its conceptually distinct meanings? Recognise that this is a dispute over linguistic capacity and the mental operation of understanding.
Disagreement arises because 'umum normally applies to amm (general) words with similar instances, but mushtarak has heterogeneous meanings.
2
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa, al-Karkhi, and others hold that a mushtarak cannot have 'umum. It is impossible to intend all meanings simultaneously due to mental limitation.
Exception: some Hanafis (Ibn al-Humam) allow it in the context of negation (nafi), but the text clarifies this is not true 'umum of the homonym.
3
Examine the Hanafi arguments
First argument: the mind cannot simultaneously attend to two detailed significations (al-nisbatayn al-mulahuazatayn tafseelan) without a preponderant factor. Second argument: empirical linguistic usage – when a mushtarak is uttered, the mind waits to determine which meaning is intended; this shows that one meaning is the default.
The passage dismantles the opposing claim that the word is 'put for each meaning absolutely' (wad' li-kulli mutlaqan) by showing that actual usage requires singling out one meaning.
4
Evaluate opposing proof and Hanafi response
Opponents cite Quranic verses like 'yasjudu' (al-Hajj 22:18) and 'yusalluna' (al-Ahzab 33:56), arguing that the same verb applies to different beings with different meanings. Hanafi response: the verb is used in its core sense (e.g., ultimate humility) that applies to all; it is not used in multiple distinct meanings simultaneously. This is a single meaning with diverse manifestations.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of preserving lexical unity while acknowledging contextual variation.

للآخر فلا يجوز الاستعمال في كل منهما، وسقط عنه ما قال في التلويح إنه ليس المراد بالتخصيص ههنا أن اللفظ له لا لغيره حتى يلزم التنافي بل المراد أن المعنى إنما هو مخصوص بهذا اللفظ من بين الألفاظ لا بغيره لكل مطلقاً) أي من غير لحاظ أن يكون مع الآخر أو بدونه فإذا قصد الكل كان الاستعمال (فيما وضع) المشترك (له) فكان حقيقة (وذلك) الاندفاع (لأن الوضع لا يكفي للحقيقة، بل يجب الاستعمال لها حتى لو وضع اللفظ ولم يستعمل أصلاً لم يكن حقيقة (ومن شرطه عدم الجمع فلو استعمل فيهما كان خطأ لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً واندفع أيضاً ما أورد في «شرح الشرح» أنه لو تبادر أحدهما كان متواطئاً لا مشتركاً، وذلك لأنه ليس يتبادر المفهوم المردد الشامل لهما، بل يتبادر كل بدلاً بأن يكون هذا هو المراد أو ذلك فافهم، واندفع أيضاً ما قيل في «شرح المختصر أن اللفظ كان لواحد واحد فإذا أريد الكل كان كل جزء لما استعمل فيه فيكون مجازاً، كيف لا واللفظ كان لواحد واحد، وأريد الآن هو مع الغير، وذلك لأن الكلام في إرادة كل بحيث يكون مناط الحكم أولاً، وبالذات كل واحد واحد لا الكل بما هو الكل، حتى يكون كل جزء مراداً ويكون مجازاً ،البتة وإرادة الكل من قبيل إطلاق الجزء على الكل إنما يصح إذا كان الكل بحيث يكون له اسم على حدة ويكون بحيث ينتفي بانتفاء الجزء عرفاً، هذا وقال الشيخ ابن الهمام بتجويز العموم في النفي أنه يراد كل ما هو مسمى بهذا الاسم، فيعم في النفي، لأن المبهم إذا ورد عليه النفي يعم، ولا يخفى أنه ليس حينئذ من عموم المشترك في شيء، بل إرادة معنى مجازي واقع تحت النفي، فيعم ولعل هذا هو مراد صاحب «الهداية»، وحينئذ فلا نزاع المجوّزون (قالوا: قال الله تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ تَرَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ يَسْجُدُ لَهُ مَن فِي
،
السَّمَوَاتِ وَمَن فِي الْأَرْضِ وَالشَّمْسُ وَالْقَمَرُ وَالنُّجُومُ وَالْجِبَالُ وَالشَّجَرُ وَالدَّوَاتُ وَكَثِيرٌ مِّنَ النَّاسِ [الحج: (۱۸] (الآية، والسجود من الناس وضع الجبهة على الأرض، ومن غيرهم غيره) وهو الخضوع القهري، وقد أريدا من لفظ يسجد لأنه أسند إليهم جميعاً (وأيضاً) قال الله تعالى : ( إنَّ اللهَ وَمَلَيْكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيُّ ) [الأحزاب: ٥٦] وهي من الله رحمة ومن الملائكة استغفار) وقد أريد أيضاً لفظ يصلون لأنه أسند إلى الله تعالى وإلى الملائكة (والجواب) عن الأول من (أن السجود) حقيقة غاية الخضوع، وهو في الإنسان يتحقق بوضع الجبهة اختياراً، فإنه غاية الخضوع في ذي الجبهة المختار ( وفي غيره) أي غير الإنسان (بغيره) أي بغير وضع الجبهة بل
The third article on linguistic principles. ... Issue: Does a homonym (mushtarak) have generality (umum)? There is disagreement about it. Abu Hanifa and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (of the Shafi'is) and Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (of us) and al-Basri and Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim (the three Mu'tazila) prevented it. Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik and the two judges Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (Shafi'is) and Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili permitted it regarding its non-contradictory meanings...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul al-fiqh dispute over 'umum al-mushtarak (whether a word with multiple distinct meanings can be used to refer to all of them at once in a general sense). The Hanafi position (as per Abu Hanifa, al-Karkhi, etc.) is that it cannot, because the mind cannot simultaneously attend to two different significations without a preponderant factor. The opposing view (Shafi'i, Maliki, etc.) holds that it is possible, especially in cases of negation. The text then presents the Hanafi arguments: first, the impossibility of simultaneous mental attention to two detailed relationships; second, the common usage that a homonym always requires context to determine which meaning is intended. It concludes that using a homonym for all its meanings at once is not valid even rarely, because the condition of usage (qasd one meaning) is absent. The exception mentioned is the case of negation, where Ibn al-Humam allowed generality, but the text clarifies that this is not true 'umum of the homonym but rather a metaphorical extension. Finally, the opponents cite Quranic verses where the same verb (e.g., yasjudu, yusalluna) applies to different beings with different meanings; the Hanafi response is that the verb is used in its core meaning (ultimate humility) which applies to all, not that it is used in multiple distinct meanings simultaneously.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that has multiple distinct meanings (e.g., 'ayn meaning eye, spring, etc.)
العموم (al-umum): The quality of a word covering all individuals or meanings of its category
الاستعمال (al-isti'mal): Using a word in a particular meaning in speech

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Hanafi argument is used to reject the generality of homonyms (umum al-mushtarak)?
A) The word is not 'amm because it is not derived from a verb.
B) The mind cannot simultaneously attend to two detailed significations.
C) The Quranic verses cited by opponents are abrogated.
D) Homonyms do not exist in the Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school definitively rejects 'umum al-mushtarak based on mental limitation and linguistic convention; a homonym without context cannot signify all its meanings at once.

Four Madhahib on Metaphorical Predication (Isnad)
Analyzing four scholarly positions on metaphorical predication in 'the spring made the herbage grow'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four positions on metaphorical predication and apply the preferred Hanafi-aligned view to analyze figurative language in legal and Quranic texts.

Core Idea
The metaphor in 'spring made the herbage grow' is in the predication, not in the words – a key principle for interpreting legal language.

This lesson explores a classic debate in usul al-fiqh: when we say a sentence like 'the spring made the herbage grow,' which part is metaphorical? Four schools of thought emerged among scholars of eloquence and usul, each locating the metaphor in a different component. Understanding this dispute is crucial for interpreting figurative language in the Quran, Hadith, and juristic texts, as it affects how muftis derive rulings from metaphorical expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
List the four scholars (Ibn al-Hajib, al-Sakkaki, 'Abd al-Qahir, al-Razi) and where each locates the metaphor: predicate, subject, predication, or meaning.
Memorize the names and their views.
2
Understand each position's evidence
For each, identify the key argument: e.g., Ibn al-Hajib says verb implies capable agent; al-Sakkaki uses isti'ara bil-kinaya; 'Abd al-Qahir emphasizes uniformity of predication; al-Razi transfers meaning to real agent.
Focus on the reasoning, not just the conclusion.
3
Examine the refutations
Analyze why the first, second, and fourth views are criticized: Ibn al-Hajib's view assumes more than the verb's meaning; al-Sakkaki's view makes subject not metaphorical; al-Razi's view borders on metonymy.
The refutations rely on linguistic analysis, not just authority.
4
Analyze the preferred view
Study the third view ('Abd al-Qahir) that metaphor is in the predication. Understand why it is 'closest to correctness' – it respects literal meanings while allowing figurative attribution.
This view is widely accepted by Hanafi scholars.
5
Apply to a legal text
Consider a juristic statement like 'the hand cut it.' Using the preferred view, explain where the metaphor lies and why it matters for legal interpretation.
This step connects to fatwa practice.

من
قد اختلف في نحو : أنبت الربيع (البقل) أي فيما إذا أسند المسند إلى ما حقه أن لا يسند إليه على أربعة مذاهب الأول أنه مجاز في المسند فإنه أريد به غير الموضوع له (وهو التسبب العادي مثلاً وإن كان وضعه للتسبب الحقيقي وذلك قول ابن الحاجب) وقرر بأن الفعل يدخل في مفهومه النسبة إلى الفاعل القادر فإذا أسند إلى غير القادر يكون مجازاً البتة (ورد) بما اتفق عليه علماء البيان من أن الفعل لا يدل بحسب أصل الوضع على أن فاعله يلزم أن يكون قادراً أو غير قادر سبباً حقيقياً أو سبباً (غير حقيقي) فإن الفعل إنما أخذ في مفهومه النسبة إلى فاعل ما لا إلى الفاعل القادر وإذا كان الفاعل أعم من المختار وغيره والسبب الحقيقي وغيره فليس هناك تسبب حقيقي هو مدلول الفعل حتى يكون الانتقال إلى التسبب العادي مجازاً ورد أيضاً بأن من الأفعال ما ليس إسناده إلى الفاعل المختار فيلزم حينئذ أن تكون هذه الأفعال مجازات والتزامه بعيد كل البعد ورد أيضاً بأن الحكم بدخول النسبة إلى الفاعل القادر لوجود بعض الأفعال مسندة إليه ليس أولى من العكس، ثم اعلم أن الخطأ المترجمين في تقرير كلامه ومرامه ،مصون عن هذه الشناعات فإنه لم يرد أن في مدلول الفعل النسبة إلى القادر بل مراده أنه لما صدر ممن لا يعتقد ظاهره عرف أن فيه تأويلاً، فأوّل هو في المسند، وحكم بأن المراد منه ما يصلح لأن يسند إلى المذكور وههنا المذكور الإنبات وهو في اللغة والعرف خلق النبات فتجوّز عن التهيؤ والاستعداد له وهو التسبب العادي، وعلى هذا القياس يؤوّل في كل مثال ما يليق به وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء فافهم وهو الذي اختاره الجونفوري في تحقيق كلامه في الفرائد ولعل المصنف إلى هذا أشار بقوله: (فتأمل الثاني أنه) أي التجوز (في المسند إليه الذي هو الربيع وهو قول السكاكي إنه استعارة بالكناية) وهي عنده ذكر أحد طرفي التشبيه، وإرادة الآخر بادعاء أنه من جنسه فههنا شبه الربيع بالقادر المختار في تلبس الإنبات في الفعل وذكر الربيع وأريد به القادر المختار بادعاء أن الربيع قادر مختار، لا أنه أريد به قادر غير الربيع فالمقصود بالذات تشبيه الربيع بالقادر، ونسبة الإنبات قرينة عليه وقال السكاكي إن هذا النحو مغن عن القول بالإسناد المجازي، فهو الأولى، فيكون أقرب إلى الضبط وأورد أنه لا يكون (مغنياً عن المجاز العقلي : (كما زعمه)
و
وقد اختلف في نحو: أنبت الربيع البقل أي فيما إذا أسند المسند إلى ما حقه أن لا يسند إليه على أربعة مذاهب الأول أنه مجاز في المسند فإنه أريد به غير الموضوع له وهو التسبب العادي مثلاً وإن كان وضعه للتسبب الحقيقي وذلك قول ابن الحاجب... الثاني أنه التجوز في المسند إليه الذي هو الربيع وهو قول السكاكي إنه استعارة بالكناية... الثالث أنه تجوز في الإسناد والربيع على معناه وكذا الإنبات، والمتكلم شبه الربيع بفاعله في التلبس، فأسند إليه الإنبات إسناداً مجازياً لمبالغة في التلبس وهذا قول الشيخ عبد القاهر وغيره من المحققين من علماء البيان وهو الأقرب إلى الصواب... الرابع: قول الإمام الرازي وهو أنه أي التجوز في المعنى فقط، والأجزاء باقية على حقائقها التي في العرف واللغة وذلك بأن ينتقل من إنبات الربيع إلى إنبات الله تعالى فيصدق به ويعلم أن النقل أي نقل الكلام من إسناد الإنبات إلى الله تعالى إلى الإسناد إلى الربيع للمبالغة فتدبر
The third article on linguistic principles: a word is assigned by personal specification, and must it be used in that meaning? There is discussion. For there is no specificity for personal specification; do you not see that they used 'al-Rahman' and 'asa' while they are assigned by generic specification? Rather, derivatives and verbs exit from the discussion. The obligators said: if metaphor did not entail reality, the benefit of specification would be lost, which is conveying the compositional meaning at the time of use. Since there is no use, there is no conveyance. We said: the entailment is denied, for the loss of a particular benefit does not necessitate absolute loss, for the validity of metaphorical usage is among the benefits and it is not lost. It was said: the falsity of the consequent is denied, for there is no impossibility in the loss of benefit. I say: if the assigner is Allah Almighty as is apparent, then the falsity (i.e., the falsity of loss of benefit) is apparent. An issue: There is disagreement about examples like 'the spring made the herbage grow' – i.e., when the predicate is predicated of what it should not be predicated of – among four schools. The first: it is a metaphor in the predicate, for it is intended for other than its original meaning, (like ordinary causation, although its original meaning is for real causation). This is the view of Ibn al-Hajib, and he argued that the verb includes in its concept the relation to a capable agent, so if it is predicated of what is not capable, it is definitely a metaphor. It was refuted by what the scholars of eloquence have agreed upon: that the verb does not, by virtue of original assignment, indicate that its agent must be capable or incapable, a real or non-real cause, for the verb only includes in its concept the relation to some agent, not to a capable agent. And if the agent includes the voluntary and other, and real cause and other, then there is no real causation that is the meaning of the verb such that moving to ordinary causation would be a metaphor. It was also refuted that there are verbs whose predication is not to a voluntary agent, then these verbs would have to be metaphors, which is far-fetched. It was also refuted that the judgment that the relation to a capable agent is included due to some verbs being predicated of it is not more appropriate than the opposite. Then know that the translators' error in stating his speech and purport is protected from these objections, for he did not intend that in the meaning of the verb is the relation to the capable, but rather his intention is that when it issues from one who does not believe its apparent meaning, it is known that there is interpretation, so he interpreted in the predicate, and judged that the intended is what is suitable to be predicated of the mentioned. Here the mentioned is the growth, and in language and custom it is the creation of plants, so he metaphorically used it for readiness and preparation, which is ordinary causation. By analogy, in every example he interprets what is suitable, and upon this nothing is refuted against him. Understand, and this is what al-Jawnpuri chose in his verification of his words in al-Fara'id, and perhaps the author referred to this by his saying 'ponder'. The second: that the metaphor is in the subject, which is the spring, and it is the view of al-Sakkaki that it is a metonymical metaphor. According to him, it is mentioning one of the two sides of the similitude and intending the other by claiming that it is of its kind. So here, the spring is compared to the capable voluntary agent in its performance of the act of growth, and the spring is mentioned and the capable voluntary agent is intended by claiming that the spring is capable and voluntary, not that a capable other than the spring is intended. So the primary object is the comparison of the spring to the capable, and the attribution of growth is a clue to it. Al-Sakkaki said that this method suffices from the claim of metaphorical predication, so it is preferable, being closer to exactness. It was objected that it does not suffice for the intellectual metaphor as he claimed. And it was also objected that it is not a metaphor in the subject because it is used in its literal meaning, and only a false claim occurs, which does not make the word metaphorical, while he judged that there is metaphor in it. The third: that the metaphor is in the predication, while the spring and the growth remain in their literal meanings, and the speaker likened the spring to its agent in performance, so he attributed the growth to it in a metaphorical attribution for hyperbole in the performance. This is the view of Shaykh 'Abd al-Qahir and other scholars of eloquence, and it is the closest to correctness. For one who traces the usages of eloquent speakers and consults his natural judgment finds this meaning appropriate. Ibn al-Hajib's dismissal due to the uniformity of the aspect of predication in all constructions in custom and language, thus deeming some predications metaphorical and others not, is arbitrary, lacking a clear distinction between 'Zaid fasted' and 'his day fasted'. For one knows necessarily that the first is in its proper place while the second is not. The solution is that every predication has a right in language and custom to occur in its proper place, which is the thing to which this predicate is attributable. So if it is moved from its proper place to what is related to it, it becomes definitely a metaphor. Know that the commentator of al-Mukhtasar in Sharh al-Mukhtasar and al-Fawa'id al-Ghiyathiyya stated the view of Shaykh 'Abd al-Qahir that there is interpretation in the construction, for the compositional form of 'his day fasted' is assigned for the performance of the verb by the agent. So if it is used and intended for occurrence in the time, it becomes definitely a metaphor. Thus the aspect of predication in 'Zaid fasted' and 'his day fasted' is not one, for the compositional form in the first is used for its assigned meaning unlike the second. So upon this, the apparent view is that the metaphor is then a representational metaphor. Then this type of interpretation, though possible, is not sound as the view of Shaykh 'Abd al-Qahir. Rather, what reliable scholars have stated is the interpretation only in the predication, while words and forms remain in their meanings. Understand. The fourth: the view of Imam al-Razi, which is that the metaphor is only in the meaning, while the parts remain in their literal meanings in custom and language, by transferring from the spring's growing to Allah's growing, affirming it, and knowing that the transference is for hyperbole. So reflect. Its clarification: the individual words and the predication which are parts of the speech remain in their literal meanings, but the primary object is not the judgment conveyed by them per se, but rather to move from it to the judgment of the predicate upon its real agent, done for hyperbole. This differs from the third view where both sides are literal and the predication is metaphorical, and the primary object is this metaphorical predication. Thus it does not differ much from metonymy. It was distinguished in al-Fawa'id by saying: In metonymy, the necessary becomes an indicator and expression for the necessitated, as 'long-sword-belt' indicates tall stature, unlike here where nothing is an indicator for the primary object per se. This is not a significant difference, since the lack of indicator here is because what is transferred from is a complete sentence, and similarly what is transferred to is an independent judgment, and an independent judgment cannot be an indicator for another independent judgment, but the method of transference is the same. There is another aspect here: comparing the form resulting from the occurrence of growth in the spring to the compositional form from the growth of the agent, thus expressing with the expression assigned for the second about the first, and this is the representational metaphor. Upon this, Shaykh Ibn al-Humam interpreted his speech, but the author did not approve and said: 'And what is in al-Tahrir'...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a key linguistic issue in usul al-fiqh: when a sentence like 'the spring made the herbage grow' is used, which component is metaphorical? The author presents four madhahib (schools). First, Ibn al-Hajib held that the metaphor is in the predicate (the verb 'grew'), because he assumed verbs imply a capable agent. But scholars of eloquence refuted this because verbs are assigned only to some agent, not necessarily capable. The author notes that the translator of Ibn al-Hajib's view clarified that he actually meant that a metaphorical interpretation is needed when the apparent meaning is unlikely. Second, al-Sakkaki held that the metaphor is in the subject 'spring' by way of metonymical metaphor (isti‘ara bi al-kinaya): the spring is compared to a capable agent, and the predicate 'growth' is a clue. However, this was criticized because the word 'spring' is used literally, and a mere claim does not make it metaphorical. Third, 'Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani and most scholars hold that the metaphor is in the predication (isnad) itself: the spring and growth are used literally, but their combination is metaphorical for hyperbole. This is the preferred view because it respects the uniformity of predication in language while recognizing metaphorical usage only when the predicate is not in its proper place. The author supports this as the closest to correctness. Fourth, Imam al-Razi opines that the metaphor is in the meaning alone: the entire sentence ''the spring grew' is used to refer to Allah's action with hyperbole, while all parts remain literal. This is similar to metonymy but not exactly the same. The author notes that Shaykh Ibn al-Humam interpreted al-Razi's view in terms of representational metaphor (isti‘ara tamthiliyya), but the author of the main text (al-Musallam al-Thubut) does not approve. The passage thus clarifies the nuanced differences between these positions and the reasons for preferring the third.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used in other than its assigned meaning due to a relation.
إسناد (isnad): The predication of a verb or attribute to a subject; the relation between subject and predicate.
استعارة بالكناية (isti‘ara bil-kinaya): A metaphor where one side of a comparison is mentioned and the other side is intended by a claim of identity.
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning; the meaning for which the word was originally assigned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, where does the metaphor lie in the sentence 'the spring made the herbage grow'?
A) In the predicate (growing)
B) In the subject (spring)
C) In the predication (the relation between subject and predicate)
D) In the meaning alone (transference to Allah)

Key Takeaway
Metaphor in language can occur not only in individual words but also in the predication (isnad), a key principle for interpreting figurative expressions in legal texts.

The Hanafi Preference for Metaphor over Homonymy in Usul al-Fiqh
Debate on homonymy vs. metaphor with Hanafi preference for metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers metaphor (majaz) over homonymy (ishtirak) by analyzing the arguments and counterarguments presented in the text.

Core Idea
Metaphor is preferred over homonymy because it produces stronger conjecture (zann) through contextual indicators.

In this passage from Fawātiḥ al-Raḥamūt, we encounter a lively debate between advocates of homonymy (ishtirāk) and metaphor (majāz) in the Arabic language. The author systematically responds to objections raised by proponents of homonymy, ultimately concluding that metaphor is preferable. This conclusion hinges on the concept of qarīnah (contextual indicator) and the epistemic strength of the resulting conjecture. Understanding this debate is crucial for applying Hanafi principles of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main dispute
The central question: which linguistic phenomenon—homonymy or metaphor—leads to more reliable interpretation of texts? The passage presents arguments for both sides.
The debate is about which is epistemically superior, not about the existence of either phenomenon.
2
Summarize the objections against homonymy
The text notes that homonymy, without a contextual indicator, disrupts understanding because the addressee cannot pick the intended meaning. Metaphor requires a qarīnah, so when absent, the literal meaning is safe.
Objection: homonymy leads to improbable combinations like opposites; metaphor treats opposition as analogy, avoiding improbability.
3
Analyze the counterarguments for homonymy
Proponents of homonymy claim it is regular (muṭṭarid), derivable, permits metaphorical usage, is more frequent, and avoids error when no context exists. The passage refutes or qualifies each claim.
The author points out that derivation is shared between literal and metaphorical meanings, so expansion remains ambiguous.
4
Understand the Hanafi conclusion
The decisive factor: the conjecture (ẓann) obtained from metaphorical usage is stronger because it relies on a preponderance of probability (ghalabat al-mannat). This makes metaphor preferable for deriving legal rulings.
The final argument: 'الظن الحاصل بغلبة المئنة أقوى مما سواه والغلبة في المجاز فهو أولى'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخاطب يحمل عند عدم القرينة على خلاف المراد فيخل بالفهم، وعند القرينة لا اختلال في شيء منهما، والجواب أن القرينة شرط استعمال المجاز، فإذا عدمت تعينت الحقيقة للإرادة وأما المشترك فلا يشترط القرينة فيخل بالفهم هذا (وأنه) أي المشترك (يؤدي إلى مستبعد) وهو الاشتراك بين المتضادين أو إلى حكم أحد الضدين على الآخر عند خفاء القرينة (بخلاف المجاز فإن التضاد مع كونه أقل نزل منزلة التناسب فلا استبعاد وأورد على التوجيه الأول أن نفس وقوع الاشتراك بين الضدين لا استبعاد فيه، وعلى الثاني أن المجاز فيه أيضاً إذا كان باعتبار التضاد وإن اعتبر تماثلاً ينتقل إلى الحقيقة عند خفاء القرينة فيحكم عليه بحكم حكم به على المجازي المضاد له فتدبر وعورض بأن المشترك يطرد لأنه حقيقة، والاطراد من لوازمه فلا يضطرب، و بأن المشترك (يشتق منه نظراً إلى المعنيين (فيتسع الكلام وفيه أن الاشتقاق مشترك بين المعنى الوضعي والمجازي، فالاتساع بحاله فتدبر (و) بأن المشترك (يصح التجوز منه لكونه حقيقة فتكثر الفائدة باعتبار إفادة المعنى بطرق شتى (و) بأن المشترك أكثر وقوعاً، كيف وأنه مستغن) في الوجود عن العلاقة) فهو أقل مقدمات والأقل مقدمات أسبق وقوعاً، و) أنه مستغن عن الغلط عند عدم القرينة فيتوقف والأولى عند خفاء القرينة، لأن استعمال المجاز من غير قرينة لا يجوز (قلنا) لا تعارض فيما ذكرنا وفيما ذكرتم إذ (الظن) الحاصل (بغلبة المئنة أقوى مما سواه والغلبة في المجاز فهو أولى.
The addressee, in the absence of a contextual indicator, interprets contrary to the intended meaning, thus disrupting understanding; with the indicator, there is no disruption in either. The answer is that the contextual indicator is a condition for using metaphor; when it is absent, the literal meaning is determined for intent. As for the homonym, it does not require a contextual indicator, so it disrupts understanding. This (and that it) namely the homonym (leads to something improbable), that is, homonymy between opposites or to judging one opposite by the other when the contextual indicator is unclear. (Unlike metaphor, for opposition, despite being less, is treated as analogy, so there is no improbability). An objection was raised to the first explanation that the mere occurrence of homonymy between opposites is not improbable; and to the second that metaphor also, if based on opposition, and even if similarity is considered, shifts to the literal meaning when the indicator is unclear, so it is judged by the same judgment as the opposed metaphorical meaning, so reflect. It was objected that the homonym is regular because it is literal, and regularity is one of its necessary attributes, so it is not inconsistent. And that the homonym (can be derived from it with respect to both meanings) so speech is expanded. There is in it that derivation is shared between the literal and metaphorical meaning, so expansion remains as it is, so reflect. (And) that (the homonym permits metaphorical usage because it is literal, so benefit increases by conveying meaning through various paths). (And) that the homonym is more frequent; moreover, it is independent) in existence from a relation) so it has fewer premises, and fewer premises are prior in occurrence. (And) that it is free from error when there is no contextual indicator, so it stops. The better is when the indicator is unclear, because using metaphor without an indicator is not permissible. (We said) there is no conflict in what we mentioned and what you mentioned, for (the conjecture) obtained (by the preponderance of probability is stronger than other than it, and the preponderance is in metaphor, so it is preferable.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage continues a debate about whether homonymy (ishtirak) or metaphor (majaz) is more problematic in language. Opponents argue homonymy causes confusion without context, while metaphor requires context but is safer. The text responds to various objections: that homonymy leads to improbable combinations like opposites, that metaphor when based on opposition can revert to literal meaning, that homonymy is regular and derivable, and that it is more frequent. The final argument says that the probability from metaphorical usage is stronger, so metaphor is preferable. The Hanafi usul position favors majaz over ishtirak for clarity and probability.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that has multiple literal meanings (homonym).
المجاز (al-majaz): Use of a word in a non-literal sense due to a relation (metaphor).
القرينة (al-qarīnah): A contextual indicator that signals the intended meaning (literal or metaphorical).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the text conclude that metaphor (majaz) is preferable to homonymy (ishtirak)?
A) Because metaphor requires a contextual indicator, which ensures clarity.
B) Because homonymy is not found in Arabic.
C) Because metaphor is more frequent than homonymy.
D) Because homonymy leads to improbable opposites.

Key Takeaway
Metaphor (majaz) is preferred over homonymy (ishtirak) in Hanafi usul because it yields stronger conjecture (zann) and avoids confusion through contextual indicators.

Hierarchy of Interpretive Preferences in Hanafi Usul
Hanafi hierarchy of interpretive preferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi hierarchy of interpretive preferences from most preferred to least preferred and apply it to resolve conflicts in textual interpretation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, avoid drastic interpretations: specification (takhsīṣ) best, abrogation (naskh) worst.

When a text admits multiple possible meanings, Hanafi usul provides a clear hierarchy of which interpretive move to prefer. This hierarchy prioritises adjustments that preserve the original wording (like specification) over those that change it (like transposition or abrogation). Understanding this ladder helps us derive rulings that are most faithful to the language of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the order from best to worst
Start by memorising the sequence: Specification (takhsīṣ) > Metaphor (majāz) = Ellipsis (iḍmār) > Transposition (naql) > Homonymy (ishtirāk) > Abrogation (naskh). The text gives three explicit comparisons: (a) naql, iḍmār, and takhsīṣ are better than ishtirāk; (b) majāz, iḍmār, and takhsīṣ are better than naql; (c) ishtirāk is better than naskh.
The text also divides ishtirāk into sub‑types: between two names > between a name and a meaning > between two meanings.
2
Understand the criterion: frequency (akthariyya)
The underlying principle is ‘al-wajh al-akthariyya’ – the more frequent and less disruptive interpretation is preferred. Takhsīṣ only restricts, naskh abrogates entirely, so the latter is least frequent.
Majāz and iḍmār are equal in occurrence, hence they are tied.
3
Apply the hierarchy to a sample text
Imagine a verse that contains a general term (ʿāmm) and a possible figurative meaning. According to the hierarchy, we first try takhsīṣ (specify the general), then if impossible, try majāz or iḍmār, then naql, then ishtirāk, and only as a last resort naskh.
This order prevents hasty resort to abrogation, which would nullify previous rulings.
4
Connect to chapter of ʿāmm and khāṣṣ
This hierarchy directly applies in the chapter of ʿāmm and khāṣṣ: when a general text conflicts with a specific proof, we prefer takhsīṣ over any other resolution because it is the least drastic.
It also relates to the order of tarjīḥ among conflicting evidence (al‑taʿāruḍ wa al‑tarjīḥ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة النقل والإضمار والتخصيص أولى من الاشتراك، والمجاز والإضمار والتخصيص أولى من النقل لما مر من الوجه والمجاز مثل الإضمار) لتساويهما في الوقوع، فلو احتملا فهما متساويان وخير منه أي من المجاز التخصيص فالتخصيص خير من الإضمار لأنه مثل المجاز والاشتراك خير من النسخ لأن النسخ أقل منه وكذا الاشتراك بين علمين خير منه بين علم ومعنى وهو خير منه بين معنيين كذا قالوا والوجه الأكثرية .
Appendix: Transposition, ellipsis, and specification are preferable to homonymy. Metaphor, ellipsis, and specification are preferable to transposition due to the reason mentioned earlier. Metaphor is like ellipsis because they are equal in occurrence; if both are possible, they are equal. Specification is better than metaphor, so specification is better than ellipsis because it is like metaphor. Homonymy is better than abrogation because abrogation is less frequent. Likewise, homonymy between two names is better than between a name and a meaning, and that is better than between two meanings. Thus they said, and the aspect is majority.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This appendix (tatimma) provides a hierarchy of preference when multiple linguistic alternatives are possible. The order is: 1) Specification (takhsis) is best. 2) Metaphor (majaz) and ellipsis (idmar) are equal and second. 3) Transposition (naql) comes next. 4) Homonymy (ishtirak) follows. 5) Abrogation (naskh) is least preferred. The criterion is frequency and avoidance of disruption. This reflects Hanafi usul principles for interpreting ambiguous texts: prefer meanings that are more common and less drastic.

Key Terms
النقل (an-naql): Changing the meaning of a word from its original to another (transposition).
الإضمار (al-idmār): Ellipsis, where a word is omitted but intended.
التخصيص (at-takhṣīṣ): Restricting a general term to specific instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi hierarchy, which is better: homonymy (ishtirāk) or abrogation (naskh)?
A) Homonymy is better.
B) Abrogation is better.
C) They are equal.
D) Neither is better.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the hierarchy of interpretive preferences (takhsīṣ > majāz = iḍmār > naql > ishtirāk > naskh) ensures that the least disruptive interpretation is always tried first.
