Metaphor's Generality: The Hanafi Rule on Majaz and Umum
Establishes the rule that majaz has umum when attached to linguistic markers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that metaphorical expressions can indicate generality (umum) when attached to linguistic markers like the definite article, idafa, or negation, and evaluate the practical consequence in the riba dispute over measured goods.

Core Idea
Metaphor can indicate generality just like literal expressions when it carries markers like the definite article.

This lesson examines a key rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: metaphorical expressions (majaz) are not automatically limited in scope. When they are attached to linguistic markers such as the definite article (al-), genitive construction (idafa), or negation (nafy), they can convey generality (umum) just like literal expressions. We will explore how this rule is applied to the prophetic hadith on riba and how it shapes the Hanafi position that riba applies to all measured commodities, not just foodstuffs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its components
Read the opening statement: 'المجاز يعم إذا لحق به موجب له كاللام والإضافة والوقوع تحت النفي كالحقيقة'. Understand that metaphor (majaz) has generality (umum) when a cause (mujib) for generality is attached, such as the definite article (lam), idafa, or falling under negation. The metaphor behaves like the literal (haqiqah) in this respect.
Note that the term 'موجب' refers to a linguistic marker that necessitates generality.
2
Examine the evidence: the hadith
The hadith 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين' contains the definite articles 'الدرهم' and 'الصاع'. Even though 'الصاع' here is used metaphorically (representing the measured commodity), the definite article indicates generality (istighraq). Thus the prohibition covers all dirhams and all sā' measures, leading to the ruling that riba applies to all measured items (makilat), including non-edibles like lime (jass).
The Hanafis consider the definite article to be a clear indicator of generality, overriding any limited understanding of the metaphor.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Some Shafi'is argue that metaphor is only used out of necessity (because a literal term is unavailable). Necessity is measured by its extent, so the metaphor should be restricted to the specific items mentioned, not extended to all measured items. Generality is an unnecessary addition.
The Shafi'i view on riba restricts it to food items based on the 'illah of taste (ta'm), citing other evidence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) The claim that metaphor is necessary is denied (mumtani'), because metaphor occurs even in divine speech, which is free from necessity. (2) Even if necessity existed, it does not preclude generality because the definite article itself is a clear evidence of generality; in that respect, the expression is literal regarding its indication of generality. So generality is established by evidence, not by necessity.
The Hanafi argument preserves the generality of the hadith, making it a definitive (qat'i) indicator for all measured items.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah)
The dispute leads to a concrete difference: Hanafis include lime (jass) and similar non-food measured items in riba, while Shafi'is exclude them because they are not food. This demonstrates how the rule on metaphor's generality directly impacts fiqh rulings.
The thamarah al-khilaf clarifies why usul rules matter for practical law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (في المجاز عموم إذا لحق به موجب له كاللام والإضافة والوقوع تحت النفي ( كالحقيقة) تعم لوجود المقتضى) للعموم (وعدم المانع عنه (فقوله) عليه الصلاة والسلام : لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين) أي لا تبيعوا ما يسعه الصاع بما يسعه الصاعان (يعم (المكيلات كلها مطعوماً وغير مطعوم فيجري الربا في نحو الجص) ولا يصح تعليل الشافعي الحرمة بالطعم، لأنه يعود على أصله بالنقض (و) روي (عن بعض الشافعية) أنه يعم لأنه ضروري) وهو يتقدر بقدر الضرورة، والعموم أمر زائد، فلا يصح (قلنا) كونه ضرورياً (ممنوع) كيف وقد ورد في كلام الله تعالى المنزه عن الضرورة (ولو سلم أنه ضروري (فالاستلزام) أي استلزامها لعدم العموم (ممنوع لأنه ( أي العموم ( بدليل) دال عليه، ففي اعتباره أيضاً ضرورة، واعلم أن كلامهم على هذا النهج يدل على المستدل أراد بالضرورة المتكلم أنه إنما يتجوز إذا اضطر ولا يجد لفظاً آخر حقيقة فيه للضرورة، وهذه الضرورة لو فرضت فلا تنافي العموم أيضاً، لأنه إذا قصد التعبير عن معنى عام ولم يجد لفظاً موضوعاً بإزائه اضطر إلى التعبير عنه بالمجاز، وإن أراد الضرورة بالنظر إلى المخاطب، وقرر الكلام :هكذا إن المجاز إنما يعتبره المخاطب ضرورة عدم صحة الحقيقة وهذه الضرورة تندفع بحمله على معنى، والعموم أمر زائد فلا يصار إليه، وحينئذ لا جواب، إلا أن العموم معنى حقيقي، لأنه ثابت بدليل، فإن اللفظ لا يدل على العموم إلا من جهة أنه محلى باللام مثلاً، وهو موضوع لعموم مدلوله فهو بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة، وإن كان باعتبار إرادة المدلول الغير الوضعي مجازاً فتدبر (قيل) في التلويح لم يعرف (الخلاف في ثبوت العموم (عن أحد كيف ولا نزاع في صحة جاءني الأسود الرماة إلا زيداً وأما استدلال الشيخ عبد السلام على صحة الخلاف بوقوعه في تقارير الأعظم بن أبي البقاء رحمه الله تعالى ففي غير محله كما لا يعني يخفى.
Issue: Metaphor has generality when a cause for it is attached, such as the definite article (al-), idafa (genitive construction), and falling under negation, like the literal. It is general because the motive for generality exists and the preventer is absent. So his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell one dirham for two dirhams, nor one sa' for two sa's' means: do not sell what a sa' contains for what two sa's contain. It includes all measured things (makilat), both edible and non-edible, so riba applies to substances like lime (jass). And it is not correct to make the cause of prohibition taste (ta'm) as al-Shafi'i does, because it would refute his own principle. And it is reported from some Shafi'is that it is general because it is necessary, and necessity is determined by its extent, and generality is additional, so it is not valid. We say: Its being necessary is denied. How so, when it occurs in the speech of Allah which is free from necessity? And even if we accept that it is necessary, the implication that it precludes generality is denied because generality is established by evidence, so considering it is also a necessity. And know that their discourse in this manner indicates that the arguer meant by necessity the speaker: he only uses metaphor when compelled and cannot find another word with literal meaning for it. This necessity, even if assumed, does not contradict generality either, because if he intends to express a general meaning and does not find a word coined for it, he is compelled to express it through metaphor. If he meant necessity with respect to the addressee, and the speech is understood as: metaphor is only considered by the addressee due to necessity of impossibility of literal meaning, and this necessity is alleviated by interpreting it to some meaning, and generality is additional, so it is not adopted. In that case there is no answer, except that generality is a literal meaning because it is established by evidence; for the word does not indicate generality except by virtue of being endowed with the definite article, for example, and it is coined for the generality of its signification, so in this respect it is literal, even if with respect to intending the non-literal meaning it is metaphorical, so reflect. It is said in al-Talwih: The disagreement about the establishment of generality is not known from anyone. How could it be when there is no dispute about the correctness of 'the black archers came except Zayd'? As for the argument of Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam about the existence of disagreement based on its occurrence in the reports of al-A'zam ibn Abi al-Baqa', it is out of place, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage states a usul al-fiqh rule: metaphorical expressions (majaz) can have generality (umum) if they are attached to linguistic markers that indicate generality, such as the definite article (al-), idafa (genitive construction like 'sā'ān'), or falling under negation (nafy). The author applies this to the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين'. The use of the definite article and the construction indicates that the prohibition encompasses all dirhams and all sā's, thus riba (usury) applies to all measurable commodities, whether edible or not, such as lime (jass). The author then refutes Imam al-Shafi'i's view that riba is restricted to foodstuffs based on taste (ta'm), arguing that this contradicts the generality of the hadith. The passage also addresses a Shafi'i argument that metaphor is only used out of necessity, so its scope should be limited to the necessary (i.e., specific items), not extended to all items. The Hanafi response is that necessity does not preclude generality, because the definite article is a clear evidence of generality, and in that respect, the word is literal with respect to its indication of generality, even if it is metaphorical with respect to the intended meaning. The passage concludes by citing al-Talwih, which denies any real disagreement on the issue, stating that claims of disagreement are baseless.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original coinage due to a relationship; in this context, it can still indicate generality if markers are present.
عموم (umum): Generality; a word that includes all individuals of its category without exception.
اللام (alif lam): The definite article that, when used for istighraq (all-encompassing), indicates generality.
إضافة (idafa): Genitive construction (e.g., 'صاعين' as part of a genitive phrase) can indicate generality in some contexts.
النفي (nafi): Negation; when a metaphor falls under negation, it can extend the scope (e.g., 'لا تبيعوا' is general negation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, how does the definite article in the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين' affect the metaphor?
A) It limits the metaphor to only the specific dirhams mentioned
B) It indicates generality, so riba applies to all measured items, including non-edibles
C) It has no effect on metaphor
D) It makes the metaphor literal

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical expressions can indicate generality in the Hanafi school if they are attached to linguistic markers like the definite article, idafa, or negation, and this principle has direct practical consequences in determining the scope of legal rulings such as riba.

The Generality of Metaphorical Expressions in Hanafi Usul
Ruling that metaphor can be general like literal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a metaphorical expression has generality according to Hanafi usul and apply the rule to derive a ruling on riba.

Core Idea
A metaphorical expression can be general like a literal one when accompanied by linguistic indicators.

In Hanafi usul, the possibility of generality is not limited to literal expressions. Metaphorical expressions can also be general when attached to certain grammatical markers such as the definite article (alif lam), construct phrase (idafa), or falling under negation. This rule has significant implications for interpreting prophetic hadiths. For instance, the hadith prohibiting the sale of one sa' for two sa's is understood to cover all measured commodities, not just food, because the word 'sa‘' is used with a definite article, making it general. This lesson explores the rule and its application in a classic dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the Hanafi rule: metaphors can have generality when attached to lam, idafa, or negation, just like literal expressions.
The rule is based on the presence of the cause for generality (mūjib) and absence of a preventer (māni‘).
2
Examine the Evidence
Look at the hadith: 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين'. The word 'الصاع' has the definite article (lam), which is one of the indicators of generality.
The hadith is a command (nahy) implying prohibition, and the generality of the object extends to all instances of the category.
3
Apply the Rule
Interpret the hadith as encompassing all measured items (makilat), both edible and non-edible, because there is no specification to food.
Thus, usury applies to items like gypsum (juss), a non-food measured commodity.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i Position
The Shafi'i school restricts the prohibition to food items based on the 'illah of taste (ta'm). The Hanafi response is that this restriction lacks evidence and contradicts the general wording.
The dispute is over the identification of the effective cause (ta'lil) for riba.
5
Reflect on the Nature of Generality in Metaphor
Understand that the generality in metaphor is considered a literal meaning from the perspective of the grammatical indicator (e.g., lam), even though the core word is used metaphorically.
This distinction resolves the objection that metaphor cannot be general.

Source Text

مسألة في المجاز عموم إذا لحق به موجب له كاللام والإضافة والوقوع تحت النفي ( كالحقيقة) تعم لوجود المقتضى) للعموم (وعدم المانع عنه (فقوله) عليه الصلاة والسلام : لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين) أي لا تبيعوا ما يسعه الصاع بما يسعه الصاعان (يعم (المكيلات كلها مطعوماً وغير مطعوم فيجري الربا في نحو الجص) ولا يصح تعليل الشافعي الحرمة بالطعم، لأنه يعود على أصله بالنقض (و) روي (عن بعض الشافعية) أنه يعم لأنه ضروري) وهو يتقدر بقدر الضرورة، والعموم أمر زائد، فلا يصح (قلنا) كونه ضرورياً (ممنوع) كيف وقد ورد في كلام الله تعالى المنزه عن الضرورة (ولو سلم أنه ضروري (فالاستلزام) أي استلزامها لعدم العموم (ممنوع لأنه أي العموم ( بدليل) دال عليه، ففي اعتباره أيضاً ضرورة، واعلم أن كلامهم على هذا النهج يدل على المستدل أراد بالضرورة المتكلم أنه إنما يتجوز إذا اضطر ولا يجد لفظاً آخر حقيقة فيه للضرورة، وهذه الضرورة لو فرضت فلا تنافي العموم أيضاً، لأنه إذا قصد التعبير عن معنى عام ولم يجد لفظاً موضوعاً بإزائه اضطر إلى التعبير عنه بالمجاز، وإن أراد الضرورة بالنظر إلى المخاطب، وقرر الكلام :هكذا إن المجاز إنما يعتبره المخاطب ضرورة عدم صحة الحقيقة وهذه الضرورة تندفع بحمله على معنى، والعموم أمر زائد فلا يصار إليه، وحينئذ لا جواب، إلا أن العموم معنى حقيقي، لأنه ثابت بدليل، فإن اللفظ لا يدل على العموم إلا من جهة أنه محلى باللام مثلاً، وهو موضوع لعموم مدلوله فهو بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة، وإن كان باعتبار إرادة المدلول الغير الوضعي مجازاً فتدبر (قيل) في التلويح لم يعرف (الخلاف في ثبوت العموم (عن أحد كيف ولا نزاع في صحة جاءني الأسود الرماة إلا زيداً وأما استدلال الشيخ عبد السلام على صحة الخلاف بوقوعه في تقارير الأعظم بن أبي البقاء رحمه الله تعالى ففي غير محله كما لا يعني يخفى .
Issue: In metaphor there is generality when it is attached to something that necessitates it, like the definite article (lam) and construct phrase (idafa) and falling under negation, just like [in] literal, it is general due to the presence of the cause for generality and absence of preventer.  So his statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's', meaning do not sell what a sa' contains for what two sa's contain, encompasses all measured things, both edible and non-edible, so usury applies to things like gypsum. And the Shafi'i's explanation of prohibition by taste is invalid, because it returns upon his principle as a rebuttal. And it is narrated from some Shafi'is that it is general because it is necessary, and it is measured by the measure of necessity, whereas generality is extra, so it is invalid. We say: Its being necessary is denied; how so when it occurs in the speech of Allah, exalted, which is free from necessity. And if it is conceded that it is necessary, then the implication (i.e., its implication of lack of generality) is denied because generality is established by evidence, so necessity also exists in considering it. And know that their discourse in this manner indicates that the arguer intended by necessity the speaker: that he only uses metaphor when forced and does not find another word literal for it out of necessity. This necessity, if supposed, does not negate generality either, because if he intends to express a general meaning and does not find a word placed for it, he is forced to express it by metaphor. And if he intended necessity with respect to the addressee, and the speech is formulated: that metaphor is only considered by the addressee out of necessity due to the invalidity of the literal meaning, and this necessity is repelled by carrying it to a meaning, and generality is extra so it is not resorted to. Then there is no answer except that generality is a literal meaning because it is established by evidence; the word does not indicate generality except by being prefixed with the definite article, for example, and it is placed for the generality of its signification, so from this perspective it is literal, although from the perspective of intending non-lexical signification it is metaphorical. So reflect. It was said in al-Talwih: The disagreement regarding the existence of generality is not known from anyone, how so? There is no dispute in the validity of 'Jā'ani al-aswad al-rumāh illā Zaydan' . As for the argument of Shaykh Abd al-Salam on the validity of disagreement by its occurrence in the reports of al-A'zam ibn Abi al-Baqa', may Allah have mercy on him, it is out of place, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the generality (umum) of metaphorical expressions (majaz). It states that when a metaphorical phrase is attached with indicators like the definite article (alif lam), construct phrase (idafa), or falling under negation, it has generality just like literal expressions. The proof is the hadith 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's', which is interpreted to cover all measured commodities (makilat), both edible and non-edible, according to Hanafis. Thus, usury (riba) applies to items like gypsum (juss). The Hanafis reject the Shafi'i view that the 'illah (cause) for riba in the hadith is taste (ta'm), because that would be inconsistent with their own principles. Some Shafi'is claim the hadith is general out of necessity, but the Hanafis argue that necessity does not negate generality. They also discuss that generality in metaphor is actually a literal meaning when viewed from the linguistic indicator (e.g., lam ta'rif), even though the core usage is metaphorical. The passage ends by dismissing a reported disagreement about generality in metaphor, citing the example of 'Jā'ani al-aswad al-rumāh illā Zaydan' as an undisputed instance of general metaphor.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز (‘umūm al-majāz): The capacity of a metaphorical expression to be general when attached to indicators like lam, idafa, or negation.
الطعم (al-ṭa‘m): Taste as a presumed 'illah (cause) for riba in the Shafi'i school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, why does the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين' apply to non-food items like gypsum?
A) Because the hadith specifically mentions gypsum.
B) Because the hadith uses a general measure (sa') with a definite article, and there is no restriction to food.
C) Because the Shafi'is agree it includes non-food.
D) Because the word 'taste' is not mentioned.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical expressions can be general when attached to indicators like the definite article, and this principle is crucial in interpreting sources of law.

The Hanafi Rule: No Combining Literal and Metaphorical Meanings
Ruling that one cannot intend both literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that prohibits combining literal and metaphorical meanings to identify when an utterance is invalid, and distinguish it from kinayah and generality of metaphor.

Core Idea
A single utterance cannot simultaneously convey both literal and metaphorical meanings as direct objects of the ruling.

This lesson examines the Hanafi usul principle prohibiting the combination of ḥaqīqah and majāz in one intent. The text explains that while Shafi'is generally allow it unless rationally impossible, the Hanafis argue it leads to logical absurdities. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly interpreting texts that could carry multiple meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Define the dispute: Is it permissible to intend both literal and metaphorical meanings in a single utterance as direct objects of the ruling?
2
Understand the Hanafi Prohibition
State the ruling: it is not permissible. Understand the proof: combination leads to three absurdities (both true, neither true, only one true without preferrer).
3
Distinguish Kinayah and Umum al-Majaz
Kinayah is allowed because the literal meaning is a prelude, not intended per se. Generality of metaphor (umum al-majaz) is also allowed because it involves one metaphorical meaning encompassing the literal as an instance.
Examples from the text: 'the pen is one of the two tongues' and 'the maternal uncle is one of the parents' are instances of umum al-majaz, not jamb.
4
Analyze the Proof Structure
Examine the logical entailment: if one intends both, the word becomes either both literal and metaphorical (impossible by consensus), neither, or exclusively one without preference. All are invalid.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
5
Recognize the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: in cases like 'qalam' (pen/lip) intended with both meanings, Hanafis rule the utterance invalid; Shafi'is may allow it if rationally possible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة المبادىء اللغوية مسألة لا يجوز الجمع بينهما أي بين المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي في الإرادة حال كونهما مقصودين بالحكم بالذات (بخلاف الكناية فإنه وإن أريد فيها الموضوع له وملزومه لكن ليسا مقصودين، بل جعل الأول، توطئة وتمهيداً للثاني وأجازه الشافعية، إلا أن لا يمكن الجمع عقلاً ( كافعل أمراً وتهديداً للتنافي بينهما أو بالنظر إلى القرينة الصارفة عن الحقيقي، وظاهر هذا يشعر أن الأصل عندهم الجمع إلا للضرورة (و) قال الإمام حجة الإسلام محمد الغزالي : يصح الجمع عقلاً لا (لغة قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية : هذا تفسير لمذهب الجمهور المانعين للجمع، ولم يقل أحد بالاستحالة العقلية (وقيل في غير المفهوم من التكرار وأما المثنى فالاستعمال فيه واحد، فيلزم الجمع بخلاف التكرار، لأن الاستعمال فيه ،متعدد فلا جمع في استعمال واحد (والتعميم في المعاني (المجازية) بأن يراد أكثر من واحد ويكون مناط الحكم كلا على الاستقلال (قيل على هذا (الخلاف) فمن جوز الجمع جوزه ومن لا فلا (وقيل: لا خلاف في منعه كما لا خلاف (في جواز عموم المجاز) وهو إرادة معنى مجازي شامل للحقيقي، وغيره ومتناول له بما أنه فرد منه لنا الاستحالات (كونه حقيقة ومجازاً في استعمال واحد، وقد اتفق على منعه كلبس ثوب ملكاً وعارية وهذا تنظير للاستحالة لا مناطها فالمناقشة فيه طائحة (أو لا شيء منهما) أي كونه غير حقيقة ومجاز (أو أحدهما أي كونه وينتفي عرفاً بانتفائه، ألا ترى لا يقال لمجموع السماء مر
The third article: Linguistic principles. Issue: It is not permissible to combine the two, i.e., the literal and metaphorical meanings, in the intent while they are intended by the ruling per se (unlike kinayah, for even if in it the subject and its concomitant are intended, they are not intended per se; rather, the first is made a prelude and preparation for the second). And the Shafi'is allowed it, unless combination is rationally impossible (like 'do it' as a command and a threat due to contradiction between them, or with respect to the indicator diverting from the literal). And the apparent of this suggests that the principle with them is combination except for necessity. And Imam Hujjat al-Islam Muhammad al-Ghazali said: It is rationally valid but not linguistically. Sahib Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah said: This is an interpretation of the school of the majority who prohibit combination, and no one affirmed rational impossibility. And it is said that in other than singular it is linguistically valid, evidenced by 'the pen is one of the two tongues': 'tongue' was intended as the specific organ literally and as that which expresses writing metaphorically; and 'the maternal uncle is one of the parents': the true father was intended literally and maternal uncle metaphorically. But there is something in it because these are not instances of combination but rather instances of generality of metaphor; in the first, that which expresses was intended, and in the second, the affectionate one. As for the saying that the dual is like repetition, so there is no combination in a single word, there is something in it that is not hidden; the intended meaning is that the understood from it is that which is understood from repetition, but in the dual the usage is single, so combination follows, unlike repetition because the usage is multiple, so no combination in one usage. And generalization in metaphorical meanings, i.e., intending more than one, and the basis of the ruling is each independently. It is said that on this there is disagreement: whoever permits combination permits it, and whoever does not, does not. And it is said: there is no disagreement on its prohibition, just as there is no disagreement on the validity of generality of metaphor, which is intending a metaphorical meaning inclusive of the literal and others, encompassing it as an individual of it. For us, what was mentioned in the common applies: the necessity of directing the soul to two relationships noticed in detail when intending them, and also that it is not complete, and lack of completion here is more apparent because the literal, due to its originality, precedes the metaphorical. Also, if combination were valid, then one of the impossibilities would follow: either it would be literal and metaphorical in one usage, and there is agreement on its prohibition like wearing a garment as ownership and loan, and this is a comparison for impossibility not its basis, so discussion about it is idle; or it would be neither literal nor metaphorical; or it would be one of them, i.e., either literal only or metaphorical only; and both are false. As for the last, due to preference without a preferrer, and for the others by consensus of the Arabic scholars. And the entailment: if one is satisfied in literal and metaphor by intending the subject so that it is the basis of the ruling or other than subject, then here both have been intended independently and congruently, so the first option follows; and if it is conditioned in the literal that non-subject is not intended, and in the metaphor that the subject is not intended, then in our case both are intended, so the second option follows; and if conditioned in one without the other, then the third option. So reflect. Thus the desired is made clear by the soundest proof. It is said that it is metaphor in the composite because it is other than what the word is placed for, and there is no impossibility in it. We say: The word is used for each one individually, and the basis of the ruling is each one individually, and the word is used in each congruently, not for the composite, because it is not the basis of the ruling. How so? If the intended were the composite metaphorically, there would be no relation between it and the literal; and calling the part by the name of the whole is conditioned by it being named with another name, and it is customarily negated by its absence. Do you not see that the composite of the sky is not called 'bitter'?
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that one cannot intend both the literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings of a word in a single utterance when both are the direct objects of the ruling. This is in contrast to kinayah (allusion), where the literal meaning is a prelude to the intended meaning. The Shafi'is generally allow combination unless it is rationally impossible; al-Ghazali allowed rationally but not linguistically. The Hanafi position (majority) prohibits it. The text argues that combining leads to one of three absurdities: 1) The word becomes both literal and metaphorical simultaneously (impossible by consensus), 2) It becomes neither, 3) It becomes exclusively one without the other, which lacks a preferrer. The case of the dual (two 'tongues') is addressed: it is not combination but rather 'umum al-majaz (generality of metaphor) because the intended meaning is one (e.g., 'the tongue' meaning 'organ' or 'language' is not both at once). The same applies to 'al-khal' (maternal uncle). The text then discusses 'ta'mim fi al-ma'ani al-majaziyyah' (generalization in metaphorical meanings) and concludes that the view of prohibition of combination is correct. Finally, it refutes the argument that the word could be used as a metaphor for the composite meaning, explaining that the word is used for each individual, not the composite.

Key Terms
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam‘ bayna al-ḥaqīqah wa al-majāz): Intending both the literal and metaphorical meanings of a word in a single utterance per se.
الكناية (al-kināyah): A figure where the literal meaning is intended as a prelude to the intended meaning, not as the direct object of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the main reason for prohibiting the combination of literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance?
A) Because it is linguistically impossible.
B) Because it leads to one of three absurdities.
C) Because the Shafi'is allow it.
D) Because it is only allowed in kinayah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule prohibits combining literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance per se, as it leads to logical absurdities; exceptions like kinayah and umum al-majaz are not true combinations.

Combining Haqiqi and Majazi Meanings: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Debate on the permissibility of combining literal and figurative meanings in a single utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position against combining haqiqah and majaz in one utterance and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of umum al-majaz.

Core Idea
A single utterance cannot simultaneously convey literal and figurative meanings—to do so leads to logical impossibility.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a word can be used to intend both its literal (haqiqi) and figurative (majazi) meanings at the same time. The Hanafis argue it is impossible, while the Shafi'is generally permit it. Understanding this debate is crucial for interpreting legal texts where a word may have multiple potential meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: is it permissible to intend both the literal and figurative meanings of a single word in one utterance?
The Hanafis reject this; Shafi'is accept it unless rationally impossible.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafis forbid combination, arguing that it leads to one of three logical impossibilities (being both literal and figurative, being neither, or being only one without a preponderant).
Their proof is rational (dalil aqli) and appeals to consensus of Arabic scholars.
3
State the Shafi'i position and their evidence
Shafi'is generally allow combination, citing examples like 'al-qalam ahad al-lisanayn' (the pen is one of the two tongues) where 'lisan' seems to intend both the physical tongue and the means of writing.
They also argue that the dual form is akin to repetition, so no combination occurs.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis refute the examples as instances of umum al-majaz (general figurative meaning) rather than simultaneous distinct intentions. They also reject the dual-as-repetition argument because the dual is a single usage.
This distinction is key: umum al-majaz involves a single figurative meaning that includes the literal as an individual, not two separate meanings.
5
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects legal interpretation, e.g., in a bequest (wasiyya) to 'mawali' when there is only one mawla. Hanafis do not extend to mawali al-mawali (clients of clients); Shafi'is may include them.
This shows how linguistic principles yield different fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المبادىء اللغوية

للثاني وأجازه الشافعية، إلا أن لا يمكن الجمع عقلاً ( كافعل أمراً وتهديداً للتنافي بينهما أو بالنظر إلى القرينة الصارفة عن الحقيقي، وظاهر هذا يشعر أن الأصل عندهم الجمع إلا للضرورة (و) قال الإمام حجة الإسلام محمد الغزالي : يصح الجمع عقلاً لا (لغة قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية : هذا تفسير لمذهب الجمهور المانعين للجمع، ولم يقل أحد بالاستحالة العقلية (وقيل في غير المفرد يصح لغة، بدليل القلم أحد (اللسانين فإنه أريد في الأول المبين وفي الثاني الشفيق، وأما القول بأن التثنية في حكم التكرار فلا جمع في لفظ واحد ففيه ما لا يخفى، إذ المراد أن المفهوم منها ما هو المفهوم من التكرار وأما المثنى فالاستعمال فيه واحد، فيلزم الجمع بخلاف التكرار، لأن الاستعمال فيه ،متعدد فلا جمع في استعمال واحد (والتعميم في المعاني (المجازية) بأن يراد أكثر من واحد ويكون مناط الحكم كلا على الاستقلال (قيل على هذا (الخلاف) فمن جوز الجمع جوزه ومن لا فلا (وقيل: لا خلاف في منعه كما لا خلاف (في جواز عموم المجاز) وهو إرادة معنى مجازي شامل للحقيقي، وغيره ومتناول له بما أنه فرد منه لنا ما مر في المشترك) من لزوم توجه النفس إلى نسبتين ملحوظتين تفصيلاً عند إرادتهما، وقد أيضاً أنه لا يتم وعدم التمام ههنا ، أظهر، لأن الحقيقي لأصالته أسبق من المجازي (وأيضاً) لو صح الجمع (يلزم أحد الاستحالات (كونه حقيقة ومجازاً في استعمال واحد، وقد اتفق على منعه كلبس ثوب ملكاً وعارية وهذا تنظير للاستحالة لا مناطها فالمناقشة فيه طائحة (أو لا شيء منهما) أي كونه غير حقيقة ومجاز (أو أحدهما أي كونه إما حقيقة فقط أو مجازاً فقط وكلاهما باطل) أما الأخير فللرجحان من غير مرجح وللباقي بإجماع أهل العربية، والملازمة لأنه إن اكتفى في الحقيقة والمجاز بإرادة الموضوع له بحيث يكون مناطاً للحكم أو غير الموضوع له، فههنا قد أريدا استقلالاً ومطابقة فيلزم الشق الأول ، وإن اشترط في الحقيقة عدم إرادة غير الموضوع ،له وفي المجاز عدم إرادة الموضوع له فقد أريدا فيما نحن فيه فيلزم الشق الثاني، وإن اشترط في أحدهما دون الآخر فالشق الثالث فتأمل، فقد تبين المطلوب بأقوم حجة (قيل) إنه (مجاز في المجموع) لأنه غير ما وضع له اللفظ ولا استحالة فيه (قلنا: اللفظ) استعمل (لكل) أي كل واحد واحد ومناط الحكم كل أي كل واحد واحد، واللفظ مستعمل في كل مطابقة (لا المجموع) أي لا أنه مستعمل للمجموع إذ ليس مناط الحكم، كيف ولو كان المراد المجموع مجازاً ، فلا علاقة بينه وبين الحقيقي وإطلاق الجزء على الكل مشروط بكونه مسمى باسم ،آخر وينتفي عرفاً بانتفائه، ألا ترى لا يقال لمجموع السماء والأرض سماء أو أرض فإن قيل : أريد المجموع بطريق عموم المجاز بأن يكون فرد المفهوم آخر استعمل فيه اللفظ قيل ( أما ) الإرادة بطريق) عموم المجاز فلا نزاع فيه .
The third article: Linguistic Principles. Issue: It is not permissible to combine them, i.e., the literal meaning and the figurative meaning, in intention when both are intended directly by the ruling (unlike kinayah, for although in it the subject-matter and its necessary concomitant are intended, they are not both intended directly; rather the first is made a prelude and groundwork for the second. The Shafi'is permit it, unless combination is impossible rationally (like commanding and threatening for mutual incompatibility between them, or due to the context that diverts from the literal. This apparent meaning suggests that the principle with them is combination except out of necessity). Imam Hujjat al-Islam Muhammad al-Ghazali said: It is valid rationally but not linguistically. The author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: This is an interpretation of the majority who forbid combination, and no one has claimed rational impossibility. It is said that in other than a single word it is valid linguistically, evidenced by 'al-qalam ahad al-lisanayn' (the pen is one of the two tongues), where 'lisan' intends both the physical organ (literally) and the means of writing (figuratively). And 'al-khal ahad al-abawayn' (the maternal uncle is one of the two parents) intends the real father literally and the uncle figuratively. But there is criticism: these are not instances of combination but of general figurative meaning (umum al-majaz), for in the first the intended is the means of writing, and in the second the affectionate uncle. As for the statement that the dual is equivalent to repetition, so there is no combination in a single word, there is obvious flaw. For what is meant is that the understood from it is the same as from repetition, but the dual is a single usage, so combination follows, unlike repetition where usage is multiple, so no combination in a single usage. Generalization in figurative meanings by intending more than one and making each independently the legal basis. It is said that the disagreement applies: whoever permits combination permits it, and whoever does not, does not. It is also said: There is no disagreement on its prohibition, just as there is no disagreement on the permissibility of umum al-majaz, i.e., intending a figurative meaning that includes the literal and other things and encompasses it as an individual. For us (Hanafis) the same as previously in the case of the homonym (mushtarak): the necessity of directing the mind to two distinct relations in detail when intending both. And it is also evident that it is not complete, and incompleteness is more apparent here because the literal, due to its originality, precedes the figurative. Also, if combination were valid, one of three impossibilities would follow: (1) it being simultaneously literal and figurative in one usage, which is unanimously prohibited, like wearing a garment both as ownership and as loan—this is an analogy for the impossibility, not its basis, so criticism of it is futile; (2) it being neither literal nor figurative; (3) it being one of them, i.e., either only literal or only figurative. Both are invalid. The last is invalid due to preferment without a preponderant, and the others by consensus of Arabic scholars. The implication: if one is satisfied in literal and figurative with intending the subject-matter as the basis of the ruling or other than the subject-matter, here both are intended independently and congruently, so the first case follows. If one stipulates in literal not intending other than the subject-matter, and in figurative not intending the subject-matter, then both have been intended in our case, so the second case follows. If one stipulates in one but not the other, then the third case. So reflect. The required has been established by the soundest proof. It is said that it is figurative in the aggregate (majaz fi al-majmu'), because it is other than what the word was placed for and there is no impossibility. We (Hanafis) say: The word is used for each individual, and each individual is the basis of the ruling. The word is used for each congruently, not for the aggregate, because the aggregate is not the basis of the ruling. How can it be? If the intended was the aggregate figuratively, then there would be no relation between it and the literal. And applying the part to the whole is conditioned by the whole being named by another name, and it ceases customarily with its cessation. Do you not see that the totality of sky and earth is not called sky or earth? If it is said that the aggregate is intended via umum al-majaz, by making the word used for an individual of a different concept, then it is said: Intending via umum al-majaz is not disputed.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the permissibility of combining literal (haqiqi) and figurative (majazi) meanings in a single utterance when both are intended. The Hanafi position, stated at the outset, is that it is not permissible. The passage then explains exceptions: kinayah (allusion) is not included because although both the subject-matter and its necessary concomitant are intended, they are not both intended directly; one is a prelude. The Shafi'is generally allow combination unless rationally impossible, as when there is contradiction between meanings or a contextual indicator prevents it. Imam al-Ghazali is cited as allowing it rationally but not linguistically. The author of Matali' al-Asrar clarifies that the majority forbid it, but no one claims rational impossibility. The text then discusses examples like 'al-qalam ahad al-lisanayn' and 'al-khal ahad al-abawayn', which some argue prove combination, but the response is that these are instances of umum al-majaz (general figurative meaning) rather than simultaneous literal and figurative intention. The argument that the dual form is like repetition is refuted because the dual is a single usage, so combining would occur. The Hanafi proof revolves around the impossibility of simultaneously intending two distinct relations (to the literal and figurative meanings), leading to three absurd consequences: the word being both literal and figurative (impossible by consensus), being neither, or being only one of them without a preponderant. The passage concludes with a technical discussion of whether the word is used for each individual or for the aggregate, affirming the Hanafi view that each individual is intended, not the aggregate, thus avoiding combination. The teacher should note that this dispute has practical consequences in legal interpretation, such as in bequests and oaths.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal meaning for which a word was originally coined
مجاز (majaz): The figurative meaning that is other than the original coinage and requires a contextual indicator
كناية (kinayah): An expression where both the subject-matter and its necessary concomitant are intended but one is a prelude
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): A general figurative meaning that encompasses the literal meaning as one of its individuals

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is combining haqiqah and majaz in one utterance invalid?
A) Because it leads to a rational impossibility.
B) Because the Quran forbids it.
C) Because the Shafi'is allow it.
D) Because it is not found in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits combining literal and figurative meanings in a single utterance because it leads to logical impossibility, and apparent counterexamples are actually instances of umum al-majaz.

Literal vs. Customary Metaphor: The Hanafi Dispute on Priority
Usul al-Fiqh dispute on priority of literal vs. customary metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding which meaning takes priority and apply the distinction to oath rulings.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa holds literal as original; Sahibayn prioritize customary metaphor due to immediate understanding.

In Hanafi usul, a key dispute arises when a word has both a literal meaning and a customary metaphorical meaning. Abu Hanifa insists on adhering to the original literal meaning unless there is a clear preventer, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argue that customary usage creates immediate understanding that overrides the literal. This disagreement has practical effects in oaths and other legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle of each position
State the principle that each side relies on: Abu Hanifa uses asalah (originality); the Sahibayn use tabadur (immediate understanding).
These are the foundational justifications.
2
Analyze the example of the oath on meat
Examine the case of someone swearing not to eat meat and then eating human flesh. Note that literally, human flesh is meat, but custom excludes it. See how each side rules differently.
The fatwa generally follows the Sahibayn's view not to break the oath.
3
Understand Fakhr al-Islam's explanation
Fakhr al-Islam links the dispute to whether metaphor is branch-like in ruling or in the speaker. The Sahibayn consider it branch-like in ruling, so the metaphorical ruling covers the literal; Abu Hanifa considers it branch-like in the speaker, so the speaker's intention prioritized.
This explanation is critiqued later in the passage.
4
Evaluate the critique and the author's conclusion
The author points out that Fakhr al-Islam's reasoning would imply that every metaphor encompassing the literal should be preferred, not just customary ones. The true basis is asalah vs. tabadur.
The author sides with the view that tabadur is a strong preventer.
5
Apply to other cases (wheat, Euphrates water)
Consider other oath examples like 'I will not drink Euphrates water' or 'I will not eat wheat'. In each, custom may define the scope. Note the practical thamarah al-khilaf.
The dispute is limited to contexts not entirely based on custom; where custom dominates, the customary meaning is inevitable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الحقيقة المستعملة أولى من المجاز المتعارف عنده عملاً بالأصل) فإن الحقيقة أصل فمهما أمكن لا يصح العدول عنه، في الحاشية»: هذا لا ينافي ما سيأتي أن التخصيص بالعرف عملياً كان أو قولياً يصح، وذلك لما سيأتي أن العام المخصص حقيقة عند الفقهاء، وهذا لا يتأتى منا، فإنا لا نقول بكونه حقيقة وعندهما بالعكس) أي المجاز المتعارف أولى من الحقيقة المستعملة للتبادر إلى الفهم فإن التعارف يوجب التبادر بلا ريب، ولا تعارضه الأصالة، لأن الأصالة إنما تقتضي الحمل عليه إذا لم يمنع مانع والتبادر والتعارف مانعان قويان فافهم فإنه أحق بالقبول وقيل : تساويا) فيتوقف حتى يتعين أحدهما بالدليل (أقول : ينبغي أن يكون النزاع فيما لم يكن مبناه على العرف كالأيمان لأن ما يكون مبناه على العرف يفهم منه المتعارف بالضرورة ولهذا أفتوا بعدم الحنث عنده في حلفه لا يأكل لحماً بأكل لحم آدمي إذا كان الحالف مسلماً) مع أن اللحم حقيقة يصدق على لحم الآدمي، قال «مطلع الأسرار» لا حاجة إلى التقييد بالإسلام في لحم الأدمي، فإن الكافر والمسلم سواء فيه، ونقل عن الزاهد العتابي أن الفتوى عليه وإلا ففيه رواية أخرى في المعتبرات كالهداية وغيرها أنه يحنث لكونه في الحقيقة، ثم إن ما ذكر غير ظاهر وإن كان قبل هذا القول من قبل أيضاً، لأن مسألة لحماً الفرات والحنطة أيضاً في اليمين الذي مبناه على العرف قال الإمام فخر الإسلام إن هذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في فرعية المجاز فلما كانت الفرعية عندهما باعتبار الحكم كان اعتباره أولى، ففي مسألة أكل الحنطة لما كان حكم المجاز المتعارف شاملاً لحكم الحقيقة اعتبراه ، وعنده : لما كان باعتبار التكلم اعتبر التكلم فرجح الحقيقة للأصالة، وهذا التعليل يدل على أن الخلاف في المجاز المتناول للحقيقة ثم في هذا التعليل نظر، لأنه لو تم لدل على رجحان كل مجاز متناول للحقيقة، سواء كان متعارفاً أم لا ، ثم أن اعتبار الخلفية في التكلم لا يوجب اعتبار الحقيقة وإن تشبث بالأصالة فهي كافية ولا حاجة إلى الخلفية في التكلم فالحق ما أشار إليه المصنف أن المبنى للخلاف أنه اعتبر الأصالة وهما التبادر.
Issue: The literal meaning in use is preferred over the customary metaphor according to him (Abu Hanifa) based on the principle of originality, for the literal is the original, so whenever possible, it is not correct to deviate from it. In the gloss: This does not contradict what will come that specification by custom, whether practical or verbal, is valid. That is because the general that is specified is considered literal according to the jurists, but this is not from us, for we do not say it is literal. According to them (the two companions), the opposite: i.e., the customary metaphor is preferred over the literal meaning in use due to its immediate understanding (tabadur), for custom/customariness causes immediate understanding without doubt, and originality does not oppose it, because originality only requires being carried upon it if there is no preventing obstacle, and immediate understanding and custom are strong preventors, so understand, for it is more deserving of acceptance. And it was said: they are equal, so one must wait until one is determined by evidence. I say: The dispute should be in what is not based on custom, like oaths, because what is based on custom is understood customarily by necessity. For this reason they gave fatwa that he does not break his oath if he swears not to eat meat and then eats human flesh, if the swearer is Muslim, even though 'meat' literally applies to human flesh. Matla' al-Asrar said: There is no need to restrict to Islam in the case of human flesh, for both the disbeliever and Muslim are equal in it. And it is transmitted from al-Zahid al-Attabi that the fatwa is upon it; otherwise, there is another narration in reliable works like al-Hidayah that he does break his oath because it is literal. Then what is mentioned is not apparent, even though before this statement it was also from before, because the issue of meat, the Euphrates, and wheat also in oaths based on custom. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: This difference is built upon the difference regarding the branch-like nature of metaphor. Since, according to them (the two companions), the branch-like nature is in terms of ruling, so considering it is preferable. In the issue of eating wheat, since the ruling of the customary metaphor covers the ruling of the literal, they considered it. According to him (Abu Hanifa), since it is in terms of the speaker, he considered the speaker and thus preferred the literal due to originality. This reasoning indicates that the difference is regarding metaphor that encompasses the literal. Then there is a problem in this reasoning, because if it were complete, it would indicate the preponderance of every metaphor that encompasses the literal, whether customary or not. Then considering the branch-like nature in the speaker does not necessitate considering the literal; and even if he clings to originality, it is sufficient and there is no need for branch-like nature in the speaker. So the truth is what the author indicated: that the basis of the difference is that he (Abu Hanifa) considered originality and they considered immediate understanding.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the principle that when a word has both a literal meaning (haqiqah) and a customary metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'arif), which takes priority. Abu Hanifa (عنده) holds that the literal meaning is preferred due to originality (asalah), meaning that the literal is the original basis and should not be abandoned without strong evidence. The two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the opposite: the customary metaphor is preferred because it is immediately understood (tabadur) by people, and custom provides a strong preventer from applying the literal meaning. Some say they are equal, requiring external evidence. The author suggests that the dispute is relevant where custom is not the basis, but in areas like oaths where custom governs, the customary meaning is inevitably understood. For example, if someone swears 'I will not eat meat' and eats human flesh, most jurists say he does not break his oath because custom restricts 'meat' to animal meat, despite the literal application to human flesh. Fakhr al-Islam explains that the disagreement stems from how metaphor is considered: the Sahibayn view metaphor as branching in terms of ruling (the ruling of the metaphor applies to the literal as well), while Abu Hanifa views it as branching in terms of the speaker (the speaker intends the literal unless there is a clear sign). The passage critiques this reasoning, concluding that the real basis is that Abu Hanifa emphasizes originality, while the Sahibayn emphasize immediate understanding.

Key Terms
الحقيقة المستعملة (al-haqiqah al-musta'malah): The literal meaning that is actually used in speech, often the original lexical meaning.
المجاز المتعارف (al-majaz al-muta'arif): A metaphorical meaning that has become customary or conventional in common usage, to the point of being expected.
التبادر (al-tabadur): Immediate understanding or spontaneous interpretation: what comes to mind first upon hearing a word without context.
الأصالة (al-asalah): The principle of originality or default status: the literal meaning is considered the original and is presumed until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is the customary metaphorical meaning preferred over the literal meaning?
A) Because the literal meaning is ambiguous and difficult to enforce in oaths.
B) Because custom causes immediate understanding (tabadur), which overrides the original default.
C) Because the literal meaning is only applicable in non-Islamic contexts.
D) Because the Sahibayn reject the concept of literal meaning altogether.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute on literal vs. customary metaphor hinges on whether asalah (originality) or tabadur (immediate understanding) should prevail, with practical consequences in oath rulings.

Interpretation of Ambiguous Terms in Oaths: The Case of 'al-Furat' and 'Wheat'
Analysis of interpretation of ambiguous oath terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principles of haqiqa, majaz, and urf to resolve disputes over ambiguous terms in oaths, and evaluate the tarjih between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When an oath uses ambiguous terms without intention, customary usage (urf) overrides literal meaning according to the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines how Hanafi jurists interpret ambiguous terms in oaths when the swearer has no specific intention. The classic example: someone swears 'I will not drink from the Euphrates nor eat wheat.' Does drinking from a canal fed by the Euphrates violate the oath? Does eating bread made from specific wheat violate? We will follow the rich discussion in Fawatih al-Rahamut, where the author presents the views of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, and then critiques the attempts to distinguish between specific and non-specific objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and absence of intention
Recognize that the oath uses ambiguous terms ('al-Furat', 'wheat') and the swearer has no niyyah. This triggers interpretation by default principles of usul al-fiqh.
No qarina or context exists.
2
Apply the usul principle: haqiqa vs urf
Abu Hanifa defaults to haqiqa (literal meaning): 'al-Furat' means the act of dipping (kar'), 'wheat' means the actual grain. The Sahibayn use urf (customary meaning): 'al-Furat' means any water attributed to it, and 'wheat' means what is normally made from it (bread, etc.).
Hanafi usul holds that in the absence of intention, the word is taken in its haqiqa unless there is a reason to deviate.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf for each term
For 'al-Furat': Abu Hanifa says only dipping by mouth; Sahibayn say any method of drinking water from the Euphrates. For 'wheat': Abu Hanifa says only the grain; Sahibayn say bread and other customary products.
The canal example: Sahibayn say no violation if water is from a canal because attribution is severed in urf.
4
Examine the distinction between specific and non-specific wheat
Some jurists distinguish: for non-specific 'wheat', bread violates by consensus; for 'this wheat', there is dispute. The author argues this distinction is arbitrary because custom is the same.
The author claims that common usage does not differentiate, so the swearer should violate in both cases.
5
Conclude with the tarjih
The author concludes that the Sahibayn's view is more appropriate (al-ashbah ma qala), and even Abu Hanifa was not consistent in applying the literal principle. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) favors customary interpretation.
Tarjih based on consistency with urf and practical application.

لا يشرب من الفرات ولا يأكل الحنطة ولا نية لشيء من المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي فعنده انصرف إلى الكرع في الأول (و) إلى ( عينها ) أي عين الحنطة في الثاني فإنه حقيقة الكلام وهي أسبق وعندهما إلى مائه اعترافاً في الأول : أي الماء المنسوب إليه، سواء كان بالاغتراف أو بالأواني أو غيرهما، بحيث لا ينقطع النسبة إليه في العرف، حتى لو اتخذ منه نهراً فلا يحنث بالشرب به أصلاً لأنه انقطع النسبة عنه في العرف (و) ينصرف عندهما (إلى ما يتخذ منها من الخبز وغيره في الثاني، ولا يحنث بما يصير بالعمل جنساً آخر، فلا يحنث بالسويق، لأنه غير جنس الحنطة، ولهذا يجوز السويق بالدقيق متفاضلاً عندهما كذا قالوا بيع وتأمل فيه (وبعضهم فرق بين حنطة معينة وغير معينة فقالوا في غير المعينة بأن يقول : لا آكل حنطة، يحنث بالاتفاق بالخبز ،وغيره لأن المتعارف فيه على ما يتخذ وفي المعينة بأن يقول : هذه الحنطة، فعلى الخلاف فعنده لا يحنث بالخبز بل بالعين وعندهما يحنث على كل حال، لأن العادة فيه مختلفة (أقول : ولك أن تدعي (الاشتراك أي اشتراك المعينة وغير المعينة (في العرف مطلقاً) أي في مطلق الإطلاق وإن كان الغالب في الاستعمال بعض أنحائهما، وهو (ما اغترف، أو المتخذ فينبغي أن يحنث مطلقاً) أي في المعينة وغير المعينة، فالفرق تحكم، ولا يخفى أنه على هذا يستدرك قوله : وإن كان الغالب، فإن قلت : يجوز أن يكون متعلقاً بقولهما، أي العرف مشترك في أكل العين والمتخذ والكرع والاغتراف، فينبغي أن يحنث مطلقاً بطريق عموم المجاز، قلت ظاهر من حيث اللفظ، لكنه مختل جداً، فإن المذكور من قول الصاحبين في أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام هو هذا كما أشرنا إليه وفي الهداية»: الأصح أنهما قائلان المجاز، فيحنث مطلقاً، فحينئذ لا توجه لهذا الإيراد عليهما ، إلا أن البعض فهموا أنهما بعموم قالا بالحنث بالمتخذ أو الاغتراف دون العين وبالعين عنده فكأن المصنف نقل أولاً هذا القول ثم اعترض، فصحح الحنث مطلقاً هذا وقد ظهر لك من هذا القيل والقال أن الأشبه ما قالا، والإمام أيضاً غير مستمر على هذا الأصل، والله أعلم بحقيقة ما عليه عباده الصالحون .
He does not drink from the Euphrates nor eat wheat, and has no intention of either the literal or metaphorical meaning. According to him (Abu Hanifa), it is understood as dipping (into the river) in the first, and (as) the very grain (i.e., the actual wheat) in the second, because that is the literal meaning of the word and it is primary. According to them (the two disciples - Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is understood as 'its water' in the first, acknowledging: i.e., the water attributed to it, whether by scooping, using vessels, or otherwise, as long as the attribution to it is not severed in common usage, even if a canal is made from it, he is not considered to have violated the oath by drinking from it at all, because the attribution is severed in common usage. And according to them, it is understood as what is made from it (the wheat) of bread and other things in the second. He does not violate the oath by what becomes another type through processing, so he is not considered to have violated by eating sawiq (parched barley), because it is not of the same type as wheat. For this reason, sawiq is permissible in exchange for flour with disparity (in weight) according to them. Thus they said about a sale: reflect upon it. And some distinguished between specific and non-specific wheat. They said about non-specific wheat when one says 'I will not eat wheat,' he violates by consensus by eating bread and other things because the customary understanding is of what is made from it. In the specific case, when he says 'this wheat,' then there is disagreement: according to him, he does not violate by bread but only by the actual grain; according to them, he violates in any case because custom differs. I say: You may claim that the term is shared between specific and non-specific in common usage absolutely, even if in most usage one of its aspects predominates, namely what is dipped or what is made. So he should be considered to violate absolutely in both specific and non-specific cases. So the distinction is arbitrary. It is not hidden that on this basis one should retract his statement: 'even if the predominant...' If you say: It is possible that this [statement] is attached to their view, meaning that common usage is shared between eating the actual grain and what is made from it, and dipping and scooping. So he should be considered to violate absolutely by way of general metaphor. I say: It is apparent from the wording, but it is very confused. For what is mentioned from the two companions' statements in the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam is this, as we have indicated, and in al-Hidayah: The most correct opinion is that they both hold the metaphorical interpretation, so he violates absolutely. Then there is no basis for this objection against them, except that some understood that they both said, by generality, that violation occurs by what is made or by scooping, but not by the actual grain; and by the actual grain according to him. The author first transmitted this statement and then objected, and then corrected that violation is absolute. This has become clear to you from this discussion: that the more appropriate is what they (the two disciples) said. And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is not consistent on this principle. Allah knows best the truth of what His righteous servants are upon.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage analyzes how ambiguous terms in oaths are interpreted when the swearer has no specific intention. The example is 'I do not drink from the Euphrates nor eat wheat.' The author presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Hanifa takes the literal meaning: 'drinking from the Euphrates' means drinking directly by dipping (kar'), and 'wheat' means the actual grain. The disciples take a more customary interpretation: 'Euphrates' means its water by any method, and 'wheat' means what is normally made from it (bread, etc.). The disciples also hold that processing into a different substance (like sawiq) avoids the oath because it is no longer wheat. Then a distinction is introduced: when the wheat is non-specific ('wheat' in general), there is consensus that eating bread violates; when specific ('this wheat'), there is dispute: Abu Hanifa still insists on the literal grain, while the disciples apply the same customary interpretation. The author then argues that the distinction is arbitrary, and claiming that all cases are covered by a shared custom leads to the conclusion that the swearer should always violate. He quotes from al-Hidayah that the correct view is that the disciples used metaphorical extension (majaz) and that violation is absolute. The author concludes that the disciples' view is more appropriate and notes that Abu Hanifa himself was not consistent in always taking the literal meaning.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa): The literal, primary meaning of a word as originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or extended meaning of a word, used when the literal is not intended.
عرف (urf): Common custom or usual practice among people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not drink from the Euphrates' and then drinks from a canal fed by the Euphrates, does he violate his oath?
A) Yes, because he drank water originally from the Euphrates.
B) No, because the water is no longer attributed to the Euphrates in common usage.
C) Yes, because the literal meaning includes any water originating from the Euphrates.
D) No, because he had no intention to include canals.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when an oath contains ambiguous terms and no intention, the Sahibayn's reliance on customary usage (urf) is the preferred method, leading to violation in most cases.

When the Literal Meaning is Abandoned: Causes and Examples
Explains four cases where literal meaning is abandoned with oath examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes for abandoning the literal meaning (haqiqa) and apply them to classify oath scenarios as per Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The literal meaning is default but abandoned for impossibility, difficulty, or disuse—then the customary meaning governs.

In legal interpretation, words are generally taken in their literal sense. However, Hanafi usul recognizes that sometimes the literal meaning cannot hold due to intellectual impossibility, customary impossibility, extreme difficulty, or disuse by custom or law. This lesson explores these exceptions with examples from oaths, illustrating how the intended meaning shifts to what is customary or legally recognized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of abandonment
Distinguish whether the literal meaning is intellectually impossible, customarily impossible, difficult, or disused.
2
Analyze the example in the text
For each cause, examine the given example (e.g., 'you are my eldest son by age' for intellectual impossibility).
3
Determine the applicable meaning
Apply the Hanafi principle: if literal is impossible/difficult/disused, the intended meaning is what is customary or legally recognized.
4
Apply to oath scenarios
For each example, state the ruling: does the oath make the person guilty? Under what condition?
5
Note the role of custom and law
Recognize that 'urf (custom) and shari'ah can disuse a literal meaning, and the ruling changes with custom.

الحقيقة تترك لتعذرها عقلاً) كأنت ابني الأكبر سناً، وهذا القسم لا يكاد يوجد عندهما (أو) لتعذرها (عادة) وإن جاز عقلاً (كلا يأكل من هذا القدر) فإنه محال في العادة (فلما يحلها) أي فينعقد لما يحل القدر، والأظهر يحله ( أو ) تترك (التعسرها) وإن لم يكن متعذراً (كمن الشجرة) أي لا يأكل منها، فإن أكل عين الشجرة متعسر ، وإن أمكن (ف) يحنث (لما يخرج) منه (مأكولاً) كالثمر والشيرج وإن لم يأكل شيء منه فعلى ثمنه (أو) تترك الهجرها عادة، وإن سهل كمن الدقيق أي لا يأكل منه (فلما) الدقيق متخذ (له) أي لأجله كالخبز وغيره، ولو تكلف وأكل عين الدقيق فقيل : لا يحنث لأنه سقط اعتبارها وقيل يحنث، لأن الحقيقة لا تسقط بحال، فعندهم ههنا عموم المجاز، قال الإمام فخر الإسلام الأول أشبه، ثم إن فخر الإسلام

الكلام، وليس فيه تخفيف (وقد يتعذران) أي الحقيقة والمجاز (فيلغو كبنتي لزوجته الثابت (نسبها أما تعذر الحقيقة فظاهر، وأما تعذر المجاز (فلا يقع الطلاق للمنافاة بين تحريم النسب وتحريم النكاح) وهو الطلاق، فإن الأول تحريم مؤبد مناف للنكاح بخلاف الثاني، فإنه حادث وأثر من آثاره، وأما التحريم المؤبد فليس إثباته في وسعه بخلاف الحرية من ابتداء الملك، وإنما لا يحمل على التشبيه كما نقل عن علماء البيان حتى يكون ظهاراً ، لأن كونه تشبيهاً لا ينبغي أن يقال به، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة الاستقرائية أن المقصود من هذا الكلام استعارة أو تجوز في الإسناد، والأول قد بطل، والثاني لا يفيده فائدة شرعية، إذ لا يلزم منه الظهار إن نوى هذا التحريم المؤبد يجب أن يكون يميناً بل إن قصد به التحريم مطلقاً فهو يمين، كما في تحريم الأمة فإن تحريم الحلال يمين هذا والله سبحانه عليم بأحكامه . قال المصنف : (أقول : لو نوى الطلاق من تحريم الوطء اللازم لموجب اللفظ) المفهوم تبعاً له ( كاليمين) المنوي (من) لازم موجب النذر) هل يقع أم لا) يقع (فافهم) فإنه إن كفى هذه الإرادة فالوقوع للطلاق لازم ههنا ، وإلا فلا يتم كلامكم هناك، وقد عرفت أن الكلام على هذا النحو لا يتم هناك أيضاً.
يقع
أن
Issue: The literal meaning is abandoned due to its impossibility intellectually, like 'You are my eldest son by age.' This category hardly exists among them (the two disciples). Or due to its impossibility customarily, even if intellectually possible, like 'He does not eat from this pot' — for it is impossible customarily. So (the oath is understood) as what dissolves (the pot's contents)? i.e., it is directed to what dissolves the pot? The more apparent is that it is directed to what dissolves it. Or it is abandoned due to its difficulty, even if not impossible, like 'He does not eat from the tree' — i.e., he does not eat from it. For eating the tree itself is difficult, even if possible. So he violates by what comes out from it as eatable, like fruit and sesame oil, and if he does not eat anything from it, he is obligated for its price. Or it is abandoned due to its disuse in custom, even if easy, like 'He does not eat flour' — i.e., he does not eat from it. So (the oath is understood) as what the flour is made for, i.e., bread and other things. And if he makes effort and eats the actual flour, it is said: he does not violate because its consideration has fallen away; and it is said: he violates, because the literal meaning never falls away. Then according to them here is general metaphor. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said the first is more appropriate. Then Fakhr al-Islam included these examples under difficulty; perhaps he took a broader meaning than difficulty and similar. And he gave an example for disuse like 'He does not place his foot in so-and-so's house.' And if the disuse is due to custom, then the ruling changes with its change, which is apparent. Or due to its disuse by law (shar'), because what is disused by law is like what is disused by custom; for a Muslim's custom is only what he takes from the law. So he does not violate by adultery in his oath 'He will not marry a foreign woman' — for 'marriage' is understood as the contract, not the act of intercourse, which is its literal meaning in language, because it is disused by law. Except with intention, because he intended what the speech can carry, and there is no leniency. And both (literal and metaphorical) may be impossible, so they become void, like 'You are my daughter' said to his wife whose lineage is established. As for the literal meaning being impossible, it is apparent. As for the metaphorical being impossible, then divorce does not occur due to the contradiction between prohibiting lineage and prohibiting marriage, which is divorce. For the first is an eternal prohibition contradictory to marriage, unlike the second, which is occasional and an effect of it. As for the eternal prohibition, its establishment is not in his capacity, unlike freedom from the beginning of ownership. And it is not taken as a simile as transmitted from the scholars of rhetoric so that it would be zihar. Because it being a simile should not be said; for we know by necessary induction that the purpose of this speech is metaphor or figurative attribution. The first (simile) is invalid, and the second (figurative attribution) gives no legal benefit, for neither zihar nor divorce follows from it by claiming union between this daughter and the wife. The utmost that follows from it is an eternal prohibition, which is not in his capacity. Unlike what we said about 'my son', for emancipation from the moment of ownership is in his capacity. Reflect on this. As for the scholars of rhetoric, their intention is not a simile with its tool estimated, because they say it is an eloquent simile. If the tool is estimated and the estimated is like the mentioned, then there is no difference between it and what the tool is mentioned in. Also, the i'rab is not remaining, so what is the clue for estimation? And these people of great skill in literary sciences, how can they say such a statement? For as Shaykh 'Abd al-Qahir said: poetry descends to something degraded. Rather, their meaning is that there is a figurative attribution ('aqlī majāz). So attributing 'lion' to Zayd and claiming that he is he is an exaggeration in the simile that is considered mentally, not that the simile is the meaning of the speech. Reflect. And we do not mean by 'void' that it has no meaning at all, so that it could be objected that the invalidity of figurative usage does not entail invalidity of other figurative uses; for it could be affection or the like. Rather, we mean that it has no meaning that results in a legal ruling. Then the speech is not free from something: why is it not permissible that it intends divorce by the common factor of the very prohibition and the notoriety of the daughter's prohibition? Do you not see that dullness in the dull person is of one kind and in the donkey of another kind, even though it is used figuratively? And if you examine instances of metaphor, you will be certain that sharing in the common factor from all aspects is not necessary. And in this expression there is a kind of desire for exaggeration, so it should be irrevocable divorce (talāq bā'in). And we may also say: If he intends this eternal prohibition, it must be an oath (yamīn); nay, if he intends prohibition absolutely, it is an oath, as in the prohibition of a slave-woman, for prohibiting the lawful is an oath. This, and Allah knows His rulings. The author said: I say: If he intends divorce from the prohibition of intercourse necessitated by the implication of the wording, understood implicitly, like the intended oath (s) from the necessary consequence of the cause of a vow, does it occur or not? It occurs (fafham). For if this intention suffices, then the occurrence of divorce is necessitated here, otherwise your statement there is not complete. And you have known that speech in this manner is not complete there either. Occurs (yaqa') that (an).
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of when the literal meaning (haqiqa) of a word is abandoned due to various reasons: impossibility (intellectual or customary), difficulty, or disuse (by custom or law). It provides examples: 'You are my eldest son by age' (intellectually impossible if the addressee is not a son), 'he does not eat from this pot' (customarily impossible because one eats the contents, not the pot), 'he does not eat from the tree' (difficult to eat the tree itself, so the oath applies to its fruit), and 'he does not eat flour' (disused custom—flour is typically eaten as bread). Then it addresses the scenario where both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible, illustrated by a man telling his wife 'You are my daughter.' The literal meaning is impossible because she is not his daughter; the metaphorical meaning (e.g., implying she is permanently forbidden like a daughter) is also problematic because it does not legally produce divorce or zihar. The text engages with scholars of rhetoric who might interpret it as a simile (tashbīh), but the author argues it is rather a figurative attribution with no legal effect. However, the author also suggests that if the speaker intends divorce or a permanent prohibition, it could be effective as an oath. The final part contains a comment by the author (al-musannif) on the scenario of intending divorce through the implication of the wording. The text is dense and includes many polemics, but the core principle is that when the literal meaning is impossible, difficult, or disused, the listener must consider the customary or legal application of the term.

Key Terms
تعذر (ta'azzur): Impossibility, either intellectually or customarily.
تعسر (ta'assur): Extreme difficulty, short of impossibility.
هجر (hajr): Disuse of the literal meaning due to custom or law (shar').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'He does not eat flour' (لا يأكل الدقيق), what is the ruling if he eats the flour itself according to the preferred view?
A) He violates the oath because the literal meaning applies.
B) He does not violate the oath because the literal meaning is abandoned due to disuse.
C) The oath is void because both literal and metaphorical are impossible.
D) He must pay the price of the flour to avoid violation.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning is abandoned for impossibility, difficulty, or disuse; the intended meaning is then determined by custom or law.

When Both Meanings Are Impossible: You Are My Daughter
Examines a problematic oath where both literal and metaphorical are impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a case where both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible and evaluate the legal consequences (void or oath) according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
When literal and metaphorical are both impossible, the speech yields no legal effect unless intention revives it.

A man says to his wife 'You are my daughter.' Literally impossible, and metaphorically impossible as a permanent prohibition (zihar/divorce) because it would be contradictory. Hanafi scholars explore whether this statement can be interpreted as an oath, a divorce, or simply void. This lesson dissects the intricate arguments and distinguishes the 'bint' case from the 'ibni' case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two impossibilities
Literal: she is not his biological daughter, so the word 'bint' cannot apply. Metaphorical: claiming eternal prohibition (like divorce) is not in his power and contradicts marriage; thus metaphorical meaning also fails.
The text says 'تعذر الحقيقة فظاهر' and 'تعذر المجاز فلا يقع الطلاق للمنافاة'.
2
Consider the rhetorical perspective
The scholars of rhetoric might see it as a simile (tashbih), but the author refutes this: it is figurative attribution (majaz 'aqli) with no legal benefit.
The author discusses the views of 'ulama' al-bayan.
3
Compare with the 'ibni' example
In 'You are my son', the metaphorical meaning (emancipation) is possible and legally effective. Here, permanent prohibition is not in his capacity.
The text explicitly contrasts: 'بخلاف ما قلنا في ابني فإن الإعتاق من حين الملك في وسعه'.
4
Examine the role of intention
If the speaker intends divorce or permanent prohibition, it could be effective as an oath or divorce. The author considers both possibilities.
The text says 'إن نوى هذا التحريم المؤبد يجب أن يكون يميناً' and the final part discusses intention and implication.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi ruling
Default: no legal effect. With intention: it can become an oath (yamin) or possibly a divorce. The author's comment suggests nuance.
The final part by al-musannif is complex and cut off, but the core principle is clear.
