خفي أدير الحكم على دليله ،هذا قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية لتوجيه المقام إن من الألفاظ ما لا يكون سبباً للحكم، بل هو ثابت في نفسه أو منتف كالأقارير، فإنها لا تلزم الأحكام بل تدل على المصداق، وهو متحقق في نفسه بسبب، ومنها ما هو سبب لحكم خارجي لولا تلك الألفاظ لم يكن هناك، والمولى المحقق لا ينكر اعتبار المعاني رأساً، بل مقصود قدس سره أن هذه الألفاظ من القبيلة الثانية وأنها بعد إقامتها مقام المعاني ومن حيث دلالتها عليها أسباب خارجية لأحكام خارجية، مثل سببية القتل للموت لا أن شأنها شأن الإخبارات وليس لها معان متحققة في الخارج كما في الإخبارات، بل هي أنفسها معان يمكن أن يخبر عنها ويدل عليها، وإنما يقصد بهذه الألفاظ وقوع آثار خارجية لا إفادة معان ذهنية، ولم يقصد هذا المحقق قدس سره أن هذه الألفاظ أنفسها من حيث هي أصوات أسباب كيف وحينئذٍ يلزم وقوع طلاق المعتوه عنده ولم يقل هو به، وهذا كلام متين غاية المتانة، لكن بقي فيه إلى الآن نوع من الخفاء، فإن صيغ العقود والفسوخ كما إخبارات فهي إنما تدل على طلاق واقع بسببه الذي هو تطليق من الزوج ثابت اقتضاء مثلا، فحال هذه الصيغ وحال الأقارير سواء في عدم كون شيء منها أسباباً، نعم: يصح هذا على قول من يقول بكونها إنشاءات إلى أن يقال : هب أن الأمر كذلك إن شدد، لكن لما كان سبب وقوع الأحكام المخبر عنها بهذه العقود مدلولاً لهذه الصيغ اقتضاء ، ولا يمكن حصول العلم بتحققه إلا بهذه الصيغ فأقيمت هذه الصيغ ،مقامها فالأثر الحاصل به حاصل بها، فقيل : قد قصد منها آثار خارجية، وهذا بخلاف الأقارير، فإنها إنما تدل على المخبر عنه الثابت بسبب يمكن العلم بها من غير وساطة هذه الألفاظ هذا غاية الكلام في هذا المقام، ولا يبعد يقال : إن الذي وقع في عبارة المولى المحقق أن سببية الإعتاق لإثبات القوة مثل سببية خارجي للأثر الخارجي، فيمكن أن يراد به أن ألفاظ العقود والفسوخ التي لا يؤثر فيها الهزل والإكراه ولا يشترط فيها الرضا سببيتها كسببية أسباب خارجية لآثارها الخارجية وذلك لأن بوقوع طلاق الهازل والخاطىء عرف أنه غير متوقف على الرضا والقصد إلى المعنى، كما في ،، سييء أن سبب
Second benefit: It is said that these words, i.e., the legal formulas of contracts and dissolutions, are external causes for rulings, on the example of the causality of killing for death in the external world. They intend the occurrence of the ruling, which is intended by the creation through the way of intending a specific external effect from an external cause, not by intending their meanings that affect the rulings. Thus these words have no meaning that is considered at the time of effecting; rather, they themselves are external meanings that can be intended to be indicated by other words. He said in the Hashiyah: This statement has been transmitted by some students of the martyr investigator Qutb al-Din al-Sahali, may his secret be sanctified. And I think it is far from him, and Allah knows best the truth. End. I say: That is an astonishing thing, for then how can metaphorical usage from them be valid? Since they have no meaning, from what thing is one transferred to something else? And how can these words be described as (literal) since they are indicating words? And how can the combination between them (literal and metaphorical) be required, while they require it in places and extricate themselves? And how can suspension (ta'liq) be accepted, for it is a characteristic of meanings? And how can it be true religiously (diyanatan) if the opposite of the apparent is intended? Since they have no meaning, the intention becomes invalid, and there is no apparent whose opposite is intended. And other such corruptions. Rather, the truth is that consideration is for the meaning first and primarily in the causality of the ruling, which is internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). Then, due to its hiddenness, the ruling is made to rotate upon its indicator, which is the word, existentially and non-existentially. For a hidden matter is not built upon for the ruling due to impossibility, like the dispensation of travel. The actual basis for dispensation is hardship, but since it is not fixed and the considered amount is subtle, the ruling is made to rotate upon its indicator. This is what is said in Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya to orient the station: Some words are not a cause for the ruling, but the ruling is established in itself or negated, like acknowledgments (iqrarat), for they do not necessitate rulings but indicate the reality, which is realized in itself by a cause. And some words are a cause for an external ruling that would not exist without those words. The Master Investigator does not deny the consideration of meanings entirely, but his purpose—may his secret be sanctified—is that these words are of the second group, and that after being established in place of meanings and by virtue of indicating them, they are external causes for external rulings, like the causality of killing for death, not that their affair is like reports and they have no realized meanings in the external as in reports. Rather, they themselves are meanings that can be reported about and indicated. Only external effects are intended by these words, not the communication of mental meanings. And this investigator did not intend that these words themselves, insofar as they are sounds, are causes; how [could that be]? For then the divorce of an insane person would occur according to him, but he did not say that. This speech is extremely sound. But there remains some obscurity up to now: For the formulas of contracts and dissolutions, like reports, only indicate a divorce that occurs by its cause, which is the husband's repudiation (tatliq) subsisting as a necessary implication (iqtida'), for example. So the state of these formulas and the state of acknowledgments are equal in that none of them are causes. Yes, this is valid according to one who says they are creations (insha'at). Until it is said: Suppose it is so, even if intensified, but since the cause of occurrence of the rulings reported by these contracts is indicated by these formulas by way of implication, and knowledge of its realization cannot be obtained except through these formulas, these formulas are established in their place. So the effect obtained by it is obtained by them, and it is said: External effects are intended from them. This is contrary to acknowledgments, for they only indicate the reported matter that is established by a cause that can be known without the mediation of these words. This is the gist of the discussion in this station. It is not unlikely to say: That which occurred in the expression of the Master Investigator is that the causality of manumission (i'taq) for establishing power (al-quwwa) is like the external causality for the external effect. So it can be intended that the words of contracts and dissolutions, in which jest and coercion have no effect and consent is not a condition, their causality is like the causality of external causes for their external effects. That is because, by the occurrence of the divorce of the jester and the mistaken person, it is known that it is not dependent on consent and intention of the meaning, as in ... and ..
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical discussion (faa'idah) about the nature of the verbal formulas (sigha) used in contracts (uqud) and dissolutions (fusukh), such as 'I divorce', 'I free', etc. The issue: are these words just external causes (asbab kharijiyya) that directly produce the legal effect, like killing causes death, independent of their internal meaning? Or do they have meanings that are the true cause? The text reports a view (attributed to some, possibly Qutb al-Din al-Sahali) that these words have no meaning; they are themselves external meanings and are not signs of internal concepts. The author strongly refutes this, pointing out absurd consequences: if they have no meaning, how can metaphorical usage (tajawuz) exist? How can they be literal (haqiqa) or figurative? How can ta'liq (suspension of divorce on condition) be valid, which relies on meaning? How can one intend the contrary of the apparent (khilaf al-zahir) religiously? The author asserts the truth (al-haqq): the primary consideration is for the meaning (al-ma'na), which is internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). Then, because that meaning is hidden, the ruling is made to revolve around its external indicator, the utterance (al-lafz). This is analogous to the dispensation of travel (rukhsat al-safar): the true cause is hardship (mashaqqa), but since hardship is not precisely determinable, the ruling is attached to the apparent cause (travel itself). The passage then quotes Matali' al-Asrar to refine the position: there are two types of words: those that merely indicate a pre-existing reality (like acknowledgments, iqrar), and those that are causes for external rulings that would not exist without them (like contracts and dissolutions). The investigator (al-Muhaqqiq) does not deny meaning entirely; he means that these words, by establishing themselves in place of meaning and indicating it, become external causes. However, they are not mere sounds; if they were, then even an insane person's utterance would cause divorce, which is not accepted. The author then adds his own analysis: these formulas are like reports (ikhbarat) in that they indicate a divorce that is realized by its cause (the husband's will), and thus they are not independent causes; they are like iqrar. But then he nuances: since the cause is necessarily implied by the formula, and knowledge of it is only through the formula, the formula is treated as the cause in effect. The final part discusses the difference with iqrar: in iqrar, the fact acknowledged can be known by other means, whereas in contracts, the only way to know the cause is through the words. The passage ends with a note that jest and mistake circumstances show that these words act like external causes independent of internal consent.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي (al-kalām al-nafsī): Internal speech, the conceptual meaning in the mind that is the true locus of intention.
الأسباب الخارجية (al-asbāb al-khārijiyya): External causes that directly produce effects, like killing causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary cause of legal rulings in contracts?
A) The external utterance itself.
B) The internal meaning (al-kalam al-nafsi).
C) The judge's fatwa.
D) Neither – rulings are based solely on apparent words.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the internal meaning (al-kalam al-nafsi) is the true cause of legal rulings; the utterance is an indicator adopted due to the hiddenness of meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Meaning of Waw: Sequence or Absolute Conjunction?
Debate over the linguistic import of the Arabic waw (and).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position that 'waw' denotes absolute conjunction and apply this principle to derive rulings from Quranic texts and fiqh examples.

Core Idea
The Arabic 'and' (waw) does not impose sequence — it is purely conjunctive; order must come from external clues.

This passage dives into a foundational usul dispute: does the simple particle 'waw' carry any implication of order? The Hanafi school, relying on linguistic consensus and Quranic evidence, firmly holds that it does not — a position with direct consequences for how we interpret legal texts and contracts. We will examine the evidence, address powerful counterexamples, and see why the Hanafi view prevails despite some dissenting grammarians.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds waw is for absolute conjunction (jam' muṭlaq) with no inherent sequence. Note the reliance on consensus of linguists like Sibawayh.
Some grammarians dissented, but the Hanafi view treats their opposition as negligible.
2
Analyze the apodosis argument
Observe that waw is invalid in the apodosis (jazā') of conditional sentences, unlike fa which indicates sequence. This shows waw cannot imply order.
The argument is that if waw indicated sequence, it would be valid in apodosis like fa.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Compare Surah al-Baqarah 2:58 (enter prostrating then say repentance) with al-A'raf 7:161 (reverse order). Since both describe the same event, if waw indicated sequence, one would be false.
This is a strong proof of waw's pure conjunctive meaning.
4
Address the fiqh counterexamples
The text shows that apparent sequence in divorce and marriage cases is due to external factors (loss of subject, modification) not waw itself.
Understanding these examples deepens the application of the rule.
5
Apply the principle
Conclude that in legal texts, waw does not create order unless a separate indicator exists. This affects interpretation of contracts and oaths.
For example, saying 'I married X and Y' without context does not imply sequence.

Difference of opinion. Fawatih al-Rahmut sharh Muslim al-Thubut part one. One, so reflect. For us is transmission from the imams of language that it (waw) is for absolute conjunction, and among them is Sibawayh, and it has been repeated from him until consensus was transmitted. And among the transmitters of consensus are al-Sirafi, al-Suhayli, and al-Farisi. And it was objected that Tha'lab, Qutrub, Hisham, Abu Ja'far al-Dinawari, and Abu 'Amr al-Zahid opposed this, as in some commentaries of al-Minhaj. Therefore the author presented it in the passive voice, and perhaps the transmitter intended the consensus of the majority and disregarding the opponent because the matter is clear and not open to dispute, so reflect. And for us also is its invalidity in the apodosis (jazā'), for if it indicated sequence it would be valid in the apodosis like the fa', because fa' indicates sequence and is valid in the apodosis. And the entailment is prevented as in al-Tahrir, using thumma as evidence, for it indicates sequence and is not valid in the apodosis. I say: refuted because delay (tarākhī) in waw has not been asserted by anyone. So if it contained sequence, it would be either without respite or absolutely with or without respite. Then it would be valid in the apodosis and would not be like thumma, since thumma has respite which contradicts the nature of the apodosis. And this is insufficient, for it does not follow from the two letters being equal in meaning that they are equal in the validity of annexation, and the removal of reliance on thumma does not remove the prevention, so ponder. And they argued for the chosen view by the impossibility of contradiction in the precedence of prostration over the saying 'hitta' as in Surah al-Baqarah: 'And when We said, Enter this city and eat from it wherever you will in abundance, and enter the gate prostrating and say, 'Repentance'' and the reverse, meaning the precedence of 'saying repentance' over prostration as in Surah al-A'raf: 'And say, 'Repentance' and enter the gate prostrating' while the story is one in both surahs. So if waw indicated sequence, contradiction would necessarily occur. And by the impossibility of the expressions 'Zayd and 'Amr fought each other' and 'Zayd and 'Amr came, the former before the latter' – i.e., they argued by the impossibility of these two constructions on the assumption that waw indicates sequence, otherwise contradiction would occur, and they are certainly correct. They said it is not for sequence but for absolute conjunction. And repetition after it – i.e., they argued by the necessity of repetition in expressions like 'Zayd and 'Amr came after him' on the assumption that it indicates sequence, and this is not considered repetition, so it is known that it is not for sequence. And it was answered regarding the three aspects by the permissibility of figurative use in waw, for it is for sequence in the original sense, and here it is used figuratively for conjunction. We said: figurative use is contrary to the principle, so it is not resorted to except with evidence indicating that the literal meaning is something else, and there is no such evidence, so the aspects are validated. And a counterexample was presented against waw being for absolute conjunction: first, his saying to one who has not been entered: 'You are divorced and divorced' results in one divorce according to us, just as if it were with fa' and thumma. And if sequence were not understood from waw, she would have been divorced by one. And the answer is that the one divorce is not because of sequence understood from waw, but rather due to the loss of the subject before the second utterance because of the succession of the two utterances and there is no modifier that requires the first to be dependent on the second. Rather, the rule of the first utterance is established at the time of its utterance, and the subject is lost. Then if the rule of the second were established, it would be established at the time of its utterance, and its time is after the time of the first in which the subject was lost, so it would be futile. And waw has no role in this. That is because the principle in constative speech is that the judgment is not separate from it at all, except for an impediment like a condition and similar modifiers. If you say: It has been reported from Imam Muhammad that it only occurs after completion of the last, so the succession in occurrence is not due to succession in utterance. He answered: And what is reported from Imam Muhammad that it occurs after completion of the last is interpreted as knowledge of it, i.e., of occurrence, because until he finishes, the possibility of a modifier exists, so knowledge is not obtained. And for this reason – that occurrence in constative speech does not lag behind utterance – the marriage of the second slave woman is invalidated in the third article on linguistic principles, his saying: 'This one is free, and this one upon reaching' regarding a guardian marrying off his two slave women to one man in one contract because the first becomes free and her marriage is effective before the second, so the suspended marriage of the second is invalid from the outset due to the prohibition of marrying a slave woman over a free woman. And it may be objected that the prohibition of marrying a slave woman over a free woman is only initially, not in continuance. How so? If he marries two slave women in one contract and then one is freed, the other's marriage is not invalidated. And initially, the state of inception and suspension is considered; at that time they are both slave women. If the state of effectiveness is considered, they are both free, so there is no basis for invalidity. But you may say that marriage in reality is the effective one, for the suspended one is on the verge of becoming marriage; how could it be when it does not permit what marriage was legislated for? So it is marriage in one sense not another. When the first is freed, her marriage takes effect and she is free, so the other no longer remains a subject for the inception of marriage; rather, she joins the prohibited women as long as she is a slave woman and this free woman is under him. So the suspended contract is invalidated and does not take effect with the occurrence of freedom because what is invalidated does not return. Then it remains that according to this, the marriage of the second should not be invalidated upon marriage without the husband's permission and suspension of his approval, for the first's marriage is also suspended, and it does not remove the subject nor invalidate the other suspended one. And a distinction is made: it is said that the second's marriage is invalidated if the husband approves the first's marriage before her freeing, otherwise not. And it appears from al-Taysir that he commits to it, for he said: if by this freeing the first's marriage is not completed and remains suspended upon her approval or the approval of her guardian, then the second's marriage is not invalidated. But this commitment is not regarded, for Imam Fakhr al-Islam said in explaining the form of this issue, quoting from al-Jami': 'He married off two slave women to a man without the permission of their master and without the permission of the husband.' End. And in al-Kashf, he said: 'If he frees them in two separate words or connected, the second's marriage is invalidated, and the first's marriage remains suspended upon the husband's approval.' And this is not fully clear except if you understand what is missing from you: that the marriage of a free woman, even if suspended, removes the slave woman from the domain of inception of marriage, rather she becomes among the prohibited women, but temporarily. And what is not a subject for the inception of a contract is not a subject for approval and effectiveness because it is analogous to inception, since by it what marriage was legislated for is realized. This is the utmost of speech, so reflect. And a second counterexample was presented: his saying: 'I approve the marriage of so-and-so and so-and-so' in the case of a guardian marrying off two sisters from the husband's side in two contracts, and if they were in one contract, it would not be suspended but invalid from the outset. From him, i.e., from the husband, he may approve one contract alternatively, not both together, lest he become a husband of two sisters. And this form is presented as a counterexample because both marriages are invalidated, as if he said: 'I approve their marriage.' So then it is known that it (waw) is for simultaneity, otherwise the first marriage would be valid and the second not. And the answer is that this is not because the meaning of waw is accompaniment, but rather because the speech is suspended until its end. If at its end there is a modifier that changes the first from validity to invalidity, for example, even if it is a modifier by annexation (bi-damm), the modifier operates and the entire speech becomes like one speech. Otherwise, if there is no modifier, the rule of the speech is established from the time of its existence as passed in the divorce case. And in our case here, the second marriage modifies the first marriage from validity to invalidity, so the first part of the speech is suspended until its end, and the rule of both is established together. So 'I approved the marriage of this and this' becomes like 'I approved their marriage,' for this reason, not because waw indicates simultaneity. I say: If we settle this, then what is in al-Tahrir is repelled: that the invalidator is the simultaneous annexation like 'I married them both' or 'I approved them both,' and that is not present here. There is no ordered annexation in wording, even if it exists in meaning, because it is dependent on suspension, i.e., because the invalidity is due to the modifier, not the waw.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh debate on the meaning of the Arabic conjunction 'wa' (و). The issue is whether 'wa' inherently indicates sequence (tartīb) or merely absolute conjunction (mutlaq al-jam') without any order. The Hanafi position, following the majority of linguists like Sibawayh, is that 'wa' is for absolute conjunction. The passage presents evidence and counterarguments.

First, the author cites consensus from leading linguists that 'wa' denotes absolute conjunction, though some like Tha'lab dissented. The author notes that the opposing view is weak. Then a proof is given: if 'wa' indicated sequence, it would be valid in the apodosis (jazā') of conditional sentences like 'fa' is, but it is not; and like 'thumma' which indicates sequence but is invalid in the apodosis. So 'wa' cannot indicate sequence. The objection that 'thumma' has delay is answered: even if 'wa' had sequence without delay, it would be valid in apodosis, but it is not, so the argument stands.

Further proof is drawn from the Quran: in Surah al-Baqarah, the order is 'enter prostrating and say repentance,' while in al-A'raf it is 'say repentance and enter prostrating.' Since both describe the same event, if 'wa' indicated sequence, one of the verses would be contradictory, which is impossible. Therefore 'wa' does not indicate sequence. Additional linguistic examples like 'Zayd and 'Amr fought each other' and 'Zayd and 'Amr came, the former before the latter' are grammatically sound, but if 'wa' indicated sequence, they would be problematic. Thus 'wa' is for absolute conjunction.

The opponent's response that 'wa' can be used figuratively is dismissed because figurative use requires evidence, which is absent. The default is literal meaning.

Then the text addresses two fiqh counterexamples that seem to suggest sequence in 'wa'. The first: if a man says to his wife 'you are divorced and divorced,' in Hanafi fiqh only one divorce occurs (if she is not yet entered), just as if he said 'you are divorced fa' or 'thumma divorced.' This might imply that 'wa' does not add a second divorce due to lack of sequential effect. But the answer clarifies that the one divorce is not because 'wa' indicates no sequence; rather, it is because the subject (the wife's divorceability) is lost after the first utterance. The second divorce is impossible because the marriage is already dissolved. This is a matter of timing and not due to 'wa'.

The second counterexample involves a guardian marrying off two slave women to one man without permission. If he frees the first before the husband's approval, the second marriage is invalid because a free woman cannot be married alongside a slave woman. Some argue this implies sequence. But the answer shows that the invalidity arises from the modifying effect of the second contract on the first, not from the conjunction. The discussion includes detailed fiqh rulings on suspended marriages.

Finally, a second counterexample about approving the marriage of two sisters in two contracts: if the husband says 'I approve the marriage of this and this,' both are invalid because he cannot have two sisters simultaneously. The author argues this is not because 'wa' indicates simultaneity but because the speech is suspended until the end, and the second modifies the first. The invalidity is due to the modifier, not 'wa'.

Overall, the passage firmly establishes the Hanafi position that 'wa' is purely conjunctive without any implication of order.

Key Terms
الواو (al-wāw): The Arabic conjunction 'and' – according to the Hanafi position, it denotes absolute conjunction (jam' muṭlaq) without any implication of sequence or order.
الترتيب (al-tartīb): Sequence or order – the property that some conjunctions (like fa and thumma) impose on the connected elements, indicating that one occurs after another.
الجمع المطلق (al-jam' al-muṭlaq): Absolute conjunction – joining elements without any implication of order, simultaneity, or precedence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the primary meaning of the conjunction waw (و)?
A) Sequence (tartīb) without delay.
B) Sequence with delay (tarākhī).
C) Absolute conjunction (jam' muṭlaq) without sequence.
D) Simultaneity (muqārana).

Key Takeaway
The conjunction waw in Arabic carries no implication of sequence; it merely indicates conjunction, a principle with direct implications for interpreting legal texts and contracts.

The Conjunction 'Wa' and Its Effect on Compound Utterances in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing the conjunction 'wa' in a suspended marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that the conjunction 'wa' does not imply simultaneity, and apply it to determine the validity of a suspended contract when the second part changes the ruling.

Core Idea
The conjunction 'wa' does not necessitate simultaneity; the ruling of the whole depends on the effect of the latter part.

In this passage, a complex fiqh case about marrying two slave women becomes a springboard for a deep usul principle: how to interpret the conjunction 'wa' in compound legal utterances. The Hanafi school argues that 'wa' does not necessarily indicate simultaneity; rather, the later part of a statement can retroactively change the ruling of the earlier part, similar to the case of divorce. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning and its implications for suspended contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fiqh case and its initial ruling
The case: an unauthorized guardian marries two slave women to one man in a single contract. One slave woman is freed; her marriage becomes effective, and the other marriage is void from the start. Understand the facts and the Hanafi ruling.
2
State the objection based on the meaning of 'wa'
Objectors claim that if the contract was made with 'wa' (and), it implies simultaneity, so both marriages should be considered together. They argue that at the time of the contract, both were slaves, so the subsequent freeing should not retroactively invalidate the second.
This objection challenges the ruling by focusing on the linguistic meaning of the conjunction.
3
Explain the Hanafi response using the principle of taghyir (change)
The Hanafi response: the ruling depends on whether the latter part of the statement changes the former. If it does, the whole is treated as one utterance, and the effect is determined by the latter part. In this case, the second marriage changes the first from valid to void, so the 'wa' does not indicate simultaneity.
The principle is: 'al-kalam muwaqqaf 'ala akhirihi' (the statement is suspended until its end).
4
Relate to the broader usul rule about compound statements
This principle extends beyond this case: in any compound statement joined by 'wa', if the second part changes the ruling of the first, the whole is considered as a single unit. The 'wa' itself is neutral; it does not carry a meaning of order or simultaneity.
This is a key Hanafi position in usul al-fiqh, contrasting with views that assign a specific meaning to 'wa'.
5
Apply to the example of authorization of marriage of two sisters
If a man says 'I authorize the marriage of these two sisters' (with 'wa'), the Hanafi view is that he may validate only one, because the second changes the first (making it incestuous). The 'wa' does not force simultaneity; the latter part invalidates the first.
This illustrates the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): the difference in ruling based on the interpretation of 'wa'.

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a hypothetical marriage scenario. The author explains a ruling from the Hanafi school: if an unauthorized guardian marries two slave women to one man in one contract, and then one is freed, the marriage of the freed one becomes effective, voiding the other's marriage from the start. The objection raised is that the prohibition of marrying a slave woman over a free woman applies only initially, not in permanence, and that at the time of the contract both were slave women. The author defends the ruling by arguing that marriage is essentially valid (nāfidh), not suspended; once the first becomes free, the second is no longer a valid subject for marriage. The discussion then shifts to the meaning of the conjunction 'wa' in the phrase 'I authorize the marriage of so-and-so and so-and-so.' The author refutes the claim that 'wa' implies simultaneity, instead arguing that the ruling depends on whether the latter part changes the former, similar to the case of divorce. The analysis involves a critique from the book al-Tahrir and a response, ending with a nuanced classification of types of change (taghyir).

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without authorization, especially in contracts.
النكاح (al-nikāḥ): Marriage contract; in this context, the act of marriage by a guardian.
الواو (al-wāw): The Arabic conjunction 'and'; here debated whether it implies sequence or simultaneity.

Does 'Waw' Imply Sequence? A Hanafi Refutation of Four Proofs
Refuting arguments that the conjunction waw indicates sequential order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that the conjunction 'wa' does not imply sequence (tartib) by analysing four objections and their refutations.

Core Idea
The word 'wa' indicates mere conjunction without sequence; any necessary order must be proven from external evidence like the Sunnah.

This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between scholars who argue that the Arabic conjunction 'wa' inherently implies sequential order and the Hanafi school which holds that 'wa' simply conjoins without any temporal sequence. The proponents of sequence cite four proofs from the Quran, Hadith, and the practice of the Companions. The Hanafi commentator systematically refutes each, showing that the required sequence in those examples is actually derived from other sources such as the Prophet's explicit instructions, not from the word 'wa' itself. This lesson walks through the four proofs and the Hanafi responses, clarifying how linguistic meaning is established in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proofs for sequence
Locate the four arguments presented by the proponents: (1) Quran 22:77 'Bow and prostrate'; (2) Quran 2:158 about Safa and Marwa; (3) the Prophet's instruction to the preacher; (4) the Companions' objection to Ibn Abbas. Note the evidence type for each.
The passage uses 'qālū' (they said) for each proof.
2
Analyse the Hanafi response for each proof
For each proof, identify the Hanafi rebuttal (starting with 'qulnā' – we say). The key argument is that sequence is derived from the Sunnah or other context, not from the word 'wa' itself.
Pay attention to the distinction between linguistic meaning and external evidence.
3
Extract the Hanafi principle regarding 'wa'
From the responses, infer the Hanafi principle: 'wa' indicates absolute conjunction (jam' muṭlaq) without any inherent order. Any additional implication requires external proof.
This principle is consistent with the Hanafi view that the literal meaning of 'wa' is mere association.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider how this linguistic disagreement affects fiqh rulings. For example, when the Quran commands 'establish prayer and give zakat' (2:43), a Hanafi does not infer a sequential order between the two; the Shafi'i may see a recommended or required order in some contexts.
The practical difference lies in the validity of performing conjoined acts in any order without additional evidence.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Take a new Quranic verse with 'wa' (e.g., 'and seek help through patience and prayer' 2:153). According to the Hanafi principle, does the 'wa' imply that patience must come before prayer? No, it is absolute conjunction. Any order would be from the Sunnah.
This step consolidates the ability to apply the principle in istinbat.

The proponents of sequence said: First: Allah said, 'Bow and prostrate' (Surah al-Hajj 22:77), and sequence between them is obligatory with the conjunction by waw, so it is known that it indicates sequence. We say: We do not concede that sequence is understood from this noble verse; rather it is understood from his saying (peace be upon him and his family and companions): 'Pray as you have seen me praying.' So this command guided us to the obligation of sequence... [continues with all four proofs and responses]
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute about whether the conjunction 'wa' (and) implies sequential order (tartīb). The proponents of sequence give four proofs: (1) the verse 'Bow and prostrate' in which sequence is obligatory; (2) the verse about Safa and Marwa where order is required; (3) the Prophet's instruction to the preacher to say 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger' using singular, indicating order; (4) the companions' objection to Ibn Abbas prioritizing Umrah over Hajj based on the verse. The Hanafi commentator refutes each proof, arguing that the obligation of sequence is derived from the Sunnah (the Prophet's practice), not from the word 'wa' itself. The word 'wa' is merely conjunctive and does not by itself imply order. The commentary notes that the proofs from the verses are not valid because sequence is established by other evidence, and the examples (like the preacher) are about emphasis and awe, not sequence. The passage ends with the fourth proof and an incomplete response.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartīb): Sequential order or arrangement, especially in ritual acts like prayer and Hajj.
الواو (al-wāw): The conjunction 'and'; debated whether it indicates order or mere conjunction.
الواو العاطف (al-wāw al-'āṭif): The conjunctive 'wa' connecting two elements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why does the verse 'Bow and prostrate' not prove that 'wa' implies sequence?
A) Because the verse is not authentic.
B) Because the sequence of bowing and prostrating is known from the Sunnah, not from the word 'wa'.
C) Because bowing and prostrating are not sequential acts.
D) Because the word 'wa' in that verse indicates simultaneity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the conjunction 'wa' does not inherently indicate sequence; any obligatory order must come from external sources like the Sunnah.

The Waw and Order (Tartib) in Legal Texts
Debate on whether the conjunctive waw implies order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether the waw implies order and evaluate the four arguments presented by the proponents of order.

Core Idea
The waw does not imply order; order must be established by separate evidence (Sunnah), not the conjunction itself.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the meaning of the conjunctive waw (واو) is a foundational linguistic issue with significant legal consequences. Does the waw imply a sequence between actions, or does it merely join them without order? This lesson explores the Hanafi position that waw indicates absolute conjunction (al-jam' al-mutlaq) and examines four key arguments from proponents of order, along with the Hanafi responses. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to derive rulings from the Quran and Sunnah with precision.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Identify the central question: Does the waw imply order (tartib)? The Hanafi school holds it does not; it is for mere conjunction. This sets the stage for evaluating opposing arguments.
Remember that the Hanafi position is that order must be proven by independent evidence, e.g., the Sunnah.
2
Analyze the first argument: 'Bow and prostrate' (Quran 22:77)
Proponents argue that the waw in this verse implies order because the prayer requires order. The Hanafi response: order is derived from the hadith 'Pray as you have seen me pray,' not from the waw itself. Note that the waw is neutral; it does not specify sequence.
The Hanafis emphasize that the ruling on order in prayer comes from the Sunnah, not the Quranic wording.
3
Analyze the second argument: Safa and Marwah and 'Start with what Allah started with'
Proponents claim the verse 'Indeed Safa and Marwah are among the symbols of Allah' with the hadith 'Start with what Allah started with' shows the waw implies order. The Hanafi response: the hadith is a directive (useful for us), not a binding proof from the verse; order is established by Sunnah. Moreover, the rituals themselves do not imply a fixed order between Safa and Marwah.
The principle 'Start with what Allah started with' is itself a Sunnah, not a linguistic inference from the waw.
4
Analyze the third argument: The Prophet's rebuke to the preacher
The preacher used the dual form 'Whoever disobeys them' instead of 'Allah and His Messenger.' Proponents say the rebuke was for violating order. Hanafi reply: the rebuke was for lack of glorification (ta'zim) in the singular form, not for order. Disobedience to Allah and His Messenger is essentially one act, so order is not applicable.
The Hanafis point out that the singular form emphasizes the unity of disobedience, while the dual form diminishes glorification.
5
Analyze the fourth argument: The companions' objection to Ibn Abbas
Companions objected when Ibn Abbas interpreted the verse 'Complete Hajj and Umrah' as allowing Umrah before Hajj. Proponents say this shows the waw implies order (Hajj before Umrah). Hanafi response: the objection was not about order but about the waw being for absolute conjunction (jam' mutlaq). Specifying a particular order (e.g., Umrah before Hajj) is arbitrary; the waw does not dictate any sequence.
The Hanafis conclude that the waw is neutral; any order must be proven by separate evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائلو الترتيب قالوا :أولاً: (قال الله تعالى : (أَرْكَعُواْ واسْجُدُواْ ﴾ [الحج : (۷۷] ووجب الترتيب بينهما مع العطف بالواو فعلم أنه للترتيب (قلنا) لا نسلم أنه فهم الترتيب من هذه الكريمة بل فهم من قوله ( وآله وأصحابه: «صلوا كما ) رأيتموني أصلي فهذا الأمر هدانا إلى وجوب الترتيب، ثم إن الأمر جلي لكن دلالة هذا الحديث على وجوب الترتيب بين الركوع والسجود محل تأمل فتأمل فإذن الأصلح التمسك بما قد وقع في حديث الأعرابي الذي ورد لبيان حقيقة الصلاة بكلمة ثم (و) قالوا (ثانياً) قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَابِرِ اللَّهِ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۵۸] مع أن الترتيب بينهما واجب، وفهم من الواو كيف (و) قد قال عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام ابدؤوا بما بدأ الله به قلنا : إنه) مفيد (لنا لا) أنه ( علينا) وإنما كان علينا لو فهم من الكريمة وليس الأمر كذلك بل فهم من الحديث، ولو كان الواو للترتيب لما احتاجوا فيه إلى السنة على أنه لو سلم الترتيب فلا ههنا كيف و لا ترتيب في الشعائر) فلا الاستدلال بشيء لا يصح فيه الترتيب عليه (و) قالوا :(ثالثاً : أمره للخطيب قل ومن يعص الله ورسوله عندما خطب ومن يعصهما، وعاتبه عليه وقال بئس الخطيب أنت فلو لم يكن للترتيب فلا معنى للعتاب على الإتيان بصيغة التثنية، والأمر بإيثار الواو العاطف (قلنا) لا نسلم أن العتاب كان لفقدان الترتيب المفهوم في العاطف بل للتعظيم كيف وفي الإفراد تعظيم وتهويل فلذا أمره به قيل ويدل عليه أي على أن الأمر بالإفراد للتعظيم لا للترتيب أن معصيتهما لا ترتيب فيها فإن معصية الرسول هو بعينه معصية الله، فلا وجه للترتيب أقول يجوز التقدم عقلاً بين معصية الله ورسوله (فافهم) وأنت تعلم أن التقدم ما لا يتصوّر اللهم إلا باعتبار أن معصية الله ممنوعة بالذات ومعصية الرسول لأجل كونها معصية الله فافهم (و) قالوا :(رابعاً : إنكارهم على ابن عباس تقديم العمرة على الحج) في القرآن (بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَتِمُوا الْحَجَ وَالْعُمْرَةَ لِلَّهِ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۹٦] فقد فهموا الترتيب مع الواو (قلنا) لم يكن إنكارهم لفهم تقديم الحج على العمرة، بل ذلك لأن الواو للأعم) من تقديم العمرة أو تقديم الحج أي الجمع المطلق ( فالتعيين) أي تعيين تقديم العمرة (تحكم) هذا.
Those who assert order said: First, Allah said: "Bow and prostrate" (Surah Al-Hajj 77) and order between them is obligatory while they are conjoined with waw, so it is known that the waw is for order. We say: We do not accept that order is understood from this noble verse; rather it is understood from his statement (and his family and companions): "Pray as you have seen me pray." So this command guided us to the obligation of order. Then the command is clear but the indication of this hadith for the obligation of order between bowing and prostration is subject to reflection, so reflect. Therefore the better course is to stick to what occurred in the hadith of the Bedouin that came to explain the reality of prayer with the word "thumma". And they said, secondly: Allah said: "Indeed Safa and Marwah are among the symbols of Allah" (Al-Baqarah 158) while order between them is obligatory, and it is understood from the waw. How? And he (peace be upon him) said: "Start with what Allah started with." We say: It is useful for us, not binding upon us. It would be binding only if order was understood from the verse, which is not the case; rather it is understood from the hadith. If the waw were for order, there would be no need for the Sunnah. Even if order is conceded, there is no order in the rituals. So the proof with something in which order is not valid is not sound. And they said, thirdly: The command to the preacher to say "Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger" when he preached and said "Whoever disobeys them" and he rebuked him and said: "What a bad preacher you are." If the waw were not for order, there would be no meaning to the rebuke for using the dual form and the command to prefer the conjunctive waw. We say: We do not accept that the rebuke was for the lack of order understood in the conjunction; rather it was for glorification. How? In the singular there is glorification and awe, so he commanded him to use it. It is said that it indicates that the command for singular is for glorification, not for order, because disobedience to them has no order in it, for disobedience to the Messenger is the same as disobedience to Allah. So there is no basis for order. I say: It is possible rationally to give precedence between disobedience to Allah and His Messenger. Understand! And you know that precedence is inconceivable unless it is considered that disobedience to Allah is intrinsically prohibited and disobedience to the Messenger is because it is disobedience to Allah, so understand. And they said, fourthly: Their objection to Ibn Abbas regarding giving precedence to Umrah over Hajj in the Quran by His statement: "And complete Hajj and Umrah for Allah" (Al-Baqarah 196). They understood order from the waw. We say: Their objection was not for understanding precedence of Hajj over Umrah, but rather because the waw is for the general (meaning either precedence of Umrah or precedence of Hajj, i.e., absolute conjunction). So specification (of precedence of Umrah) is arbitrary. That is it.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether the conjunctive waw (واو) implies order (tartib) in legal texts, a key usul al-fiqh issue. The proponents of order (qā'ilū al-tartīb) bring four arguments from Quran and practice. First, the verse commanding bowing and prostration: they claim the waw shows order. The Hanafi response refutes that order is derived from the verse itself; rather it is from the Prophet's hadith 'Pray as you see me pray.' Second, the verse about Safa and Marwah: order is understood from the waw? The Hanafis respond that the principle 'Start with what Allah started with' is a directive for us, not a binding proof from the verse itself; order is established by Sunnah. Third, the Prophet's rebuke to a preacher: the Hanafis explain the rebuke was for lacking glorification, not for violating order. Fourth, the companions' objection to Ibn Abbas: the Hanafis say the waw indicates mere conjunction, not order; specifying one order is arbitrary. The passage thus defends the Hanafi position that waw does not imply order, relying on separate evidences.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunctive letter waw, debated whether it implies order or mere conjunction.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Order or sequence required between two actions in legal rulings.
الجمع المطلق (al-jam' al-mutlaq): Absolute conjunction or combination without implying order.

Preference of Conjoining to the Nearer Element
Rule on preferring conjunctive attachment to the nearer antecedent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that conjoining to the nearer antecedent is preferred, and identify when a diverting factor (sārif) changes the attachment.

Core Idea
Conjoining to the nearer antecedent is preferred, but a diverting factor can redirect it.

This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut establishes a key linguistic-legal rule: when a phrase is conjoined to a preceding text, it should be attached to the nearest possible element unless an indicator diverts it. We will examine the rule, its examples (including a Quranic verse), and a scholarly objection that is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
State the principle: 'al-‘ațf ‘alā al-qarīb aulā min al-‘ațf ‘alā al-ba‘īd' – conjoining to the nearer is better than to the distant.
2
Analyse the legal example
Consider the conditional: 'If you enter, then you are divorced and my slave is free.' Identify the nearer antecedent (the consequence clause) and explain why the slave's freedom is also conditional on entry.
The conjoined phrase 'my slave is free' attaches to the nearest element, so it shares its ruling.
3
Understand the diverting factor (sārif)
In the example 'and your co-wife is divorced,' the explicit predicate indicates a sārif, diverting conjoining to a distant antecedent. Identify why the speaker's explicit mention changes the attachment.
Without the explicit predicate, the phrase would be conjoined to the nearer consequence.
4
Examine the Quranic example
In Surah al-Nur (24:4), the phrase 'and those are the disobedient' is conjoined to a distant clause. Identify the sārif: the change of addressee from the rulers (plural) to the Prophet (singular).
The change in addressee serves as an indicator diverting conjoining.
5
Evaluate the objection from al-Talwīh
Understand the objection that variation of address is permissible in eloquent speech. Then analyse the author's response: although permissible, it is not preferable, so the rule holds.
The objection misunderstands the role of preference (awlawiyya) vs. permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الأولى : العطف على القريب أولى من العطف على البعيد (فعلقت الحرية بالدخول في قوله : إن دخلت فأنت طالق وعبدي حر) إذ الجزاء أقرب، فالعطف عليه ،أولى فلا يصار عنه إلى البعيد، فيتعلق الحرية أيضاً بالدخول (إلا لصارف) عن القريب إلى البعيد فحينئذ يعطف عليه (نحو : وضرتك (طالق فإن إظهار الخبر قرينة، على أنه لم يقصد العطف على الجزاء وإلا لكفى : وضرتك (ومنه : وَأُولَيكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ ﴾ [النور: ٤] فإنه يعطف على الجملة البعيدة دون القريبة لأجل صارف تعدد المخاطبين لأن الخطاب في المعطوف عليه لو عطف على القريب وهو فاجلدوا ولا تقبلوا للأئمة) بدليل صيغة الجمع (دون المعطوف فإن الخطاب فيه للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وتعدد الخطاب في المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، وإن جاز إذا كان الخطاب في أحدهما بحرف الخطاب، لكنه لا شك في عدم أولويته، وهذا القدر كاف لترجيح العطف على ما إذا يعطف عليه يتحد المخاطب، فإيراد صاحب التلويح بأن اختلاف الخطاب جائز وواقع في كلام الفصحاء إذا كان الخطاب في أحدهما بالحرف طائح لا توجه له فيما نحن بصدده فتدبر.
The first benefit: Conjoining to the nearer is better than conjoining to the distant. For example, the suspension of freedom upon entry in his statement: 'If you enter, then you are divorced and my slave is free.' Since the consequence (jazā') is nearer, conjoining to it is better, so it is not turned away to the distant one; thus freedom also becomes suspended upon entry. Except if there is a diverting factor from the nearer to the distant, then in that case it is conjoined to it, like: 'and your co-wife is divorced.' For the explicit mention of the predicate is an indication that he did not intend conjoining to the consequence; otherwise 'and your co-wife' would suffice. And from that is the verse: 'And those are the disobedient' (An-Nur 4). It is conjoined to the distant sentence rather than the nearer due to a diverting factor: the multiplicity of addressees. Because the address in the conjoined-to element, if it were conjoined to the nearer which is 'then flog them and do not accept their testimony' is addressed to the rulers (by the plural form), while the conjoined element is addressed to the Prophet (peace be upon him). The multiplicity of address in the conjoined and conjoined-to, even if permissible when the address in one is by a letter of address, is certainly not preferable. This is sufficient to prefer conjoining to that which unifies the addressee. So the objection of the author of al-Talwih that variation of address is permissible and occurs in the speech of eloquent speakers when the address in one is by a letter is baseless; it has no bearing in our context. Reflect.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage establishes a key grammatical-legal rule in usul al-fiqh: when conjoining (عطف) a phrase to a preceding text, it is preferable to attach it to the nearest antecedent rather than a distant one. The rule is illustrated by a legal example: in the conditional statement 'If you enter, then you are divorced and my slave is free,' the phrase 'my slave is free' is conjoined to the consequence ('then you are divorced') because the consequence is nearer. Thus, the slave's freedom is also conditional upon entry. However, an explicit indicator (like mentioning 'your co-wife is divorced' separately) can divert to the distant antecedent. The Quranic example from Surah An-Nur (24:4) is given: the phrase 'and those are the disobedient' is conjoined to a distant sentence due to a change in addressee (rulers vs. Prophet). The passage then refutes an objection from the commentator al-Talwih, asserting that the rule stands despite possible variation of address.

Key Terms
العطف (al-'atf): Grammatical conjunction linking two elements with a shared ruling.
الصارف (al-sārif): An indicator that diverts conjoining from the nearer to the distant antecedent.
الجزاء (al-jazā'): The consequence clause in a conditional sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'my slave is free' is conjoined to the condition 'if you enter'.
B) Because 'my slave is free' is conjoined to the nearer consequence 'then you are divorced'.
C) Because 'my slave is free' is an independent oath.
D) Because the speaker intended both to be conditional.

Key Takeaway
Conjoining to the nearer antecedent is a preferred rule in Hanafi usul, but explicit indicators can divert attachment to a distant element.

Interpreting the Conjunction (Waw) in Debts and Divorce: Sharing vs Independence
Textual analysis of waw conjunction in debt and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how explicit quantification and contextual impossibility override the default of sharing (tashrik) when interpreting the conjunction waw in legal statements.

Core Idea
When waw joins two recipients, sharing is default, but explicit numbers and impossibility of fractional intent can indicate independence.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the conjunction 'waw' (and) generally implies sharing of the obligation between the conjuncted parties. However, this default can be overridden when context indicates a contrary intent, especially when a specific number is mentioned and ordinary understanding rejects a fractional result. This passage examines a debt example and a divorce example to illustrate the interplay between linguistic default and contextual clues, teaching how the Hanafi school navigates between literal interpretation and purposive understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule for waw
Note the principle that 'al-tashrik huwa al-asl' (sharing is the original state) when waw connects two recipients of a single obligation.
In the debt example, saying 'I owe Zayd a thousand and [I owe] Amr [a thousand]' defaults to each getting 500.
2
Compare with the divorce scenario
Examine the statement 'This one is thrice divorced and this one [said] when divorced' – note that applying the default would give each one and a half divorces.
The text argues that this fractional result is practically impossible as an intended meaning.
3
Analyze the argument for independence
The specification of the number 'three' and its placement before the conjunct are evidence of complete separation (kulliyat al-wahshah) – the speaker intended each to receive the full three.
This is an example of tansis (explicit specification) overriding the default.
4
Examine the counterargument and its refutation
A counterargument says that conjunction is still evidence of sharing, but the text notes that the manifest absurdity of intending one and a half divorces provides a stronger deterrent from sharing.
The author concludes that the context of the divorce makes independence the only rational interpretation.
5
Appreciate the role of customary context
Realize that the interpretation depends on what rational speakers normally intend, and that custom (urf) and logical possibility guide the override.
This shows the Hanafi approach of balancing linguistic default with practical reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العام، فرع (إذا قال لفلان عليَّ ألف ولفلان فلكل واحد منهما خمسمائة) ويشتركان في الألف، لأن التشريك هو الأصل (بخلاف) قوله : (هذه طالق ثلاثاً وهذه إذ طلقتا) أي كل منهما (ثلاثاً لا اثنتين وكان الظاهر هذا، لأن بانقسام الثلاث عليهما تطلق كل طلقة ونصفاً، ويكمل النصف فيصير ثنتين، لكن لا يشتركان الظهور القصد إلى إيقاع (الثلاث) لأن التنصيص على العدد والتقديم على المعطوف يدلّ على كمال الوحشة، وقصد الإبانة (وفيه ما فيه) فإن التنصيص والتقديم، كما أنه قرينة إرادة الثلاث، كذلك العطف قرينة الاشتراك، بل هذه القرينة أرجح، لأنه الأصل كما لا يخفى هذا والحق أن ههنا صارفاً آخر عن التشريك، فإن مقتضاه إصابة كل طلقة ونصفاً، وغير خفي على المنصف أن هذا النحو من التطليق لا يخطر ببال أحد وإن كان يكمل شرعاً اللهم إلا عند اللعب والهزل أو على الندرة، فعلم أنه ما قصد التشريك، بل استقلال كل بالثلاث وهذا وجه وجيه لا يرد عليه شيء.
The general [section], branch: If someone says, 'I owe so-and-so a thousand and [I owe] so-and-so [a thousand],' then each one gets five hundred, and they share the thousand, because sharing is the default. Contrary to his saying, 'This one is thrice divorced and this one [has] said when divorced' — meaning each of them thrice, not twice. And apparently this would be the case, because by dividing the three among them, each would get one and a half divorces, and the half would be completed to make two, but they do not share; the apparent intent is to effect three [for each], because specifying the number and placing it before the conjunct indicates complete separation and intent of finality. But there is [something] in it: the specification and placement, just as they are evidence of intending three, likewise the conjunction is evidence of sharing; rather this evidence is stronger because it is the original [default]. And the truth is that there is here another deterrent from sharing: its requirement would be that each receives one and a half; and it is not hidden from the fair-minded that this manner of divorce does not occur to anyone, even if it is legally completable, except in play or jest or rarely. So it is known that sharing was not intended; rather each is independent with three, and this is a sound point against which nothing can be said.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage, under the topic of 'al-'amm' (general), discusses the interpretation of the conjunction 'waw' in matters of debt and divorce. The default principle is that the conjunction 'waw' indicates sharing (tashrik) when connecting two recipients of an obligation. So if someone says 'I owe Zayd one thousand and [I owe] Amr [one thousand]', the default is that the thousand is shared equally between them. However, in the case of divorce, when one says 'This one is thrice divorced and this one [said] when divorced', the default of sharing would mean each gets one and a half divorces, which is impossible as a practical intent. Therefore, the text argues that a different interpretation is required: the speaker intended each to be independently divorced thrice, because the specification of the number and the precedence indicate a desire for complete separation. The text addresses a counterargument that conjunction still suggests sharing, but the stronger point is that rational parties do not intend fractional divorces; thus independence is the intended meaning. This shows how context and custom can override default linguistic rules.

Key Terms
التشريك (al-tashrik): The default ruling that conjunction (waw) implies sharing of the obligation among conjuncted parties.
التنصيص (al-tansis): Explicit specification of the number (e.g., 'three') which indicates a specific intended quantum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the debt example, what is the default ruling when someone says 'I owe so-and-so a thousand and so-and-so'?
A) Each gets the full thousand independently.
B) The thousand is shared equally between them.
C) The debt is invalid due to ambiguity.
D) The second person gets nothing until proven otherwise.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction waw defaults to sharing, but explicit numbers and contextual impossibility can indicate the speaker intended independence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
The Rule of 'Idrab with 'Bal' in Iqrar and Insha'
Rational argument on 'idrab' with 'bal' in legal declarations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule regarding 'idrab' with 'bal' in iqrar and insha', and apply it to determine the number of divorces when a man says 'You are divorced once, rather twice'.

Core Idea
In iqrar, 'bal' adds to the confession; in insha', the first remains and the second adds — the turning away is invalid.

This lesson explores a key linguistic principle in Hanafi usul: how the particle 'bal' (rather) affects legal declarations. The same word can produce different outcomes depending on whether the speech act is informational (iqrar) or performative (insha'). We will examine the reasoning that leads to three divorces in the example 'You are divorced once, rather twice' and why Zufar's analogy with iqrar fails.

Guided Path
1
Classify the two speech acts
Identify iqrar (confession) as khabar (informational) and insha' (e.g., talāq) as performative. Their differing natures are key to the rule.
Iqrar can be revised; insha' creates an immediate, irrevocable ruling.
2
Analyze the function of 'bal' in each type
In iqrar, 'bal' indicates a turning away (idrab) from the first statement, but it only adds, it does not negate the first. In insha', the first statement has already taken effect, so 'bal' cannot negate it; the second statement adds alongside the first.
Zufar argued that 'bal' in insha' could replace the first, but the Hanafi response shows the first remains binding.
3
Apply to the divorce example
When a man says 'You are divorced once, rather twice', the first divorce is effective immediately. The 'bal' introduces a second divorce that adds to the first, resulting in three divorces (one + two). The turning away from the first is legally invalid because the first ruling cannot be undone.
Both Hanafi and Zufar agree on three divorces, but the reasoning differs: Hanafi says first remains, Zufar says it is replaced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية التعدد وحينئذ يلزم القول بمفهوم العدد وقد نهينا عنه والجواب: أن الانفراد قد يكون من بأن يكون الغير مسكوتاً عنه وليس من ضروريات الانفراد الحكم بعدم الآخر فانفهام الانفراد لا يوجب انفهام المفهوم فيضرب في المثال المضروب عن انفراد الدرهم بالإقرار، ويجعل معه غيره مذكوراً مقراً به، وقد يجاب بأن ما نهينا عن فهم المفهوم بواسطة القرائن، والإضراب ههنا قرينة لانفهام المفهوم فتدبر (وقياسه) أي قياس زفر على الإنشاء نحو طالق واحدة بل ثنتين، حيث يقع ثلاث لأن الإضراب عن الواحد لا يصح قياس مع الفارق، لأن الإقرار إخبار على الأصح) فلا يثبت شيئاً فلا تفريع) فيه على اللفظ ليحكم فله أن يعرض عن خبر، كأن أخبر به ويخبر بدله بخبر آخر بخلاف الإنشاء إذ به يثبت الحكم، وليس في يده بعد ثبوته أن يعرض عنه، ولقائل أن يقول الإنشاء والإقرار سواء : لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون الإضراب الإقرار عن مجرد الانفراد والمقصود أن ليس له واحد بصفة الانفراد بل معه غيره، فكذا في الإنشاء يجوز أن يكون الإعراض عن صفة الانفراد والمقصود أنها ليست طالقة بطلاق واحد فقط، بل معه واحد آخر فهي طالقة ثنتين. وإما أن يكون الإعراض عن نفس الإقرار بالدرهم، فينبغي أن لا يصح الإعراض عنه، يصح الإعراض عنه، لأنه رجوع، وذا لا يسمع ، الغير، كما أن الرجوع في الإنشاء لا يجوز إذ ليس في يده، فإذن لا فرق بينهما، وجوابه وبالله التوفيق أنه إعراض عن الواحدة بصفة الوحدة، وإثبات لها مع غيرها بخبر آخر، وهو بل ثنتان، وهذا في سعته، لأنه إنما لا يصح الرجوع في الإقرار لما أنه ظهر منه حق الغير، وبالرجوع يبطل حق الغير والرجوع بكلمة بل لا يبطل الحق أصلاً بل يؤكده لأنه إنما يرجع ليظهر ذلك الزيادة وهذا بخلاف الإنشاء، لأنه إذ قد تلفظ بطلقة واحدة فقد وقعت بصفة الواحدة، لأن الإنشاء لا يتأخر الحكم عنه ، وجعل نفسه علة للحكم فليس في وسعه أن يبطله أصلاً ولا أن يبطله بهذه الصفة ويثبته بكلام آخر بصفة أخرى، لأن الواقع لا يرتفع فإذا أراد بذلك الإعراض عن الأول والإيقاع بكلام آخر لم يبطل ما أعرض عنه لوجود علته، وقع ما يقتضيه هذا الكلام الآخر فلزم موجبهما وبطل الإعراض بهذا الوجه شرعاً، لا أنه لا تصح الإرادة من الكلام لغة هذا والله أعلم بأحكامه .
The third article on linguistic principles: multiplicity, and then the concept of the number is necessitated, and we have been prohibited from it. And the answer: that singling out may be because the other is unmentioned, and it is not a necessary consequence of singling out to rule out the other. So understanding singling out does not necessitate understanding the concept. So an example is given about the singling out of the dirham in confession, and making mention of something else alongside it. And it may be answered that what we are prohibited from understanding the concept is by way of contextual indicators, and here 'idrab' is a context for understanding the concept, so reflect. (And his analogy) i.e., Zufar's analogy on declamation such as 'You are divorced once, rather twice', where three [divorces] occur, because 'idrab' from one is not valid analogy due to a difference, because confession is informational according to the correct view, so it does not establish anything, so there is no ramification on the wording to rule, so he may turn away from a report, as if he reported it and reports instead another report, unlike declamation because by it the ruling is established, and it is not in his power after its establishment to turn away from it. And someone may say that declamation and confession are equal: because either the 'idrab' in confession is from mere singling out, and the intent is that he does not have one in the state of singling out but with another, so likewise in declamation it is permissible to turn away from the characteristic of singling out and the intent is that she is not divorced with one divorce only, but with another one, so she is divorced with two. Or the turning away is from the very confession of the dirham, then it should not be valid to turn away from it, because it is a retraction, and that is not heard, just as retraction in declamation is not allowed since it is not in his power, so there is no difference between them. And the answer, and with Allah is success, is that it is a turning away from the one with the attribute of unity, and establishing it along with another by another report, which is 'rather two', and this is within his capacity, because retraction in confession is not valid because it has come from a right of another, and by retraction the right of another is invalidated, and retraction with the word 'bal' does not nullify the right at all but rather confirms it because he only retracts to show that increase. And this is unlike declamation, because when he utters a single divorce, it has occurred with the attribute of one, because declamation's ruling is not delayed from it, and he made himself the cause for the ruling, so it is not within his capacity to nullify it at all, nor to nullify it with this attribute and establish it with another speech with another attribute, because what has occurred cannot be removed. So if he intends thereby to turn away from the first and to effect with another speech, he does not nullify what he turned away from due to the existence of its cause, and what is necessitated by this other speech occurs, so both consequences are binding, and the turning away in this manner is invalid legally, not that the intention from the speech is invalid linguistically. And Allah knows best His judgments.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rational argument about the legal effect of the word 'bal' (rather) in two types of speech: iqrar (confession/informatory) and insha' (declarative acts like talāq). The context is a discussion of the linguistic principle of 'idrab' (turning away/omission) and whether it implies a concept of number. The author first responds to an objection regarding the concept of multiplicity, explaining that singling out does not automatically negate the other possibility. Then a specific example is given: Zufar's analogy between iqrar and insha' concerning 'bal'. Zufar attempts to analogize the use of 'bal' in insha' to its use in iqrar, claiming that just as a confessor can turn away from a single dirham and add another, so too a divorcer can turn away from a single divorce and replace it with two, resulting in three divorces. The author argues that this analogy fails because iqrar is khabar (informational) and thus revisable, while insha' creates an immediate legal effect that cannot be revoked. In the case of 'bal' in iqrar, the speaker confirms the first and adds the second; in insha', the first divorce is irrevocable and the second adds as well, so both take effect, yielding three divorces. The author refutes a counterargument that both are equal by distinguishing the nature of retraction: in iqrar, retraction with 'bal' does not nullify the first but confirms it, while in insha', the first is already effective and cannot be undone. Legally, the turning away is invalid in insha', but the speech itself is valid.

Key Terms
إضراب (idrāb): Linguistically, turning away or omitting; in this context, using the word 'bal' to indicate that the speaker is correcting or adding to a previous statement.
بل (bal): A particle meaning 'rather' or 'but', used for 'idrab' (turning away) to correct or add to a preceding statement. In this passage, its effect differs in iqrar and insha'.
إقرار (iqrār): A legal confession or admission, classified as khabar (informational speech) that can be revised.
إنشاء (inshā'): Declarative speech acts that create legal effects, such as talāq (divorce) or bay' (sale). Unlike iqrar, the ruling is immediate and irrevocable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position analyzed in this lesson, what is the number of divorces when a man says 'You are divorced once, rather twice'?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None, the statement is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'bal' in iqrar adds to the first confession; in insha', both declarations take effect because the first is irrevocable — the turning away is legally invalid.
